Malthoid is primarily a roofing material—the finest roofing material made, as
we believe. But it has other uses. It is used as a floor covering where traffic
is heavy. It is used for mats in Electrical plants. It is used on Switchboards to
keep the damp from the instruments. It is used over lining-boards for walls.
It makes magnificent walls, because it is a non-conductor. It keeps houses cool
in summer and warm in winter. It is used on farms for a score of purposes.
We’d like to send you a book on the Uses of Malthoid.

The Red Page
Fast and Westralla
It, is to Henry Lawson that, after all, we
have to go when we want the real Australian
verse. It is the real Australian verse
because it expresses the flavor of Australia,
the essence of our life, because it possesses
the magic, intimate touch that makes a whole
continent akin. Lawson has lived his verse ;
it is merely his life that—in verses careless,
inchoate, and occasionally slovenly—he pours
interminably out. He clasps your hand —and
holds it, while he tells his tale. An artistic
and critical aloofness from his subject is
impossible to Henry Lawson. He just
yarns—and in the most natural way sidles
into personal digressions. Thus in his story
of Peter the Great, he breaks off to inform
his listeners of another ship-builder—Law-
son’s father.
And I’d like to say my father mastered navi-
gation too.
(He was born across in Norway, educated
fairly toell,
And he graftedJn a ship-yard by the Port of
Arundel.)
An interesting fact —to Lawson. Why
not mention it, even in a poem about the
Russians? Later on, we get further infor-
mation about his younger brother, casually
furnished at the end of a poem, the poet
being apparently quite unperturbed by, or
ignorant of, the fact that this unnecessary
postscript spoils the poem.
But if you take the trouble to cut out the
excrescences the rest is poetry. Lawson
has the power, often withheld from poets of
a. higher artistic calibre, of communicating
his effects to his readers. His stuff, in the
vaudeville phrase, gets over the footlights.
It is not the most poetic stuff; but it is
popular stuff, simple, intimate songs about
things that in themselves have their appeal
to us. Here and there in this collection of
verse, entitled My Army, O My Army! and
Other Songs (Tyrrell’s, Sydney, Is.). Law-
son achieves a poem, complete in itself with-
out Lawson’s casual asides. “Peddling-
Round the World,” like the other verses, has
appeared already in this paper; but only its
length precludes its re-quotation here. Law-
son’s conception of England as a gipsy lass,
always hawking her wares around the globe,
is finely picturesque, stressing an undying
truth about England now obscured by the
war. And that pregnant truth is brilliantly
expressed. The Slavonic Songs are remark-
able for Lawson’s graphic use of imagined
homely detail — ,
From the silence of Siberia, and the strange,
snow-deadened streets
“The Russian March” makes ns not only see,
but hear, the slow, tremendous oncoming of
the Slavonic hordes.
Russian mist, and cold, and darkness, on the
weary Russiati roads;
And the sound of Russian swear-words, and
the whack of Russian goads;....
There's the grind of tyres unceasing, where
the metal caps the clay;
And the “clock“clock." “dock" of axles
going, on all night and day....
Here and there the sound of cattle in the
mist and in the sleet.
And the scrambling start of horses, and the
ceaseless splosh of feet ....
And when the army halts
Sounds of boxes moved in waggons, and of
axes on a log —
And the wild and joyous baricing of the
regimental dog!
Sounds of pots and pans and buckets, and
the clinic of chain and hook —
And the blasphemous complaining of the
Universal Cook.
Of course, there must be Russian swear-
words, and Russian regimental dogs, and
Russian cooks. Lawson merely brings them
to our mind, and fixes them there. It
occurred to none of our other poets that it
would be worth while.
“A New John Bull”;
A tall, slight English gentleman,
Who hates to soil his hands:
He takes his mother's drawing-room
To the most outlandish lands;
and who was, Lawson mentions, “the only
gentleman that shamed the cad in me,” and
“Booth’s Drum” show Lawson at his best.
In between there are poems that show him
at nearly his worst. But the book has
throughout it an individuality: behind it is
a poet. Unhappily, it is only in occasional
poems that Lawson the poet, and not Lawson
the vanter-rite-and-will, shows himself. Still,
there is enough of Lawson the poet to please
the average reader.
The late J. P. Bourke (“Bluebush”) belongs
to an age long past in Australian literature.
Off the Bluebush, Verses for Australians
West and East Sydney; 3s. 6d.),
is presented by his editor to the decadent
critics of the East as a proof that “the most
virile and the most original poetry that has
been made in Australia since the Common-
wealth began” comes from Westralia. “For
vigor and versatility the East at the moment
has few writers to rival this little Western
comradeship” of “Bluebush” (J. P. Bourke),
“Dryblower,” “Crosscut,” “Prospect Good,”
and the rest. The editor admits that this
Westralian poetry is often inartistic.; it is
often a poor thing considered as literature;
but be pleads that it is written from a heart
to a heart, that it has sincerity, truth of
vision, sympathy comparable to that of the
old ballads.
Well, if we are to have the question of
East vci'sus West so truculently raised, if
there is to be forced upon ns a poetical civil
war at this inopportune moment, it seems
a pity that the book of “Bluebush” should be
flung down as the gauntlet of the
West. J. P. Bourke writes nice verses—of
the sort that were written universally
throughout Australia ten years ago—doubt-
less many of these verses were written ten
years ago; but then both East and West,
not forgetting North and South —contributed
those verses. Now, apparently, if we are
to believe “Bluebush’s” editor, it is only in
the West that this sort of verse is cultivated
—or survives. If “the East has more refined
writers, more cultivated and more artistic
writers ; but not more manly writers” why
is J. P. Bourke cited? It would be unfair
to quote thin futile riming exercises like
“Dreams,” “Till Day is Done,” “Dreaming
the Dream of Life” —mere words neatly
dovetailed, without emotion or thought. J.
P. Bourke does this sort of thing as easily
and as emptily as the writers of the decadent
East. It would be unkind, too, to quote more
than two lines of the soliloquy-rime of the
“The Diamond Wedding” sort: —
To-day is our diamond wedding, old wife!
Some seventy summers and more. ...
or the narratives of “Old Bill Bates, “Dan,
the Hatter,” “A Bloke from Mullingar,”
“Soaker Smith,” or “Hell for Leather”: you
will find that G. R. Sims sort of Outback
narrative in the files of this paper 20 years
ago. Its survival in the West is quaint, that
is all.
The sole individual note struck by “Blue-
bush” is to be found in his casual humor,
embodied in such strains as “Nearly a Pessi-
mist,” “Scratehin’ for a Crust,” and “Beer
is .Enough.”
That is the best that the West has to
proffer—at least, in this allegedly represen-
tative volume. But, in his own class, we
have merely to put Henry Lawson—and
Henry Lawson not at his best. Behind
Lawson’s latest volume stands an achieve-
ment that for bulk and quality is a Kos-
ciusko to an ant-hill.
The illustrations by Ned Wethered repre-
sent, . according to the editor’s claim, “the
promising effort of a Westralian designer
and illustrator, almost wholly self-taught,
aged 20.” They look it. It is not Ned
VVethered s fault that the decorations are
sometimes incongruously placed, but his col-
lection of fantods, illustrating a poem on
that subject, is the least interesting and
most gentlemanly mob that this page has
ever drunk. The full-page illustrations sug-
gest the possibility that the art of the West
stands to the art of the East in the same
relation as does its poetry.
"NEARLY A PESSIMIST.
What’s the use o' laughter,
What's the use o’ strife ,
To a gloomy shafter
In this team of Life?
Hear the whips a-crackin’
Through the atmosphere,
When the traces slacken —-
Let us have a beer?....
What's the use o' prayin'?
'Tain’t no use to curse;
What’s the use o' layin’
'Gainst the ivinning hearse?
Man at best’s a rotter,
Fried and frizzled here;
Hell can be no hotter —
Let us have a beer.
What's the use of sittin ’
Dry as blessed chips?
What's the use of spiitin ’
Through our corn-bcef lipsT
What's the use of drinkin’f
Well, that ain't so clear
To my way of thinkin ’ —
Let us have a beer.
What's the use of frettin'
'Cos you missed the pot?
What’s the use o' gettin'
In a tied-up knot
Bet you can't unravel
If you tried a year?
No. that cop don’t travel —-
Let us have a beer.
What’s the — oh. I'm, toilin’
Down the Boulder way—
Only fust been spoilin ’
Arf a quid a day.
Now you bet I’m chargin'
Homewards at my top..;.
What’s the use o’ bargin’
With a white-eyed slop?
The same calm, comfortable philosophy
is to be found in this more dignified treat-
ment of the same wet subject:—
Beer is enough. Let us he satisfied,
Nor fret our hearts with longing after gin,
And boh saloons, and vanities beside.
That lead one to the shelving edge of
sin ....
For wights who sit a-rotv along the pave,
With crackling skins, and drooping lives to
save,
Beer is enough.
Beer is enough. Let Love roost on his perch,
And coo and coo his breath away at
will. ...
The bride in orange blooms —the ivied
church —•
The two-roomed kipsy sheltered by the
hill
Sweep them aside, and fetch the frothing
bowl
To warm the cockles of one's inmost soul.
Beer is enough.
Beer is enough. Though dreamers sigh and
sigh
Of melting love, did lore e'er quench a
thirstf
Bid ever Cupid. 'neath a brazen sky
Hand out a pint to taper off a burst?
Can Daphne's lips allay the wild desire
To wade in hops, when coppers are afiref
Beer is enough.
Beer is enough. The brightest and the best
Of all the gifts the gods have handed
down! '
A nautch girl she! tuho graces all the west,
Dressed in her picture hat and. amber
gown....
There is no canker in her love—no lees
To weight one's ghost through dim eterni-
ties.
Beer is enough.
The Export of Fiction
Among the great primary industries of
this continent is literature. It is true that
the export of literature is small: this com-
modity does not figure in trade statistics: it
is unknown even to Ivnibbs, though he, him-
self, does a little private exporting of
verse-products, and his Year-Book is one
of the most valuable manufactured products
sent abroad. In most cases, however, it is
merely the partially manufactured product
that is sent abroad —the manuscript that
returns hither in the form of a novel. The
wholly manufactured product finds, of
course, an excellent local market; but, with
the exception of newspapers and official pro-
ductions, there is little literature exported
in a finished state. But the thin stream of
manuscripts drifting from our shores is re-
sponsible for a slight return flow of royalties
to Australia—and with new war-loans loom-
ing on the financial horizon, every shilling
received from exports is absolutely necessary
for Australian financial stability. Since
Australia imports the bulk of its novels, the
patriotic citizen, after having read all the
locally produced novels, should purchase the
Australian novel printed overseas. For the
patriotic Australian is thereby assuring to
Australia, in the person of the Australian
writer, the royalty profits—if any—that
would, in the ease of- an English novel,
remain in England.
But people, even though patriotic, will
not read wliat they don’t like; and no person
purchases a novel with any idea of thereby
helping Australian war finance. But if it
can 'be shown to Australian readers that
Australian writers can produce the readers’
favorite brand of fiction just as well as the
imported novelist, there is no doubt that
Australians would support the local article.
And, in certain brands of fiction goods, the
Australian novelist can equal and surpass
the Imported product. Four recent novels
prove that in the detective story, the novel
of London literary life, the humorous story
of the angel-child, and the adventure-
sentimental novel, we have writers beyond
whom, for entertainment, we need not look.
These brands of novels will not appeal to
the literary connoisseur ; but they are good,
serviceable articles that will have a wide
sale and fulfil a useful purpose. If you
want a conventional detective story, L. D.
Stranger will be pleased to supply you-with
one, set in Australia, and fully up to
sample, The Great Snake Murder (Rich-
mond, per Dymoek’s Library). If your
tastes incline to London literary life seen
from the Australian view-point, there is
Miss Bryde of England, by Alice Grant
Rosman (Melrose). If your soul needs the
humor and pathos of the good little hoy.
Isabel Maud Peacocke will delightfully tell
you all about My Friend Phil (Ward,
Lock). And the business-man, bishop
or flapper who needs the conventional
IT.S. yarn of wild-west adventure, can
have that useful article Anstralianised by
Boyd Gable in By Blow and Kiss (Tlodder
and Stoughton, per Dymock’s Library).
d*
The title, The Great Snake Murder, sup-
plies its classification. It is the usual con-
ventional detective story of a mysterious
murder in which a snake, sent in a box of
roses, kills an Australian woman. Of course,
there is a complicated will, and inevitably,
to make it harder., there are several persons
who would benefit by the death. The
mechanism of the plot is neatly constructed
and the secret of the identity of the mur-
derer as closely kept as the real name of
“L- I). Stranger.” Whoever this Little
D-; Stranger is, she knows how to write
this mechanical sort of fiction, wherein all
the characters are puppets, and less im-
portant than the strings by which they are
manipulated. And the story is obviously
Australian. But for her solution of the
murder-mystery the author has merely
adopted, and not even adapted, an ingenious
idea that has now become a commonplace.
Tim thought arises, however, whether all the
intricacies of murder plots and their expla-
nations have not long ago been worked out.
The Great Snake Murder is merely a read-
able detective-story—still, it is as readable
as the imported article.
d*
Miss Bryde of England is a very pleasant
tale. It is the story of a typically conven-
tional English girl, who, through contact
with a couple of delightful Australians in
London, gets into grips with life and gradu-
ally thaws into what may be termed the
Australian attitude. The contrast between
the narrow up-bringing of Miss Bryde and
the larger opportunities available to a girl
brought up in Australia is pleasantly indi-
cated without being patriotically' over-
stressed. There is implied criticism of Eng-
land. seen from the clear eyes of these out-
sider Australians, but never unkind criti-
cism. The alleged clever talk-of the group
of interesting people who gather around the
nucleus of the Australians’ unconventional
flat rings slightly false; but the two Aus-
tralians are quite real people. The novel is
merely an ordinary novel, done rather well.
Its value to Australian readers lies in the
impression that England and her conven-
tions make upon explorers from this con-
tinent. Its value to English readers will be
to upset some of their misconceptions about
the Australian. Alice Grant Rosman is not
concerned with vital problems: hers is a
surface view of life; but her story is
amiably and readably told.
dt
By Blow and Kiss is more than up to the
American sample. Boyd Cable can not only
turn out the Outback adventure of the neces-
sary thrill, lie can not only present us with
a typically strenuous and masculine hero
and a heroine who can be depended upon to
misunderstand him for 300 pages; but he
can set this wild and sentimental story in a
real Australian environment. There are pic-
tures of Outback conditions that are as bril-
liantly clone as by any of the Yankee ex-
perts—pictures like that of moving the
drought-stricken, starving sheep to the
ridges,* with the two carts and the sweat-
ing butchers following the mob, killing and
skinning the sheep that are too far gone:—
“The men were coated with the red dust,
and splashed and spotted with the deeper
red. Their boots and legs were soaking
despit* the dust, their arms dripped red from
the elbow, and their hands were almost too
wet and slippery to hold the blunted knives.
They worked doggedly, and with swift,
machine-like motions, slashing and ripping,
heaving the carcase over, wrenching and tear-
ing the skin off the quivering flesh." The cart
drove up, the skins were hastily flung on, the
men wiped their hands a couple of times
across the wool of the last, drew a hand
across a streaming brow, and ran to the
next group that lay huddled awaiting their
coming.” And there are equally graphic
pictures of a mob of stampeding cattle and
of a great flood. But there is no insistence
upon the harsher side of Australian life.
Boycl Cable shows that, for every blow Aus-
tralia has ready a kiss to heal that blow.
Necessarily, there is adventure in every
chapter. The false accusation of murder,
the hunted hero hiding in the ranges, wild
escapades and furious gallops—-Boyd Cable
knows what is wanted. And there is the
equally necessary misunderstanding between
the lovers, without which, of course, there
could be no adventure-novel. The humor is
obvious, the sentiment simple, the heroism
extreme; but the men and women are real,
if a little Americanised. The book is true
to its popular sample; but it is also truly
Australian.
d*
The angel-child is a stock figure in old-
maid fiction. Isabel Maud Peacocke, in
My Friend Phil, introduces us to an Aus-
tralian angel-child. Being Australian he is
naturally not so nauseous as the variety
imported to our subscription libraries from
the United States and England. Indeed Phil
is occasionally actually naughty. Of course,
Phil is the unconscious means of bringing
together a bachelor and a maid: but that is
not his sole function. His talk has the
quaintness necessary in such fiction as this;
but, despite his literary flavor, he remains
sturdily Australian. It is not the instinct
for heroism, nor an inborn courage, that
prevents him running away from the danger-
ous dog that attacks the girl. He explains
that he defended the girl, and didn’t run
away, “ ’Cos dogs can gen’rally run faster’n
little boys.” This is a sentiment that
peremptorily dismisses him from the Little
Lord Fauntlcroy class of child-heroes. Isabel
Maud Peacocke’s friend, Phil, is well worth
meeting. The story will gently amuse all
child-lovers. It is fully up to the level of
the best-imported brand of angel-child fiction.
-®
Bulletin readers need no detailed criti-
cism of the Double-Bed Dialogues, now
published in a shilling volume, with a char-
acteristic cover by Norman Lindsay, by the
N.S.W. Bookstall Co. Most of these stories
by Henry James James have already ap-
peared in this paper. However, in the book
form each chapter is headed with extracts
from The Babette Birthday Book, a selec-
tion from which sufficiently indicates its
contents: “The only way to account for the
Average Husband is the Average Wife....
No man* ever marries one woman; he
marries an Executive Committee of her
girl friends, with his Mother-in-law in
the chair... .If Woman ever wondered about
herself, wouldn’t she be amazed—-or amused?
....A clever woman can marry any man
she wants. Judging by results, either
women are not clever or they don’t want
much. There are many ways of being
dull. The pleasantest is to be married....
Sentence of imprisonment for the term of
your natural life: ‘This is so sudden!’....
After all, a man marries a harem; —and
there’s room for all of it in one nightie.
.... The wedding breakfast begins the ro-
mance : the wedded breakfast ends it.”
I-n shilling editions you can now get
Oliver Onions’ delightful Compleat Bachelor,
Locke’s Septimus, and The Florentine
Frame, by Elizabeth Robins (Murray).

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Evensong.
Not beautiful is she
That most I love and praise;
Time.weaves no witchery
About her toilsome days.
No seers or kings bow down
To laud her peerless grace;
No haughty sneer or frown
Mars her so steadfast face.
Hers is a quiet might.
All innocent of blood :
She labors day and night
To compass others’ good.
From rise to set of sun
Still is she sacrificed.
This slave of Mary’s Son,
This little bride of Christ.
Sad is she many a day.
Oft-times too sad to sing;
Hut where her footsteps stray
The sweetest flowers spring.
She has no thrilling words
To woo the throngs or tench :
She shames no quiring birds
With music of her speech.
Hut' now I sing her praise
In this my grateful song....
Priestess of simple days,
Arch-enemy of wrong!
She still with constancy
Holds open Heaven's door. . ..
The Queen of Queens is she.
The mother of the poor.
x.s.w.
FRANK MORTON.
When You Awake.
A hen you awake in the friendless dark ere
the first faint roosters crow,
And Memory’s vault is opened wide to all
the winds that blow,
llien you lie and think till your brain is
dazed, and your heart is cold and numb,
For tlu> yearning dead draw near to their
own ere the first thin light-rays come.
Ah. a silent house and a ticking clock at
work in the haunted dark,
Then a sentinel rooster’s challenge faint,
and the ghosts are upon you! Hark!
I.was a footfall light in the room, I swear,
r and a cold, caressing hand
That smoothed my brow in an olden way !
Yes, I know and understand!
All forgotten they lie in their narrow graves
where we left them long ago.
And they crave the warmth of a kinsman's
thought when the icy dawn-winds blow;
ror shades are strangers for evermore,
though their tombs make white the
land.
So we hear the call of the long-stilled voice ;
and the touch of the vanished hand
1’ alls light and cold on our faithless brows
when the world’s in slumber deep,
And we lie and stare till our laggard hearts
their tryst with the dead must keep.
How comes it else that we lie inert while
the visiting shadows sit
With the books that drug our lives at hand
and the rescuing lamp unlit?
Lie down! Lie down in your darkened
)rr ,. room > ancl shed if you can a tear!
Lis but the tribute you owe your dead—■
and your own death-day draws near.
I’ riendless and cold in their graves they lie,
for Death is a tyrant power,
ho lets no poor, grey prisoner slip; but
dawn is their one brief hour.
So bring them in as you would a guest
whose heart in his breast beats still;
Commune in spirit with those who rest in
the graveyard on the hill.
'Tis the bravest, bleakest hour you’ll know.
But conquer your dread and see
That Life is at most a fire we pass in the
march of Eternity.
O Ghosts that come in the early dark, I
welcome you in with shame!
So many of you behind—before! And I'm
alone by the flame !
N.S.W.
THE WATCHER.
The Hat.
They’ve carried him up, they have laid him
down,
A ith the smell of the harbor upon his
clothes;
The strange little man who was born to
drown,
A\ ith the little blue face that nobody knows.
The point of the chin and the curve of the
throat
Are hidden from view by a brave cravat;
And he’s buttoned up close in an overcoat;
But his head is bald—and he’s lost his hat!
Somewhere or other beneath the quay,
Bobbing about within easy touch,
Yet hiding away so that none may sec.
Is the little black hat that he needs so much.
The old black “bowler” that went with him
\\ hen he walked out under the midnight
skies;
The sheltering crown and the pulled-down
brim
That hid the purpose within his eyes.
The little black hat which I fain would
place,
Since he may not wear it upon his head,
Across the eyes and the little blue face,
And grant him the privacy of the dead.
So here is my handkerchief; even that
Must hide him from those who are round-
about,.
For a whistling urchin has found the hat
And is kicking and kicking it inside out!
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.

THE EMPTY PURSE.

The Bulletin
The National Australian Newspaper.
“Australia for the White Man.”
Printed and published by William Macleod, of
Musgrave and McLeod Streets, Mosman, for The
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the
Co., 214 George Street, Sydney, N.S.W., Australia.
PUBLISHED ONCE A WEEK.
TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas ’Number, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., ivliich should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
ihrect reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
To Travellers Abroad.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in Lon-
don, after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs.
Gordon and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.

town, Durban and Johannesburg; Dawson and Son,
Capetown and Johannesburg; and others.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 23, 1915.
THE LAST INCH OF THE FATTED
CALF’S TAIL.
By the founders of the Labor movement in
Australia an over-loaded Civil Service and
the maintenance of decorative adjuncts to
the machinery of government were regarded
as abominations. Against such wasteful
public expenditure men spoke and wrote with
passionate eloquence. Here were the weeds
that must be torn away, so that honest
administration, economy and simplicity
might bear fruit, and the burdens caused by
jobbery and ineptitude lifted from the shoul-
ders of the worker who produced all, and in
the end had to carry all. They had faith
in themselves and their doctrines, the men
who preached these things. So, in time,
the public, grown sick of seeing the annual
contributions to the State frittered away,
swept the disreputable old brigade from its
place of authority, and the new regime
arrived pledged to prudence, economy and
self-reliance. The Australian who now looks
backward and round about him for evidence
of fulfilled promise discovers himself in very
much the same position as the man who
set out to gather roses from thistles. He
finds that not only has there been failure to
realise the ideals which had such powerful
influence on the newly-enfranchised
democracy, but that these have been aban-
doned and are even treated with derision.
New conceptions have grown up with regard
to public expenditure. Lulled by sophistry
about “prosperity”; drugged by the loan-
mongers into a condition of acquies-
cence in profligacy; demoralised by the
humbug of Party Government, with
all its wretched tricks and evasions,
the people of Australia have lost their vigi-
lance, have grown uncritical, careless and,
to a very great extent, inarticulate. They
have missed the ’bus. Their relation to
Parliament differs very little from what it
was a quarter of a century ago, because
popular control of the Legislature is as great
a myth as the Legislature’s control of its
own' machine of government. The people
who were going to govern are still the gov-
erned. They are paying for it in a score of
ways; and not one quarter of the bill has
vet been presented.
* * * *
The cost of Government alone in Aus-
tralia has reached figures that are appalling.
The dreadful total of payments for Gover-
nors and Parliaments and attendant trim-
mings, including refreshment rooms, was
last year £725,265. Civil services have
swollen to abnormal proportions. Enter-
prises have been embarked on with cheer-
ful indifference to financial results. Far
and wide the scandals connected with loose
expenditure of money are common topics
of conversation. In every electorate
there is a culvert that cost a million
or something, while a bridge is falling
to pieces because someone is too tired to
drive a nail into it. In every State there
are log-rolled railways that are mops for
public money. Even such a national affair
as the Post Office has become a sieve through
which half a million disappears every year.
Land in towns and country has been re-
sumed by Governments at prices ruinous to
the struggling purchasers. Was it to achieve
this sort of thing—that the social parasite
might fatten and the speculator and boom-
ster be arrayed in fine linen—that the
era of reform was ushered in? Did
the men who made Australia to see the
old evils merely seek for the toiler an
extra shilling or two a day to be at once
rooked of it to maintain an army of public
and private parasites? No such thing can
be supposed. The unhappy truth is that the
Australian democracy has been fooled. In
return for its votes it has been loaded with
debt, and finds itself to-day burdened in an
aggravated form with every one of those
problems it set out so hopefully to solve.
Extravagant government was an evil under
Conservative rule. Does it cease to
be an evil when perpetrated under any
other sort of rule? Is waste any the less
waste under a so-called Labor Admin-
istration than under a Tory Adminis-
tration? And when through patronage Bill
is jobbed into a billet, is the jobbery any
less smellful than it was when the welkin
rang about Percy and his job and the canker
of nepotism? There is only one possible
answer to these questions; and it is because
the reply has never been translated from
words into the .guiding principles of public
policy that the Australian cash-box is leak-
ing like a water-cart. And everyone knows it
is leaking; but the traitors who put the
holes there —who for purposes of momentary
personal ascendancy betrayed the principles
of their followers —lack the courage to mend
them. In their bewilderment they can think
of nothing better than the perpetuation of
their folly, and whenever possible they pour
the proceeds of more pawn into the Treasury
while the leak grows wider and wider.
* * * *
Amid wild yells about taxing the rich man
(who passes the tax on), and in the scramble
to get away with Cohen’s good cash, Minis-
tries have come to be looked upon as distribu-
tors of largesse:—a building for this sup-
porter, a bridge or two for the other, a job in
the railway cutting for Jim, and so on and
so forth. The bill is a thing to be pushed
aside as a nuisance. But that bill has to be
paid, if not to morrow then the day after
to-morrow. Until some way of abolishing
bills can be evolved, the truths that were
truths to Australia sober are truths for Aus-
tralia with a headache. These are (1) that
waste is waste; (2) that work is work; (3)
that to a debtor country production is vital;
and (4) that, where there is waste and too
little work, sooner, or later the shortage must
be made good by taxation, the weight of
which falls on th'e shoulders of the worker.
The maintenance- of seven Cocked Hats in
Australia is a waste which this taxpayer
must make good. A post office that loses half
a million a year, largely because it does not
get fair value for" the money it spends, is a
drain on the same taxpayer. Six Parliaments
are wasteful to the point of insanity. A rail-
way built on principles of shirk places a
permanent toll-gate between the producer
and his market. With the money eaten up
by the herd of white elephants at the State
capitals, by bloated civil services, by muddle
and political jobbery at the Post Office and
multitudinous other offices, by perfunctory
supervision of expenditure on works and of
revenue from services, Australia could have
been developed in these last 10 years as much
almost as it has actually been developed out
of loans, and the workers of the country
saved the shocking drain of interest created
by the recent orgy of squander. Yet even
now, with the world a bonfire, with the
national debt leaping upwards like mercury
at the equator, with the national earnings
materially diminishing and many thousands
of men leaving the Commonwealth, the hunt
for the so-called public works loan goes vigor-
ously on. Caution, economy and toil are
spoken of by the politician with apology, as
if he were a wowser detected in a pub.
Meanwhile from the West comes the voice
of Scaddan announcing that he is out a
million, and is going to be out some more
the next time the books are added up.
Peacock has folded his hands, and with a
plaintive wail of “Goo help us!” has gone
into hiding. N. S. Wales has the Norton
Griffiths shindykit tied up in the backyard,
and a riot pending among the miners. Ryan
is beginning to suspect that Denham sold
him a pup. And John Bull is being im-
plored to send along the price of another
spree—and it all sounds just. as if Aus-
tralians were the only people in the world
unable to extract a. living from a continent.
THE HUMBUG OF IT ALL.
The world is changing daily before our eyes.
Vast tracts once occupied by civilised people
are being transformed into smouldering
deserts. The great nations of Europe and
their dependencies are developing into
armed camps where only war-business is
carried on in a serious spirit. The world’s
best blood is flowing in rivers. Epic things
are being done on a thousand battlefields,
and deeds of splendid altruism are occurring
away from the firing-line. In recognition
of the fact that the British Empire is up
against the biggest problem in its stormy
history, almost every self-respecting member
of it has adjusted his point of view to meet
the exigencies of the situation. .Provision
has to be made for so much, not only now
but in the long years to come. The enemy
has to be beaten. After that mighty task
is accomplished he will need to be dealt
with, either by the confiscation of most of
his navy or by prohibitive tariffs, so that
he' may never again be in a position to con-
vulse civilisation. And then there must be-
gin the long, long argument about the spoils.
There is an unimaginably large job anead.
Nothing can ever be the same again. Hardly
anything is. the same now; the politicians
always excepted. The greater part of the
civilised world is in chaos ; Pain and Sor-
row, preceded by their friend Fear, roam
everywiiere; the poor are giving their lives,
the rich their money, and the great their
brains to the cause; and, in the midst of
the upheaval, like a dog in a dust-storm,
appears the party politician proceeding furi-
ously with his wretched business as usual.
* * * *
The party machine may be seen at work
on all sides; and everywhere its influence is
disastrous. The Fisher Government is jib-
bing at Conscription on party grounds. The
big unions are not attracted by the idea of
universal service for the purposes of over-
seas fighting. Also, there is a big floating
vote made up of Peace Society enthusiasts,
youthful shirkers and their relatives, anti-
Nationalists of the I.W.W. stamp, and many
others, which is solid against universal ser-
vice. That is why one finds Liberal patriots
of a certain brand mischievously refusing to
state their views on the subject “till the
Government has given a lead” and explain-
ing later, with tongue in cheek, that they
didn’t mean by this remark to put their
friends of the Labor party in an awkward
position —on the contrary, all they desired
was to avoid embarrassing those who are
conducting the war! Party considerations
having thrown the Holman Government out
of sympathy with Fisher, the Premier
and a number of his colleagues put
the National Government in an in-
vidious situation by joining a universal
service league and talking a policy which
they have no power to enforce, and which
they know the Fisher party is afraid to
touch. In part, this may be patriotism;
but most of it is the other thing. In N. S.
Wales the imperishable Joseph Carrutiiers
has resurrected himself and blossomed forth
in rivalry of the more moderate C. G. Wade
as a flamboyant anti-German. It is cheap
heroism to be an anti-German these days.
The only real nerve that is required is in
the case of foreign-traders like Carrutiiers
who, after a life-time spent in helping Essen
and the rest of the great German manufac-
turing centres by the advocacy of the fiscal
legislation which those places most needed,
think to purge their old offence by con-
doning the smashing of resident Germans’
windows. That variety of courage is freely
in evidence. The loudest clamorers of our
anti-German leagues are the low-tariffists of
the past. There is no Imperial purpose to
be served by putting a brick through a Ger-
man’s window. In fact, it is a regrettable
occurrence, inasmuch as it violates law and
order. The abuse that has been directed at
Holman because he deprecated this variety
of anti-GermaniSm was Liberal-party tactics
first and patriotism a very long way after-
wards. And the same applies to most of the
other humbug that, is being talked, raved
and howled respecting local Germans by
Liberals.
In the Australian Parliament a violent
row is brewing on the Referenda question.
There is not the slightest doubt that, the
Government needs additional powers if it
is to organise the Commonwealth effectively
for war purposes. Its business during the
coming months will be not merely to train
and send away so many thousand men per
month, but to clothe, arm and equip them
and supply the needed ammunition. If the
unified Government of Britain finds diffi-
culty in doing this, how much greater must
be the problem in the case of a Federal
Government such as ours, working under a
system comprising seven States each with
sovereign industrial powers? The Fisher
Government wants the new powers badly;
but it appears to want the party advantage
which is involved in the carriage of the
Referenda questions a good deal more. The
leaders of the Liberal party have admitted
that the Federal system is too weak to be
satisfactory in war-time, and that the
National Parliament requires to be strength-
ened with fresh powers. But it won’t yield
on the political dispute which has raged,
for so long round the Referenda questions
—won’t yield even to help to end the war.
It is anti-Sosh first and pro-Empire next.
There is a middle way which has been indi-
cated by The Bulletin. It would give
neither party the chance of a political tri-
umph, but it would save time and money,
and avoid that ill-feeling which is undesir-
able in a great crisis. Briefly, the scheme
is for Parliament to put through an Act con-
ferring on itself the needed powers for the'
currency of the war , which Act the High
Court would be asked to validate in toto, as
it is empowered to do under the Judiciary
Act. But the Laborites are unwilling.
They think they see in the war ■ an engine
to give them that which they failed to get
in the less urgent days of peace.. And the
Liberals won’t have any half measures. It
is their conviction that in rushing on the
Referenda questions the Fisher party is
proceeding to its doom. Thus both sides
are gambling for a party advantage at the
country’s expense.
* * *
The ceaseless old party rows are unedify-
ing at any time; at this particular period
they are shocking, as nauseating as the
spectacle of a drunk on a hearse. When
one gets down to bedrock, what are all these
vociferous little men quarrelling and slander-
ing and hurling wild and baseless
innuendoes about? It is mostly a sordid
question of personal gain. Smith has the
luck to be in office. It is Brown’s business
to get him out. To that end Brown must
fill the public mind with distrust and hatred
of Smith-; he must show that Smith, if not
of German birth, is the possessor of a Ger-
man aunt or mother-in-law or second cousin
twice removed. The implication is that
Smith’s sympathies are with Prussia; that,
in short, he is a traitor; and that the coun-
try is being betrayed. If Brown is success-
ful he gets Smith’s job; and the scandalous
business of quickly ousting him, on the only
really reliable lines available, is cheerfully
undertaken by Smith. There is something
ludricous about the circumstance that the
whole war business of a country which is
borrowing and spending in millions, and
which is sacrificing its best men with a
lavish hand, is being hampered by this
struggle for a few jobs worth about £l5 or
£2O per week. It would pay Australia well,
in the circumstances, if it could but say to
the trouble-makers in its multitudinous Par-
liaments : Take the cash and perquisites
and in return stop wasting time and get
down to solid work. With no one in Oppo-
sition, with everybody entitled to draw
(say) £l5OO per annum and to use a
Government motor-car, the incentive for all
this humbug might cease.
A LETTER TO THE FRONT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I suppose you sometimes dream, Biel, in
between the scraps out there,
Of the land you left behind you when you
sailed to do your share:
Of Oollins-street, or Itundle-street, or Pitt,
or George or Ilay,
Of the land beyond the Murray, or “along
the Castlereagh.”
And I guess you dream of old days and the
things you used to do,
And you wonder how ’twill strike you when
you’ve seen this business through,
And you try to count your chances, when
you’ve finished with the Turk,
And swap the gaudy war-game for a spell of
plain, drab work.
Well, Bill, you know just how it is these
early days of Spring,
When the gilding of the wattle throws a
glow on everything.
The olden days, the golden days, that you
remember well,
In spite of war and worry, Bill, are with
us for a spell.
For the green is on the paddocks, and the
sap is in the trees,
And the bush-birds in the gullies sing the
old, sweet melodies ;
And we’re hoping, as we hearken, that when
next the Springtide comes
You’ll be with us here to listen to that bird-
talk in the gums.
’Tis much the same old Springtime, Bill,
you recollect of yore ;
Boronia and daffodils and violets once more
Fling sweetness over city streets, and seem
to put to shame
The cult of greed and butchery that got you
in this game.
The same old, sweet September days, and
much the same old place;
Yet, there’s a subtle something, Bill, upon
each passing face :
A thing that cannot be defined, a look that
you put there
The day you landed on the beach and
charged at Sari Bair.
It isn’t that we’re boasting, lad ; we’ve done ;
with most of that —•
The froth, the cheers, the flapping flags, the
wildly waving hat.
Such things are childish memories ; we blush
to have them told ;
For we have seen our wounded, Bill, and
it has made us old.
Not with a weary child’s regret, not with a
braggart’s pride,
But with a grown youth’s calm resolve we’ve
laid our toys aside.
And it was you that taught us, Bill; upon
that fateful day,
That we at last had grown too old for ever-
lasting play.
Andv--'aS a grown man dreams at times of
boyhood days gone by,
So shall we, when the mood is here, for care-
free childhood sigh.
But, as clean youth looks out on life, clear-
visioned and serene,
So may we gaze, and ever strive to make our
manhood clean.
When all this strife is over, Bill, there yet
is work to do ;
And in the. bloodless fights to come we shall
be needing you.
We shall be needing you the more for what
you’ve seen and done,
For you were born a Builder, lad, and we
have just begun.
There’s been a deal of talk, old mate, of
what we owe to you,
Of what you’ve braved and done for us, and
what we mean to do.
We’ve hailed you as a hero, Bill, and talked
of just reward,
When you have done the : job you’re at, and
laid aside the sword.
I guess it makes you think a bit, and weigh
this gaudy praise ;
For even heroes have to eat, and there are
other days;
The days to come when we no more need
stalwart sons to fight,
When the wild excitement’s over, and the
Leeuwin looms in sight.
Then therfeis-another fight to fight, and you
will find it tough . ...
To doff the khaki for a suit of plain civilian
Stuff: , ,
When' all the cheering dies away and hero-
worship wanes, i
Yen’ll have' tp face the old drab life and
fight for other gains ;
For still your land will need you, as she
needs each sturdy son, .
To fight the fight that never knows the firing
The < quiet° fight, the steady fight, when you
shall prove your worth, .
And milk a cow on Yarra Flats or drive a
quill in Perth.
The gold is on the wattle, Bill; the sap is
in the trees, . ,
And the bush-birds in the gullies sing the
old, sweet melodies.
There’s a good, green land awaiting you
when you come home again
To swing a pick at Broken Hill or ride
Yarrowie Plain. , , , on .
The streets are gay with daffodils, but, Hag-
gard in the sun, ,
A wounded soldier passes; and we know old
days are done. , .
For down, deep down within our heaits, is
something you put there
The day you landed on the beach and
charged at Sari Bair. _
VICTORIA.
DEN.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Futility of Roars.
It boils down to this —Australia must
produce goods. Wheat and boots and meat
and blankets and all the other things, from
mouse-traps to pianolas. And it all can be
put into one word —work. That is what
Australia has to do; that is the meaning
behind all that is worth listening to in the
flood of words now pouring over the country
about “the development of industry” and
“the limitation of imports.” It is what the
shout of “Never again” should mean if it
were an honest, one. But it happens that
this phrase has been debased to the level
of a mere catch-cry. The mouths from
which it issues with the loudest roar are
very much more concerned about making a
violent noise than giving helpful counsel
to the nation. These people are in the
majority of cases not interested in the point
that is of vital consideration to Australia.
They keep on harping day and night about
Germany—about “smashing German trade”
and not having any more business .transac-
tions with the Hun. and similar meaningless
nonsense. But how often do Australians
hear from these windbags any warning
against the perpetuation of the system of
which our trade with Austria and Germany
was only a detail? They know that econo-
mically there has been no change, that what
was fundamentally rotten before is growing
putrid now, and that instead of Australia
feeding one set of aliens she is busy doing
nothing to prevent another lot growing fat.
This is no use to Australia. It means stick-
ing in the same old hole, snoozing con-
tentedly in the sun, borrowing sixpence to
get an imported drink, and gassing about
our “abounding prosperity.” Cackle about
the throttling of German trade is of exactly
the same type of foolishness as the enemy’s
frantic strafing of England. Whatever the
result of the war, German factories and
workshops and laboratories will be reaching
out for trade before the soldiers have hung
their rifles up—and they will be getting a
share of it. Sixty million people cannot be
tied .up like a tribe of Esquimaux. But
consideration of what is going to happen
to the aniline dye, and the cheap watch, and
the piano which was born in Stuttgart but
proclaims itself as of the Bulldog Breed, is
, minor importance to Australia compared
with what is going to happen to Australian
trade. If when the war is over the country
is still afflicted with the same old lunacy
of wearing the imported sock and the in-
dented singlet—if it is wearily sending
lumps of copper over the sea and dragging
back copper saucepans and wire—then it
might as well get its socks and saucepans
from Hamburg as from Shanghai or Ala-
bama. Therefore The Bulletin wishes to
make the casual remark that the time
is ripe to knock off blowing out hot-air
regarding the Hun and to get down to busi-
ness here in Australia.
John Bull and Conscription.
Little comfort was to be got out of last
week’s news from the British Parliament.
Kitchener’s army, amazing estimates of
which had been published in American maga-
zines, is any unknown quantity in excess of,
say. 1,500,000. The inexplicit Asquith
spoke of “an aggregate of not far short of
•>,000,000 men who have offered their services
to the navy and the army.” No hint was
given as to what proportions had been offered
to the respective services, or how many of the
offers had been turned down by the medical
examiners. Of those actually accepted for
the army, A. C. Ameby, M.P., said that
“several drafts had been sent back because an
officer reported that it would be murder to
send old men into the trenches. Another
officer had told him that there were any num-
ber of unfit who were unable to march or
shoot.” All that Kitchener could say to
make the House of Lords happy was : “There
is now abundant evidence of a process of
demoralisation having set in among the
Turks”—which has been said once a week in
the imaginative press since the end of April.
It was said of Austria a year ago. As for
the life or death question of conscription,
Kitchener merely spoke of “whatever sacri-
fices may prove to be necessary,” and
Asquith enveloped himself in the usual
clouds of “ifs” and “buts.” Meanwhile, a
couple of million able-bodied slackers are
fooling away the time they should be devot-
ing to drill, and the absurd policy of “put-
ting the best face on it” encourages the
Thomases, of the House to threaten revolu-
tion if universal service is introduced. A
democracy has a perfect right to turn down
conscription if the majority feels that way,
just as the majority has the right to decide
to eat the German leek if it likes that food.
But in a democracy it is the most criminal
thing in the world for any party to talk
revolution in order to get its own way ; and it
is little less than criminal for a Government
not to set out the whole of the facts for the
democracy’s guidance.
♦
The Failure of the Policy of Borrow
and Bloat.
The enormously over-capitalised Great Cobar
Co., owned principally in England and
France, has, been gradually drifting into
worse and worse difficulties for five years
past, and now threatens to close down
altogether, in its extremity it approached
the N. S. Wales Government for a loan. The
party caucus considered the proposal, and
turned it down. Nevertheless, Holman
cabled to London that the State would stand
behind the co. for £40,000. Then lie called
a meeting of the Caucus, explained that
when Estell, the Minister for Mines, brought
the matter forward a couple of weeks before,
“he was unable to do himself justice,” and
proceeded to convince the crowd that any
apparent lack of candor in connection with
the affair had been “accidental.” Finally, of
course, he asked for ratification of his action.
Caucus strained horribly at the bolus Hol-
man offered, but swallowed it at last—and
has felt very unwell ever since. For this
hole-and-corner gambling transaction the only
shadow of justification advanced is (T) that
unless the company had been supplied with
£40,000 several hundred men would have been
thrown out of work; (2) that Mr. Holman
would have been bothered by them. The
same plea could, of course, be advanced for
shoring up the business of every incompetent
and bungling trader in the community; and
when the assistance is granted to a foreign
concern, how can it be consistently denied to
our own citizens? Still, discreditable as the
whole business is, it has its value since it
illustrates the fatuity of the wild borrowing
policy. Millions and millions have been
raised and squandered in every direction to
the great profit of sharks of every degree-
and the net result of it all is that the State
is less able to absorb the labor and industry
of the people than it was 50 years ago.

The Drunk and the Doctor.
The Bulletin has been waiting impatiently
for some time in the expectation that some-
one would do something or other in the
matter of Dr. Edgar Caro, of Sydney. He
applied recently for letters of administra-
tion in the estate of one Arthur Jamieson,
who had died in his private hospital. Jamie-
son was an inebriate who, in the words of
Justice Street, delivering judgment on
August 4, had “impaired his bodily health
and probably weakened his mental powers
by the life he had led.” He was brought to
Dr. Caro’s hospital suffering from the effects
of a drinking bout. He complained of pain,
and the doctor proposed giving him morphia.
Before doing so he suggested that the ruin
should make a will. The ruin agreed. The
doctor suggested that whatever property he
had should be left to him “to use for moral
and religious purposes.” Again the wreck
said yes. It may be noted here that, to again
quote the probate Judge, “Dr. Caro was
almost a complete stranger to deceased.”
The will made, the doctor administered a
quarter of a grain of morphia. An hour
later another quarter of a grain was given.
A few hours after that the patient died. In
his certificate the doctor said that the cause
of death was urinea. Faced later with a
grave medical difficulty, he explained that
when he said urinea he meant heart failure,
or words to that effect. The Judge in dis-
missing the doctor’s application and refusing
him costs out of the estate said that “the
dispositions in the will did not come spon-
taneously from testator,” that Caro’s explan-
ation respecting the death certificate was not
satisfactory “as to its sincerity o,r its genu-
ineness,” that he had not been sufficiently
candid and frank, and his conduct generally
had been unsatisfactory. The British Medi-
cal Association is relentless with the doctor
who advertises. A year or so ago it aston-
ished many thousands of people by expelling
a doctor who had associated himself with a
“manipulator”—a gifted person who had
cured many cases of dislocation and the like
by unorthodox methods. Here is a doctor
whose conduct has called for this scathing
reflection from the Bench —who has done
something far more discreditable (at any
rate, in the eyes of those on whom the pro-
fession depends for a living) than if he had
paid for a recital of his merits in every
paper in the Commonwealth ; and though he
may not be a quack, as the profession under-
stands the term, yet on his own evidence he
has given an incorrect death certificate.
There is another conceivable aspect of the
case, namely that the death certificate sup-
plied the correct explanation of the patient’s
death. In that event, another and more
serious field for inquiry is opened up. But
in either case the position is one which calls
for investigation by somebody. Any old
scrutiny would give the community a greater
sense of security than the murky silence
which has followed the judicial blistering.
A Thriving Industry, and the Sure Way
to Discourage It.
A witness before a N. S. Wales Parlia-
mentary Committee had this to say a few
days ago:—
I saw a series of advertisements in the Herald
requesting people to apply for the adoption of little
infants. I was so appalled at the thought of the
future prospects of these infants that I cut out the
advertisement and sent it to Mr. Green, and asked
whether his department could net take steps to pre-
vent the insertion of such advertisements. He told
me that Dr. Mackellar wrote to the Herald asking
that such advertisements should not be inserted
because of the terrible effect involved to the com-
munity and to these little waifs of children, but the
Herald still continued to insert the advertisements.
I think it ought to be an offence for the Herald or
any other paper to insert such advertisements asking
people for a premium for adopting children.
The private-enterprise baby-farmer is one of
The Bulletin’s oldest acquaintances. The
effect of her activities on the population
problem is almost as great as that of her
cousin-german, the abortionist. The “kind
lady wing take chd, cash dn, no quests,”
needs to advertise only when she is em-
barking on the profession. After she has
been in business for a while she gets a “con-
nection,” like any other successful practi-
tioner, and thereafter her calling runs itself.
There are two varieties who operate, with
moderate security, behind the ramparts which
guard the British subject’s home. The first
is a plain murderess, who for a matter of
£25 or so takes in a baby on the understand-
ing that she is to look after it for life. If
the child gets as much as £5 worth of exist-
ence it is all it can fairly expect in the cir-
cumstances. The second variety boards the
unwanted infant for a few shillings weekly
This system is liable to be crueller in the
long run than the direct-slaughter method
Every now and then the community is
shocked by police-court cases in which women
are proceeded against for having in their
possession children, emaciated and vermin-
ous, who are mostly described as the offspring
of the accused, but who are reasonably sus-
pected by the Court of being waifs. A
case came under notice the other day of a
slum household which featured three small
children. They belonged ostensiblv to the
people who rented the dwelling. The
alleged mother went daily to work, leaving
the babies tied by their legs to tables and
chairs, with a little bread soaked in sugar
and water within reach. Just as it is im-
possible for the authorities to know how
many abortions occur per annum, so it is
impossible for them to locate a fraction of
the people who go in for baby-farming in a
quiet way. It is no doubt lamentable that
tli' - ' press should aid and abet such a business,
but whoever thinks that the abuse is to be
ended, or even greatly reduced, by the mak-
ing of baby-fanning advts. an offence sup-
plies the last word in optimism. The only
way to save the .life of the unwanted child,
on a large scale, is for the State to provide
institutions where mothers can leave such
children. Also, our social laws being what
they are, the mother must be able to aban-
don her infant with as much secrecy as
though she had taken it to a baby-farm.
The wowser is bound to wail at the notion
of Sin being shielded from punishment, and
at the scandal of the State entering into
competition with assassins. But the State
can always reply that the preservation of
human life is of greater moment than either
of these things.
Let us contemplate our Holman in his advo-
cacy of Conscription:—
When one reads of the whole of the undergraduates
of Oxford and Cambridge serving in the trenches—
men who represent the intellectual elite of Britain;
upon whom Britain will have to rely for leadership
in years to come —one cannot help feeling that it is
a pity there is not some power to intervene and send
these men back to positions where they would be
doing still more valuable service for their country,
and replace them at the Front by other men who
are equally good as fighters, but whose departure for
the Front would cause less of a gap in civil life.
Either Holman believes this, in which case
he is a snob and utterly unfit to be leader of.
a democratic party, or he doesn’t believe it,
and was trying to turn the conscription move-
ment into ridicule, in which case he is a
traitor in the National Service party. Wher-
ever the truth may be, a petition should
immediately be circulated for signature im-
ploring him, in the interests of the nation, to
hold .his tongue. Meantime this deliverance
of his should be framed and suspended at
the P.L.L.’s place of meeting as the most
awful sample of blither in the Australian
Labor party’s history. It should help the
men who sweat for a living to measure up
the glib and shallow humbug who has been
masquerading as a “leader of the demo-
cracy” all these years.
The organisation of British scientific
resources seems bound to result in the
crushing defeat of extensively-advertised
German “nerve foods” and things. Thanks
to costly advertising, numerous brands of
German goods had pushed all competitive
compounds off the market; but with the
sudden aversion to the Hun came a new
confidence in local inventive capacity. Fol-
lowing Professor Ray Lankester’s critical
study of modern scientific achievement,
showing how little was the part Germany
had played in it, old Sir William Crookes
and others struck a deep note of disrespect
for German kultur, since when Britain’s
deep-rooted faith (watered by advertisement
streams) in the virtue of the German pre-
parations has withered away under the hot
blast of rival advertisements. These old
costly “foods” of one sort and another were
originally exploited on the supposition that
Germans were pre-eminent in inventive
genius. Crookes mildly remarked that it
was a supposition which was inexplic-
able to French and British chemists. The
greatest discoveries had been made outside
of Germany, and it was absurd to think
that any of the reputable commercial pro-
ducts of the enemy country couldn’t be
equalled or excelled bv British firms. The
ball, of disrespect for German boastfulness
having been thus set rolling, it gathers as it
goes along. Every German is as ardent an
advertiser of everything German as a
stinking-fish” Australian is a derider of all
things manufactured in his own country.
'^ c . world . was bluffed, bounced and
Loodled into taking pretensions of the enemy
much, too seriously. But the many-sided
conspiracy has come to light, and is being
scrutinised now from all points of view.
Ihe Old Soldier” theorises:—
The tremendous extension of the Austro-German
armies is becoming too great, apparently, for even
the resources of the two Empires. Germany and
Austria combined cannot conquer Russia, because
they cannot support the necessary burden of the
operations sufficiently to win decisive victories. The
black-soil plains over which their armies must neces-
sarily plough their slow and toilsome way are prac-
tically impassable for heavily-laden baggage trains
and artillery, except at a very slow pace. The
enormous extension of the three'fronts on which the
two central Powers are fighting, though it gives them
what strategists call the “interior lines,” gives them
no line of retreat, and compels them to carry on the
war of entrenchments that has been forced on them
to the last extremity, no matter how long that may
It is a war that must depend for its duration on
the resources of the Powers engaged, and the two
central Powers have madly, apparently in despair
burdened themselves with an impoverished ally in
Turkey, under the belief that the Unspeakable held
the greatest strategical position in Europe at Con-
stantinople. It is certainly a great position, and
would be an immensely strong one if there were
adequate resources available for its defence; but the
Turk cannot command them in either men or
material, and there is the Hun’s difficulty. He is
himself very hard pressed on every front —on none
can he much more than hold his own —-and if he
could spare help he cannot send much of it to
distant Constantinople, because every road is being
rapidly closed. Furthermore his enemies are learn-
ing to supply themselves with warlike stores, and
they are acquiring the new cult of siege-war—sap-
ping and mining-—and are accordingly becoming really
dangerous opponents. In all of which respects the
Hun is up against trouble.

dainty fish are unobtainable, and even the
common barracouta is frequently sold at Is.

is costing from Is. a lb. upwards; that cer-
tain fishermen at Port Franklin say they
reap the rich return of from -]d. to Jd. per
lb. for their catches; and that these fisher-
men offer to sell their catches to the Govern-
ment at Id. per lb. all round. The Govern-
ment has set John Weary to look into the
matter; so why the nefarious middlemen
should he in such a hurry to fleece the pub-
lic Heaven only knows.
The curse of “Quidam” :
The idea imbedded in the minds of Australian fruit-
growers appears to be that the principal desiderata
for the prosperity of the industry are wages of about
ss. a day and prices ranging from 10s. a case up-
wards. If shorn of these advantages they prophesy
nothing but ruin. This view has lately received a
shock by the publication of an account of the apple-
growing industry in Hood River Valley, Oregon
(U.S.A.), by a former resident of Dural (N.S.W.).
Wages there, it seems, range from Bs. a day upwards,
even Japanese packers getting 9s. a day, and striking
occasionally, while the grower nets only about 4s. a
case. Codlin moth, bitter pit, apple scale and
water core are all in evidence, and have to be com-
bated by spraying, etc. ; yet the industry is on such
a solid footing that, with raw land at from £2O per
acre upwards, and clearing from £lO to £3O per
acre, £6OO and £BOO per acre has been refused
for bearing orchards. The outstanding features of
the industry are that all fruit has to be graded and
packed according to certain standards, inspectors
being continually employed in preventing any
departure from these standards, and refrigerator cars
and cold-storage plants stop serious deterioration
of the fruit on its way from the orchard to the
consumer. So long as each Australian grower packs
his fruit according to a standard of his own, so that
about 200 sizes and qualities of fruit can be obtained
from any case labelled “Extra choice,” so long will
the Yankee industry be considerably more profitable
than ours. When the Australian grower organises
his calling on business lines, and when, instead of
moaning mournfully about prohibitive wages, he uses
his brain more and his hands less, he will begin to
discover that it is not outgo alone that matters,
but the difference between his expenditure and his
income. The thought of employing labor gives the
average Australian horticulturist the jumps ; yet the
really get-rich-quick person devises a method by
which he can employ 50 laborers and get a profit out
of each. It is that which allows the American
growers to buy motor-cars, while the parsimonious
Australian, who puts his brains into cold storage,
goes to sleep in a spring dray.

Some remarks of The Bulletin re Sir
Edgar Speyer are reprinted in a booklet,
"The House of Speyer,” published by the
London Financial Mail. It is mainly a
collection of articles dealing with Speyer’s
financial past and his present position as an
English baronet and- Privy Councillor. Its
incidental revelations are even more depress-
ing than its direct attacks on the German
hanker whose British title is shared by a Ger-
man wife. A list of his flotations since 1897
is mostly a record of bad investments for the
British public; yet Prime Minister Asquith,
writing to him on May 22 last, said: “The
King is not prepared to take any step in
regard to the marks of distinction which you
have received in recognition of public ser-
vices and philanthropic munificence For
example, the Madeira Railway 51- per cent,
mortgage bonds, which this munificent philan-
thropist offered for subscription at 971 in
1913, were down to 29f a couple of months
ago. But it seems waste of time to cast any
further suspicion on the character of
Asquith’s friend. It is enough that his
business is linked with that of his German
father in Frankfurt and of his German bro-
ther in New 7 York, the brother being openly
friendly with Dernburg, the truculent “diplo-
matist” wdio —as the Morning Post put it- -
“arranged the matter of the Lusitania.” More
disheartening than any of the facts concern-
ing the notorious aliens who have led!
Asquith by the ear is the exposure of Ger-
man influence in the War Office. The war
was already three months old when the
Financial Mail said :
Our Government possesses near Enfield a beauti-
fully-equipped factory for explosives. A well-known
firm of engineers, being anxious to supply machinery,
asked for drawings and specifications, 'but was in-
formed by the Government officials that all such
particulars were in the hands of Werner, Pfleidereu
and Perkins, Ltd., an engineering firm with huge
works in Germany and offices in London.
And the trouble is that nobody can feel hope-
ful that the German influence has even yet
completely spent itself in Britain.

In order to plan an Australian arsenal the Defence
Department is sending five men to India, not one of
whom, I believe, has any expert knowledge about an
arsenal. What use are two engineers, a University
teachei, a. chemist and a gunner in planning ar,
arsenal? Why not give George Sydenham Clarke,
now Lord Sydenham, the job? Why not ask the
Director of Ordnance at Woolwich to put on a couple
of draughtsmen to prepare and forward plans? What
wild extravagance it is to make a picnic for five
people to a place like India, which has no modern
arsenal, and which depends to-day on Vickers’ and
Armstrongs' for all its shells and guns and rifles 1

The Australian speaks:
Call ns to you with summer bush-smoke
blown,
When the red devil leaps amongst the trees !
Summon your sons, and we will spring from
ease
To action when our country’s need is known !
Aye! to the blazing end we are your own —-
Ready to answer when the fire-call comes,
And the wind throbs like Panic’s beaten
drums
In trembling groves whence frightened birds
have flown.
Call us again with drifting battle-smoke,
When for great Freedom’s cause you need
your sons
To hurl in your defence a thunderstroke!
Ah, show us where the sacrilege was
wrought,
And as with boughs of green old fights we
fought,
So will we flail new fiends with leaf-brown
guns!

Is the Empire Fighting Over Words or
For Its Life?
The meaning of it all is simple enough.
Are we going to save our bacon? The
answer to that settles the rumpus concern-
ing national service. There are three pos-
sible results —victory, defeat or an adjourn-
ment. By Britain and incidentally by Aus-
tralia the two latter contingencies are
synonymous with disaster. John Bull and
little Johnnie have either to take a hiding
or give the other fellow one, and the only
sane way to set about doing this latter is
to organise for the job on lines that, besides
leading to the maximum of damage to the
enemy, will cause the least amount of injury
to the victors. Expressed in terms of pounds,
shillings and pence, every month that is
spent in shilly-shallying with vital questions
represents stupendous economic loss; every
life sacrificed because of the army being of
inadequate size for its job is an asset
wasted. The Empire can’t turn back: it
must go on. Then what is the use of quib-
bling.about the meaning of conscription and
splitting verbal hairs on the subject of
national service? It was long ago evident,
lr j spite of the conspiracy to keep the truth
hidden, that the war was going to be one
of terrible intensity, that the enemy was
powerful, well-equipped and resourceful,
that with the Allies things were going
not too well. Shortages of
were at last revealed —of shells, of
machine-guns, of rifles, of men. The
experiment had been tried by Britain
or conducting a war of life and death in
the gloom of secrecy, from which the facts
only emerged by accident; and even now,
after all the glozing half-truths of a year
have been swept away, the officialdom which
chloroformed the nation into a fatuous sense
of security palters in timid, wavering hesi-
tancy with the shibboleths of peace and the
stark realities of war. The broad outlines
of the mighty struggle on land are clear
enough.. Russia is reeling backwards; on
Gallipoli the Allies have little more than a
foothold; the Italian advance into Austria
is being firmly disputed; in Belgium and
France the enemy holds his ground. The
need for every pound of pressure which the
nation can exercise is not disputed. If that
pressure can .be applied in any other way
than by calling to arms everyone who is
able to strike a blow against enslavement,
the only nation which shrinks from the task
should. explain what the method is. If
there is no working alternative, then the
flustered doctrinaires who object to defeat
beina averted by victory over their formula
should take themselves to a remote place and
crawl out again when the world has brousrht
tho great strmrerle to an end. These are
times for blood and action, not for subdued
combats about words.

Asked to select a successor in the State
Assembly to “Ted” Larkin, killed at Galli-
poli, Willoughby (Sydney) electors last
week had a Labor man and five nondescripts
to choose from. A third of the electors
voted, and the poll was so close between
Chambers (Labor) and John Haynes that
a second ballot will be taken on Saturday.
Haynes’s return would be a warning to
every man not to quit his billet lest an
enemy should collar it. Therefore at this
juncture,. when the encouragement of re-
cruiting is the main consideration, even the
Liberal electors of Willoughby should recog-
nise that their urgent national «mty is to
assist in returning the of tne same
color as' the man whose ’dace —e chosen
member will fill. Haynes should accord-
ingly go out with the most emphatic thud in
history.
“Mil” : The English position is almost im-
possible of belief. Compared with the German
menace of Britain, Australia is at peace
and impregnable; yet the attitude of the
British is plainly shown by the affirmation
of the anti-conscription resolution of the
Trades Union Congress at Bristol represent-
ing three million unionists. And those three
million unionists represent 15 or 16 millions
of the 47 millions of the U.K. The War
Committee of Workers made representa-
tions to the Government against the ex-
ploitation of bread and coal; but this drift-
ing Asquith Government wouldn’t or
couldn’t move. The Trades Union Congress
affirmed “that the workers were prepared
to make sacrifices for the common cause, but
they would not be bled by financial vul-
tures.” They invited Lloyd George to
“fulfil his promise to deal with war profits.”
The truth is this British Government has
too many wealthy friends—coal-mine own-
ing friends, contractor friends, friends who
can delay the declaration of cotton as con-
traband for nearly 10 months of war. The
Trades Unionist Congress is obstinatelv
short-sighted, but its vision is not to be
cleared by the spectacle of old men who,
over the eligible age themselves, advocate
conscription while piling up war profits,
exploiting food and firing, and handing out
to labor pre-war or less than pre-war wages.
Conscription must come; but plainly the
working men of England will not listen to it
until it also means abolition of war profits.

“From Mill Girl to Millionairess” is the
current insanity at Sydney Adelphi. It is
by Mrs. Morton Powell, the authoress of
“The Bad Girl of the Family” and “The
Lamb. Who Took the Wrong Turnip.” Per-
haps it is just as well that Shakspeare died
when he did; if he had lived into our impati-
ent times circumstances might have com-
pelled him to write blood-boilers of this type,
too. As it was he only got as far as Ham-
let, who was “The Mad Boy of the Family” ;
“THE STROLLERS,” AT SYDNEY
PALACE.
Stroller James and Stroller Halpin in the
“Faust” burlesque.
then he went, and Mrs. Powell, who remains,
has to do her best. Her mill girl is just as
enterprising as her other creations. One
lady (the respectful morning paper calls her
a “lady”), who is after a coronet and the
earl under it, stabs her mother in the back,
shells an orphanage and sets fire to a house
in order to get square with other characters
who have “resolved upon her ruin.” She is
unmasked and foiled just when the wedding-
bell-ringers are preparing to spit on their
hands. The fact that the villain has been
publicly blamed for being lackadaisical seems
to indicate either that he is a short-winded
scoundrel or that he would rather be nailing
a fowl-house together in a far suburb. Still
he threatens his mistress very nicely in the
third act, while the hero walks through the
play pleading helplessly for information like
a plain-clothes policeman. Out of consid-
eration for their feelings, this paper will not
publish the names of the players who are
implicated.

Joseph Blascheck, assisted more or less
by Miss Alyce Austin, started a brief season
at the Little Theatre, Sydney, on Saturday;
and the people with ldndly memories of
this entertainer’s art who found their way
to the remote place sniffed at the mustiness
of the house, which had had a long rest
from shows and showmen. Blascheck was
not at the top of his form, and Miss Austin
was not much in the way of reinforcements.
She is an attractive girl with a crisp touch
on the piano; but her voice is hardly up to
platform requirements, and she works so
hard over some of her items that she forces
her audience to work with her.

Stafford and Co. (there appear to be two
dog-shareholders) came to Sydney Tivoli on

anced bill is otherwise supplied by smiling

She is just the sort of singing comedienne
who would save a dull show and make a
bright one sparkle.
