“Other times, other manners.” If “Ma
Mie Rosette,” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.),
strikes the present generation as being not
quite all that Dad and Mum had cracked it
up to be, the p.g. must understand that
times have changed. Compared with the
show that brought “I Was Dreaming” into
fashion, the present one is sicklied o’er with
a pale change of cast, and situations of
great bygone moment with this regard their
currents turn awry and lose a lot of action —
as Hamlet would say. All the same, “Ma
Mie Rosette” was well worth revival. It
is a tuneful, romantic opera, with good,
durable stuff in it. In fact, Henri of
Navarre as an historic personage is all the
better for being even more dead than when
Brownlow took the floor in long buff boots
and turned his roving eye on Nellie Stewart.
Given the right type of baritone in the part
of the mashful monarch, and a more courtly
crowd of fair women and bearded men in
the Chateau scene, the other shortcomings
wouldn’t seem to matter much.
* * * *
Derek Hudson is a satisfactory Henri only
when he sings. He lacks inches, mannah
and necessary medieval royalty about the
legs. He frowns when he. should express
urbanity, he has not the crudest notions of
suave seductiveness, and when he has nothing
to say or . sing he doesn’t count in the pic-
ture. Miss Cadman makes just a charming
Rosette as far as she goes. Her tuneful pre-
sentation of a cup of milk to the King, and
some of her tiff with the rustic Vansong in
the first act, are admirably done. It is when
she talks back to Corisande, and subsequently
goes looking for trouble in her nightie, that
Miss Cadman shows her want of dramatic
ginger and business resource. Reg Roberts
is the best-placed member of the co. His
somewhat weird accent is permissible in a
young Navarrean hayseed of long ago, and
as soon as he drops his Y.M.C.A. method of
playing the lover and begins to chuck his
feelings off his chest, Reginald is really force-
ful and even magnetic. The chief honors of
Saturday night’s performance fell to Van-
song. He was a devil of a Roberts when
roused, and his rough treatment of Rosette
when he caught her in her nightie and
Henri’s arms was worth all the money
charged for admission. Miss Ethel Morxi-
son is an unduly large, loud and emphatic
Corisande. . Phil Smith is always pretty
good as Bouillon, whilst Addie Lennard is a
very variable Martha, and Bantock suggests
an old warrior who is sadly out of condition.

The last nights of “Under Cover” will
probably last another week at Melbourne
Royal, after which Muriel Starr and the
felodramatic co. are to appear in “Nobody’s
Widow,” described as pure, unadulterated
comedy.
“What Happened to Mary” before is
happening to Mary again at Melbourne
King’s, but it is a new heroine who is leav-
ing powder, on John Willis’s breast. Miss
Lesley Adrienne is the lady now, and she
gives girlish emphasis to the in-no-cence
that matches her flaxen hair and her far-
away look. No one seeing the actress could
deny her physical suitableness for the part.
She seems the predestined rural victim of
the dinners and cash proffered by the young
Nut in yachting flannels. Richard Bellairs
is the aforesaid youth, whose disrepute is
presented with a crushing wardrobe and a
smile ample for all purposes. In general
wickedness he is a varnished chip of the old
block. . Edmund Duggan is the old block;
and his scowling, unsteady glance is sup-
posed to suggest that his child-abandonment
lies, heavy on. his mind. The old man has
obviously a bigger burden on his waistband
than on his thinking apparatus, but that is
the festive result of circumstances over
which an expensive appetite has most con-
trol. Bert Bailey in the semi-nautical ap-
pointments of Cap’n Jogifer loads the pro-
ceedings with sea wheezes and plods along
on a stiff leg as before. Miss Roberts’ sniv-
elling is out of tune for the part of Henry
Craig’s soiled dove ; the effectiveness of Mrs.
Winthrop (Miss Bevan) is somewhat ham-
pered by a voice with grit in it; and the
way Guy Hastings as John Willis grabs his
Mary in the end shows that his previous
rigid decorum was only worn in business
hours. Other individual achievements are
Tuck Wintergreen (Fred Macdonald) and
Mrs. Peart (Miss Adeson), who tickle the
public with varying success.

At Melbourne Princess’s the queer adven-
tures of Mary Latimer, Nun, are about to
be discontinued, for “The Rosary” will
return to notice on Saturday.
** * *
The Follies, now back at Melbourne Tiv-
oli, are mostly the same Cannot, Isabelle
D’Armond and Vera Pearce, etc., in a more
or less different assortment of items. The
difference between a Follies turn and any
ordinary turn of Tivoli commerce is that it
doesn’t last long and never takes an encore,
which is a praiseworthy peculiarity. Frank
Greene is the new man who sings alone, or
v/ith the same old chorus, in the garden by
the sea, and Greene’s limited range of warble
is generally understood to have soulful in-
tentions. The opening part of the pro-
gramme is supplied by the nimble Crouch
and Welch, Alfredo, Weems, Desmond and
Dalton, some of whom crop up as Follies
later on.
* * * *
Piquo, a clown of quaint energv on the
horizontal bar, and Maud and Gill, two
bouncing trampoline experts, are leading
the acrobatic division at Melbourne Bijou
this week. In the forefront of the vocal
brigade are Ward Lear, George Fredo and
Annita Primrose. Ward has made the imi-
tation of squeaky wheels and similar noises
his life work, while Fredo is a Dutch-
Amurkan musician and joke-fancier who
exhibits several japes which he caught quite
young. Finally there is Miss Primrose, a
female impersonator who reveals her
changes of costume in full view of the
audience. The turn is not particularly well
acted or sung, but the lady’s figure gives it
a sort of physiological interest.

This week the Hoyt management in Mel-
bourne is illustrating the charms of the
popular film actress, Marguerite Clark, in
a costume romance anent Gretna Green.
Next week the same lady will be exhibited
in five reels of an absorbing story entitled
“The Goose Girl.” Meantime Hoyt’s con-
tinue the hurried narration of the mining
frenzy, “The Master Kev,” which has now
reached such a state of blud and bad feeling
that most of the cast are hollering for iodo-
form and bandar^.
In the middle act of “Playing with Fire”
(produced by the Adelaide Repertory
Theatre) Schnitzler unexpectedly manages
to be dull. The quiet, rather gloomy Franz
gives a careless affection to Christine, who
lives for him only. Presently he is killed
in a duel over another woman, and she has
a nervous breakdown. Beginning and end,
good; second act, plain padding. The acting
was sound, as usual. Whether Christine is
supposed to have charm does not appear; Ivy
Stacey stressed the jealous and exacting
side of the character, and rose well to the
painful scene at the end. Neill gs lover
and Nave as father did Well the little they
had to do, Ray Walsh as a careless friend
was amusing at the start and dignified at
the finish, and Kitty Sullivan was delightful
as a reckless little milliner. This lady has
all the charm of the born actress who has
not yet acquired professional stage-tricks.
The prompter played an unusually fine in-
nings in this piece. The other item of the
evening was “Halt!” a ode-act thrill by a
clever Adelaide lady whose name everyone
knows but who shrinks from print. The
construction is amateurish, an entirely un-
necessary character being brought in at
curtain-rise, and two more at its fall; but
the central idea is well worked. The girl,
living on an up-country station, is going to

** * *
The drama in Adelaide is getting busy
again at last. lan Maclaren, Charlotte Ives
and a strong co. begin an absurdly-short sea-
son at the Royal with three evenings of the
Gibraltar spy play, “Within the Lines.”
Simultaneously, the Tivoli —which has had
Miss Barr and her variety co. for a week —
starts “Lady Godiva,” oddly (but correctly)
described as a “costume drama,” with Elwyn
Harvey to do the bareback-riding act. Sil-
ver, Parsons and Alderman played finely on
their respective instruments at a Town Hall
“patriotic.” At the King’s, Frank Sidney
leaps, Poincare imitates birds and things,
ventriloquist George Ford continues to'be in-
sulted by his wooden friend, and a Victor
Prince combination puts up a long turn
which it calls “revusical.”
A real veteran has died in Adelaide —
Charles Hill, who came to Australia in the
ship that brought the first news of -the
Crimean war, and was promoter of the
Society of Arts of South Aus. in 1856. Art
was always his strong point, and engraving
his specialty. His drawings for an official
picture of the Great Exhibition’s opening
in 1851 brought him the acquaintance of
the Duke ’Of Wellington; and he could talk
of having supped with Tom Hood. He was
91 when the end came.
Another R.C. bishop has gone beyond the
border, Dr. Higgins, of Ballarat, having set
out last week. He was 77 and had been in
the priesthood for nearly 60 years. His first
post in Australia was as auxiliary to Car-
dinal Moran at Sydney; then he went to
Rockhampton to complete and open a cathe-
dral and acquire 19 new churches; and in
1905 lie landed at Ballarat. The clergy of
the diocese had petitioned for the appoint-
ment of Arch-priest Marshall as bishop, but
Higgins was' the lifelong' friend of Arch-
bishop Carr and got the appointment. He
devoted lljmself with great earnestness to
building schools, homes and hospitals, con-
tributing generously to the funds from his
own purse, for the kindly riverence was a
rich man and the brother of one who was
richer still.
So many mistakes have been made by our
side that it is a tonic to read of the mis-
takes of the enemy in organisation—the
point on which he was supposed to be
strongest. A writer with inside knowledge
relates that some'jackets were wanted for
the prisoners’ camp at Posen, and a dealer
got the order, though he did not know where
to find the goods. He applied to a business
friend, who applied to another, and the
jackets were got from a factory in Thurin-
gia. The manufacturer charged six marks
apiece, but on their way to Posen they went
round by Breslau and Berlin, passing
through seven hands and leaving sixpence in
each, so that at their destination they cost
the Government 9J marks. Such muddles
were mostly the fault of badly rattled Gov-
ernment departments which allowed house-
agents to contract for carriage wheels, and
so forth. In poor old Austria things were
worse. But things always are worse in
Austria.
“Costs” : Government departments remind
me of a harmless old lunatic they once had
at New Norfolk (Tas.). Their foible is
producing forms and regulations. Hers was
washing clothes. The other day I had to
collect some money for a soldier who before
leaving for Tophet executed a power of
attorney in my favor. I called on the mili-
cary paymaster and explained that I was
an attorney appointed under seal and all
the rest of it. No good. To begin with he
said he had never heard of my man, and to
prove that it was not worth while finding
out whether he existed informed me that in
any case I must get a “pay-agent” form
executed. The. power of attorney was as
useful as a tailor’s bill. Anywhere else it
would and does answer all purposes; but in
his dealings with the one department from
which my man ought to get most considera-
tion it is apparently valueless.
The terrible experiences of a settler in
the Northern Territory have just been out-
lined by Robert G. Perreau, of Stanleton,
in a letter published by the Palmerston
Times (M.L.). It appears that Perreau
landed with his wife and seven children at
Darwin 18 months ago in order to take up
a block of 1000 acres. He states that the
land is splendid, that it will grow anything
and that he consequently has no excuse for
not working it. His main trouble is, how-
ever, that the Government advanced fencing
material and sufficient building material to
build a good large house, outbuildings and
cow sheds; also 18 milking cows, 50 goats,
three good horses, seven pigs and farm imple-
ments. Instead of dying so that he could
come away from the place, they all insist on
doing remarkably well. Also, instead of
the climate playing havoc with the children,
they absolutely refuse to be sick, and insist
on wearing out clothes and eating large
meals.. The embittered Perreau, in report-
ing this sequence of calamities, suggests that
other Maorilanders should pack up and get
away to the North and the sunshine, and put
up with the same misfortunes. Tiie
Bulletin can only suggest that if, after
inquiry by a .Royal Commission, it is found
that settlers in the Territory are staggering
under such intolerable hardships, the whole
thing should.be exposed and the climatic and
other conditions declared unconstitutional.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Harrison 0.” : My “Poverty Point” com-
ments on “The Fight,” the first production
ot the “Australian National Theatre,” at
Oakleigh (Melb.), have moved J. A. David-
the founder of the theatre, to inquire
(B. 16/9/T5) : “Why should ‘Harrison O.’
ignore the fact that the play deals with
syphilis?” I didn’t “ignore” it —I didn’t
know it. True, some of the speeches seemed
inapplicable to a consumptive, but the fact
that the young man had syphilis was never
mentioned even in the most guarded manner,
ihe writer journeyed back from Oakleigh
with a well-known Bulletin versifier, an
equally well-known black-and-white artist,
and a daily pressman—all hardened play-
goers. The question as to what really was
the matter with the lad who was made the
butt of the doctor’s long-winded orations
was raised; but it was quickly agreed that
there was no reason for supposing that he
had anything other than consumption. Mr.
Davidson now lets us into the secret that the
youth had consumption and syphilis, and
that it was on account of the latter that the
girl to whom he was engaged threw him over.
This last was not even adumbrated. I
gathered, as did others, that the young man
was dropped because the heroine learned of
the shabby manner in which he had jilted a
former sweetheart. “Does the moral have
to be pointed with an axe?” asks Mr. David-
son. No; and there is no reason either why
it should be chopped off with an axe and
buried in Mr. Davidson’s back-yard. If the
man had syphilis why not sav so?
Why present him with consumption in addi-
tion?. Why, if the play “deals with syphi-
lis, is the disease not once mentioned—not
even by some innocuous daily-newspaper
euphuism such as the “red plague”? If the
heroine broke with her fiance because he had
syphilis, why drag in the unnecessary yarn
of the broken-hearted former sweetheart
whom he had jilted? Really, if Ibsen had
written “Ghosts” on similar lines the world
might now be wondering whether Oswald
Alving s troubles was measles or ingrowing
toenails.

The latest enterprise of the Branscombe
Proprietary is a Garden Theatre overlooking
Rushcutter’s Bay, Sydney. Cost of con-
struction, £12,000; object, profit from open-
air entertainment. The amphitheatre will
seat 1700 spectators, and the audiences at
the opening of the show early next month
will be entertained with what Branscombe
calls “drawing-room vaudeville” by the
Violet Dandies —the finest, he avers, of all
the “Dandy” cos. that have made good in
Australia and Maoriland.
“Jwosp”: Australian Aggie Thorn didn’t
have to wait long for a job in the Land of
the Proud. The little woman, clearing out
from her bad luck, went over with Fred
Niblo and his wife, and straight away was
roped in by John Oort for a leading part in
“The Princess Pat,” a new Victor Herbert
comic opera.
Philip Lytton, Ltd., the dramatic caterers
for country shows in N.S.W. and Queens-
land, think they have a sure winner in
Henry Fletcher’s “The Waybacks,” which
they are bringing to Sydney Palace next
month. In an abbreviated and untitivated
form it has been tried out at Wagga and
sundry other centres of Cocky interest, and
the box-office results were so good that
everybody concerned has strong hopes of
being able to apply for a million or two of
the next war loan.
“Norbell”: Poverty Point in Brisbane is
feeling the stress of the times. One con-
tinuous picture-house has pulled down the
blind, and some of the others are sending out
S.O.S. signals. I went to hear Wallace
Brownlow sing at a suburban hall the other
night. He was well supported, but a fune-
real gloom was on the proceedings, and after
the one-time matindc hero, who was in fine
voice, had sung one song as a guarantee of
good faith, it was announced that their
money would be returned at the doors to
those patrons who desired it.
m- Boy”: Guy Innes, news editor of
Melbourne Hekald, has collaborated with
-bred. Hall, who used to pilot Rickards’s
orchestra _ through the shoals of ragtime, in
a patriotic song, “When Australia’s Roys
Come Home.” Innes is an old-established
Bulletin bard, and is responsible for a
large share of.“ Under the Clocks,” the frivo-
lous column in the Herald which during
the last couple of years has been making the
Argus’s “Passing Show” look so sick. He
could write in a more lofty strain than the
pre-arranged title of the ditty warranted,
but it is doubtful if he could perpetrate any-
thing more likely to fill a long-felt want at
patriotic concerts and suburban “evenings.”
“8.J.”: George Dawe, who has been
both hero and villain for Bilanderson, has
gone into camp at Seymour. That he is not
lacking in nerve was shown when, in com-
pany with Dorothy Grimston and Harry
Hill, he attempted to establish an entente
cordiale between patrons of the Tivoli and
Bernard Shaw, by presenting “How He Lied
to Her Husband.” True, it was a failure ;
but the fault was Shaw’s, not Dawc’s.
“0.K.” on Wagner for concert purposes:
In the recent orchestral concert at Melbourne
Auditorium the orchestra was put into the shade by
Gocssens, the Belgian baritone. Goossens is a drama-
tic artist, having a sufficiently big voice, full charged
with natural melody and passion. He neither under-
states the case nor piles on the agony. It happened
that his brilliant turn was followed by the orchestra’s
prelude and closing scene of Wagner’s “Tristan and
Isolde.” This had no dramatic meaning whatever.
It was just a gloomy, boresome waste of trouble. Of
course, the synopsis on the programme spoke of the
“marvellous tone-color of this heart-rending scene,”
but it really carried no more persuasive force than
any of the other dirgey Wagnerian numbers—instance
“MARY LATIMER — NUN,” AT MEL
BOURNE
PRINCESS'S.
The disgraceful lord.
Canon Hill.
Wotan’s long-winded orations—which are occasionally
uttered from the concert platform. Terribly dull bits
of Wagner are imagined by cranks to contain in
tbeir orchestration all the essential harmony which is
achieved in the combination of music, song, dramatic
action and pictorial effect on the stage, whereas these
sombre bits, when torn from their operatic setting, are
equally vague and heavy. Why will musicians—some
of them—persist in claiming impossible power of ex-
pression for Wagner’s music on its own? Melancholy
horn notes and solemn bars of silence may be all right
in their place, which is grand opera. The same re-
mark applies to long stretches of undulating dirge.
But there is very little “tone color’’ in anything
of Wagner’s except the bits that suggest going to
church, or Sunday evening at home with prayers
before bedtime. And if the color were audible the
tints would not be charming. Wagner wrote the
gloomiest of operas for the stage with a merciless
regard for the tout ensemble. He didn’t write music
for the concert platform. Some scenes in his operas
leave a mental impression which is abiding, and the
work as a whole leaves one with a headache which
is transitory. But in his music, heard apart from
the opera, there is no abiding charm. When a really
soulful person like myself has heard the overture to
“Tannhauser” played 50 times he gets to hate it.
It doesn’t bear being hackneyed, like an old Verdi
melody.
‘‘H.O.” ; What is the matter with “Fair”
(B. 16/9/’l5) ? My sniff at some Yankee
plays—“ Bought and Paid For,” “The Law
of the Land” and “Potash and Perlmutter”
—-have inspired him to mention in a trium-
phant tone of ink that the authors of these
mistakes —Klein (fine old English name!),
Broadhurst and Montague Glass —were born
in England. Of course they were; but this
makes them no less Yankee dramatists than
A. L. Gordon, who was not born in Aus-
tralia, was an Australian poet. The three
plays mentioned were written around Yankee
characters, in an Amurkan setting, for
United Shakes consumption in the first
place, and the tail of the star-spangled eagle
is over them all.
“Billy Blue”: The first theatre in N. S.
Wales outside Sydney was built at Emu
Plains in 1830. The Sydney Gazette an-
nounced that the new building “was opened
with the comedy ‘John Bull’ and the melo-
drama ‘Rob Roy,’ performed in a manner
reflecting great credit on the different indi-
viduals composing the dramatis personae.”
The bills announced “John Bull” and the
“Lying Valet,” but in compliance with the
request of several ladies the latter was
abandoned. The old-time reporter announced
that “amongst the highly respectable audi-
ence of upwards of 200,” he noticed “nearly
40 ladies and gentlemen”—and he proceeded
to mention the 40 sheep, so pointedly dis-
tinguished from the eight score goats.
“De Oriel”: He took a keen interest in
the Melbourne show, and the ventriloquist’s
doll caused him huge merriment. After the
twenty-fifth laugh a worried look spread
over his whiskers. “Why. this is a fake,”
he snorted. “The doll isn’t talking at all!
It’s only a dummy.” I think he came
from Sydney.
“Eryander” : That “save” of Dan Barry’s
(B. 9/9/T5l reminds me. It was at a Milan
theatre. _ The leading lady, having quar-
relled with her bosom friend, was putting
aside, for return, every present she had
received from the person, when a filmy
petti _ carried away at the halliards and
deposited itself in a graceful circle around
the lady’s ankles. The house gasped, but
not the lady. Calmly stepping out of the
nebulosity, she lifted it, folded it, and de-
liberately placed it with the other articles.
‘‘Another of the cat’s presents,” she said.
“I’m glad it reminded me of its existence!”
Then the roof had hard work to stay on.

The latest howl in Sydney about prefer-
ence is from the Shop Assistants’ Union,
which complains that Chief Secretary Black
employs non-unionist hands in the State
fish-shops. The union, of course, may not
include any retail fish-sellers, and Black
would be justified in refusing to put a
draper’s assistant on to the job lest he
might, in a moment of forgetfulness, sell
the State’s fish by the yard. Still it is a
public misfortune that the union which
talks least about strike is most liable to be
overlooked. Little points of preference are
never forgotten when militant unions like
the Wharf Laborers, or the Seamen, or the
Railway Workers are concerned.
Europe is not buying jewels just at
present, thank you ; nor is it able to suggest
any time when the man might call again.
So the Queensland gem trade has gone to
live in Queer-street, attempts to open a
market in America having failed. The
pearl-shell industry has also been knocked
endways, and there was a case in the courts
the other day affecting it. Burns, Philp and
Co. had advanced large sums-to various con-
signors of pearl-shell on the security of the
cargoes they had forwarded; and the firm,
tired of waiting for the consignors to realise,
wanted its cash. The Equity Court, how-
ever, decided that the co. could not recover
its advances by a sale of the shell, but it
could sell sufficient to reimburse itself for all
reasonable incidental expenses for freight,
storage, etc., if the amount was not paid
within 14 days. If it isn’t paid it looks as
if someone who doesn’t mind waiting for
something to turn up will be able to get
something worth keeping.
“Hamer”: The announcement that an
Anglo-French loan of a “billion dollars” is
to be floated in America revives an old argu-
ment. According to the rules of arithmeti-
cal progression a billion is 1,000,000,000,000
—a million millions—minor denominations
being easily described as a hundred millions,
a thousand millions and a hundred thousand
millions. But the U.S.A. have made
a thousand millions a billion for the
same reason that they make a place of two
stores and a saloon a city—the feverish desire
to let little things appear big. It is about
time the meaning of the billion was settled,
anyway. In the next war, when 9724 million
troops are in the field, and credits to the
extent of £17.650.000,000,351 are passed to
pay the preliminary expenses, it will be
handy to know how to say the figures so
that the next fool, will be able to understand
you. And meantime billions are convenient
things in which to measure the distances to
various stars and the propinquity of a politi-
cian to his platform.
From The Bulletin’s old contributor
“Jobber,” at present nursing a wound in
Mena House Hospital, Egypt:—
Re successful opportunists (B. 24/6/’l5). I’d like
to parade a few for inspection. There’s the man who
manages to keep well away from the firing line, and
never touches a rifle. He has been known to buy,
from sailors, luxuries like condensed milk, chocolate,
etc., at a ridiculous price. There may be nothing
wrong with that. But isn’t there something wrong
when he retails his purchases to the men behind the
guns at a profit of anything up to SOO per cent.?
There’s an object in khaki not more than a million
miles away from the Front who was caught at that
game, and I’m glad to say he promptly had the
kybosh put on him. I’d like to parade, too, the
rotter who took 80 “disasters” (the best part of an
Australian “quid”) out of a purse he sneaked from
under the pillow of a badly-wounded soldier. Aren’t
these pretty close to the limit ? Oh, no, they’re not
all glorious things that our chaps do, and don’t you
in Australia do us the bad service of lifting us on a
high pedestal and putting a halo round us. The
higher you put us the worse we’ll bump when you
find that we’re not a bit better than we were when we
left.
FALSE PRETENCES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve been to see a show again.
Why did I do this foolish thing?
It brought remorse and bitter pain—
Regret for cash that’s taken wing.
But in the daily ads. they say
There never was so great a play.
I went to see, in point of fact,
“A work of genius, every act!”
I sat it out, and every scene
A fresh infliction was to me;
The plot was thin, the talk was mean,
And on the whole it seemed to be
A musical debauch; likewise
The wit would make your gorge to rise—
Fit pabulum for people who
Make faces through a fence at Kew.
If baker’s bread is not just what
The baker’s man pretends it is,
They fine the seller. Like a shot
They’re down upon the butcher’s biz
If all his meat is “tripe.” We crush
The dairyman who’s milk is slush.
Let’s make these showmen keep the pact
And edict of the Pure Foods Act!
VICTORIA.

VISITOR : “That looks a dangerous weapon!”
BINGHI : “My word! Go through arm, go through leg, an’ his girth—
“close-up go through bingey.”
“Bad Australian” rolls it along behind the
Taffies who have come to congest the
Northern Territory :
Years ago the Government of this country (Argen-
tine) persuaded some Welshmen that it was the finest
place in the world for the propagation of leeks and
eisteddfods; and when the men of Harlech marched
in it dumped them down at Chubut or scattered
them promiscuously over the face of the country
where the Patagonians come from. What a country
it is! The blizzard works overtime seven days a
week ; the natives hide behind their whiskers; the
goat and the ox wear their wool reversed through
wandering about with their backs hunched up against
the gale. The sheep and the wheat and the oats are
generally blown out by the roots, and every family
holds the mortgage down flat with a weight in the
middle in case capital should fly out of the country.
Well, the Welshmen fought the good fight against
Nature and banditos who often had the tacit approval
of the sergeant of police. But the Government didn't
keep as closely to its covenant as Caradoc ap Ewan
had a right to expect; so when the “Australia agent’
(vide the local press) appeared among the descend-
ants of the Druidical priests He found the time ripe
for his “nefarious proper ganda’’ (also vide the local
press) and managed to work up an exodus. There-
fore it was that lately some fine colonising material
swung out from Puerto Madriga for Australia. They
are good stuff these people. But don't make them
n.ny promises that are not to be carried out to the
letter. Give them a fair deal and more Llewellyn
and Rhys will follow. Meanwhile the local press is
as wild as a Turk, and if the man with the proper
ganda puts his face round the Horn again his- pelt
will decorate the front door of the Casa Rosada.
* * * *
A job for George Black: —
This is purely a man’s point of view, but it should
be worth the consideration of the weaker (-ankled)
sex. It is nearly 10 years since that Yankee abomi-
nation, the “Cuban” heel, first made its disastrous
presence known to Australia, and during that period
it has been responsible for more physical anguish,
deformity and discomfort than anything worn by the
sex since the mediaeval girdle of chastity—which, by
the bye, was the cause of the old saying, “Love
laughs at locksmiths.” And now we have the super-
Cuban heel, and every flapper, flapperette and flap-
perina goes up and down not, like Agag, treading
delicately, but bobbing, yawing, reeling and stagger-
ing like small boats in a big sea. And their ankles!
Seven out of every ten of our girls and women have
got weak, deformed or turned-in ankles—and some
haven’t got any visible ankles at all! Of these latter
there are two kinds—the “broomstick” and the “Mul-
lingar heifer” understanding, leg or extremity. The
former has no calf worth mentioning; the latter has
a calf which begins under the knee and ends at the
heel. If girls of 17 or 18 suffer from weak ankles,
how will they feel (and look) when they become
mothers ? Imagine men abandoning their immemorial
common-sense heels for a spindly, wobblesome, foot-
sprainin'g, pointed monstrosity which tilts the un-
happy wearer forward and (as doctors know) has a
most serious effect upon the legs, the spine and the
lower internal organs. Who will help me form the
Women’s Low-Heel League, and advocate a duty of
1-200 per cent, on Yankee boot and shce abortions?
Dic-k (Noel) Wells, one-time well-known
mining man of Australia, was a sergeant at
the Dardanelles until last week lie fell into
the casualty list for the second time. While
training with the Australian army in Egypt
lie got a hint as to the probable procedure of
Moses when striking the rock. Wells was
much exercised as to the origin of the vast
quantities of silicified wood in the Libyan
desert. The “overflow of the Nile” theory
was not acceptable to him, since Nile waters
do not bear sufficient silica in solution to con-
vert old forests into compact chalcedony.
A BIG DROUGHT.
OOTBACK KID (to small brother who has dropped his penny through the jetty): “It's no
good howlin’, I tell yer-yer won’t get it back now till the bloomin’ dam dries
up:”
While supervising the erection of targets on
a rise in the desert, surrounded by flat rock,
stepping down in almost unnoticeable ter-
races for a radius of half a mile, centred by
broken mounds of cellular siliceous mineral,
he suspected an extinct geyser. After locat-
ing several more, he advised Sir Wm. Oakes,
managing director of the Heliopolis Tram-
way Co., to put down a bore at one of them.
At 300 ft. the co. has struck good water. Moses
got the water at surface; but that was 5000
years ago, and probably at that period the
vent of the geyser tapped by the great law-
giver was only beginning to choke, and Moses
had only to crack the siliceous sinter to set
her spouting again.
* * * *
Hr. Cuscaden, about the only man in the
Melbourne City Council with any knowledge
of the science of public health, was quietly
turned down the other day. Referring to
the increase of infant mortality in the city,
Cuscaden said that' it was directly traceable
to impure milk. “The present method of
handling milk was appalling. .. .The whole
supply of Melbourne should be under muni-
cipal control.” The Council proceeded with
the next business. But when Attorney-
General Mackinnon asked the city to permit
fruit and fish hawkers to continue to trade
in Queen-street, the Council, which can see
only the shopkeeper who pays the swollen
rent and passes it on to the consumer, de-
cided on a war of extermination. The citi-
zen who can’t afford to buy fish and bananas
at the price of ice in Tophet' doesn’t count;
and the murdering of infants with bad milk
is a vested interest.

The lady with a morbid imagination is no
modern development. Adelaide Register dis-
inters, in its 50-years-ago column, the official
report of the Inspector of Railways in 1865:
“Gentlemen passengers, as well as railway
officers of all classes, constantly refuse to
travel singly with a stranger of the weaker
sex, under the belief that it is only common
prudence to avoid in this manner all risk of
being accused, for purposes of extortion, of
insult or assault.” The “weaker sex”—from
which the Unprotected Male had to flee in
“common prudence” !
“Tibs”; The daily papers make it plain
that the movement lias been carefully thought
out. The Bear will fight stubbornly back to
the Ural mountains, luring Von Ilindenburg
into Siberia. Then they will lure him on
with terrific Josses amid a hurricane of shells
to Tomsk, dispute the passage of the Yenesi
and heavily repulse him on the banks of the
Lena. Still maintaining an unbroken front
before the decimated Prussian Guard, the
Bear will entice him on to Vladivostoek
Then it will have him where it wanted him
The Slavonic hordes will pour themselves
across Canada and the United States, take
ship at N. Yark, and suddenly reinforce the
armies of Joffre and French. Just as spring
is beginning to clothe the landscape in a
mantle of green they will, with a mighty
shout, leap on the enemy from the rear and
sideways and settle the situation once and
for all. And then we shall know for certain
that the daily papers were right all along
when they gave us darkly to understand that
the Russians’ retreat was a deep laid scheme.
The Maoris continue to enlist to fill the
broken ranks of their countrymen at Galli-
poli. Most of the big North Island tribes
have been sending in their quotas of young
men steadily, and these are being trained
near Auckland. But there are some parts of
the Island where the feeling against the
pakeha still rankles, and several tribes have
thusfar stood out of the recruiting, though
most of them are said to be-wavering now.
The next lot to go to the Front will leave
from Wellington, and it has been arranged
that before they sail they are to be shut up
in the local Town Hall with their most
famous chiefs, who will tell the youngsters
a few things that it will be good for them
who go to Egypt to know.
When the bottom dropped out of the Tas-
mania gold mine at Beaconsfield (Tas.)
one of the things left behind was a line set
of brick and concrete buildings which cost
over £20,000 and could be bought now for
£2OOO. The State Government is turning
over the idea of changing them into a
munitions factory in the hope that if it
starts one and runs it during the war the
Federal Government will take it over when
the row is only a bad memory ana a long
bill. There are forges, steam hammers and
other odds and ends on the premises, and
floor space for over 100 lathes, while there
are enough empty houses in Beaconsfield to
shelter a few thousand munition workers.
Away back in the ’7o’s an attempt was
made to work the mountains of iron ore
near Beaconsfield, but the stuff didn t
answer with the methods used in those
days, though the Bessemer process might
possibly make first-class metal out of it.
A PENINSULAR SOLILOQUY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sea is bosker blue, an’ far below
I watch the strings of boats that come and go
01-f to the Red Cross ships. Far over'ead
A blasted ’plane is cracklin’, an’ some Red
Cross blokes lug past a cove ’arc! ’it or dead.
The evenin’ sun shines soft upon the scene ;
Far ’orf a sniper planted in the green
Thick bushes cracks ’is evenin’ ’ymn ;
A major torf (1 used to work for ’im)
In ragged pants goes mooehin’ fer ’is swim.
I lies and Watch the shrapnels bust along
The veller ’ill, and ’alken to the song
The twelve-inch boozers sing up oil the height—
The sort the blokes ’as christened Turk's Delight.
I'd just as soon be me as them to-night.
Down goes the sun be’ind the bloomin’ isles.
The shells flash bright, and then the creepin’ files
Of mules come crawlin’ up from orf the beach
(Which ’art gets outed ere the top they reach).
Oh, ’Oly Wars, a plugged mule is a peach !
Sometimes I think of ’omo and ’ef and all
The things so far behind, but mostly fall
To thinkin’ barmy-like about me food,
Or screwin’ down to see me boots is good,
Or cursin’ two-up coz me brass is blued.
’Ow we got ’ere, it’s just a looney dream,
’Art dark, orl wet —-the beach ; the ’on-id scream
Of blokes ’arcl ’it, and ’amis that grab and slide
Orf boats, and sink ; things driftin’ with the tide ;
Then one mad, ’owling rush right up the side.
'Struth! It was 'ot! Me cobber got ’is lot
Fail- in the neck. Old Mick and our ’cad pot
Gets outed at the jump. Some Turkey cow
Grabs at me legs. I trips ; but, any’ow,
I give ’im nothin’! He ain't worryin’ now.
Next thing I knows me ’ands is wet wif gore.
We’re diggin’ in like ’Ell. Oh, strike ! The roar
And whiz and groans. But, 'ere, I’m goin’ to
quit—-
Fergit it all. We’re ’ere, an’ ’ere we sit.
But, take it straight, Australia done ’er bit.

•‘Clnulah” : There was a Festival at Mel-
bourne Exhibition last week, to help build a
new synagogue —at Carlton. The Jewry is
divided in opinion about this. Hebrews love
to coneentrate, even more than R.C.’s; hence
there is usually resistance to any new syna-
gogue. The first in Melbourne was built in
Bourke-street west, and is now very much
(»ut of the way. The second resulted from a
split among Melbourne Jews, engineered by
Rabbi Itintel, and was the cause of much
bitterness. The division was between the
English and foreign Hebrews. Itintel suc-
ceeded in establishing the fine East Mel-
bourne Synagogue, now under Lenzer, a
Russian, who arrived as singer. I have been
told that he was an operatic basso. He had
to learn English here. There is perfect har-
mony nowadays between West and East;
also with St. Kilda Synagogue, under Dang-
low. The Carlton tabernacle, yet in a small
way, has for its pastor Chodowski, who came
hither from Maoriland.

“I 1 .II.” : lie was a very large, heavy per-
son, with a pair of hands like hams. The
bunch was discussing the war-census and
alternative occupations. The heavy one ex-
plained, in response to a suggestion, that lie
couldn't dig—hadn’t been used to it. Didn’t
know that he could do anything else, either.
“Well,” said somebody, “they’ll give you a
shovel, all right.” Somebody else mentioned
cooking as a suitable job for a beavv citizen
who couldn’t dig. The large man’s eyes
lighted up, and he licked his chops slowly.
“M-yes,” he. murmured, “I hadn’t thought; of
that. Cooking, of course.” And “cook” it
was, on the card.

In the course of an address to a country
branch of lied Cross workers in Victoria,
Dr. Edith Barrett stressed the necessity for
greater effort and cited a cable from Adrian
Knox, in Egypt, stating that “owing to the
heavy demand from Lemnos all stocks were
exhausted.” She said she had replied to
this that a further 2000 cases had been
despatched. The local paper broke the
news next day : “Heavy demand for lemons ;
all stocks exhausted ; 2000 cases sent”—and
tlm Red Cross people sank beneath the
avalanche of fruit which rolled in for days
alter. When at last they fought their way
out they set to and loaded a waggon for the
Melbourne military hospitals.

About 978 inventors have lately taken out
patents for life-protecting devices to be used
by soldiers. These range from things that
are picks and shovels by day and handy
covers for the heart by night, to others that
are egg-boilers in the morning, shaving sets
at lunch and skull-protectors in the even-
ing. One man has devised a combined blot-
ting-pad and armored shirt. But if the sur-
geons’ reports are worth anything all this
vast amount of inventive genius had been
better employed in designing an instrument
for recovery of the national p.n. The mod-
ern bullet is the most irresponsible, capri-
cious affair on earth. The old bullet just
went in “plunk,” and generally stayed there
until the recipient died or a surgeon fished it
nut and filled lip the orifice with a strip of
blanket. But the long, thin, nickel-plated
affair that comes Whining along nowadays
like a locust behaves in the manner of an
intoxicated riddle. When it wants to take
a man in the heart it enters by the left toe,
skirts the ankle, and taking a running jump
from the calf to the thigh, avoids the knee,
then climbs up the femoral artery, nips
over the appendice, and grabbing the dia-
phragm leaps nimbly into the left ventricle.
Or it gets in on the elbow, comes out seek-
ing re-entry through the fifth rib. and bolts
away into vacancy from the left ear, with-
out doing discoverable injury. In these
cases the soldier is generally given a day off
to play cricket. There is a man hack in
Sydney with a bullet-hole in him but no
bullet-hole out of him. yet no bullet can be
found in him ; another lmd a bullet from east
to west across where his bully-beef went to.
Therefore, in view of the undisciplined char-
acter of the modern bullet, it seems as if the
best protection against it is to get hold of the
other fellow l>y the slack of his pants and
rub his nose in the mud before he has time
to fire nis gun.

“Carfax”: Patriotism demands that the
Into door should he opened wider. I de-
nounce the wowser as a German spy
and the early closer as a Menace to the
Nation. Look over the daily papers for a
few weeks back, and find dozens of Hun
sympathisers dealt with for reckless
blatherskite by indignant
beaks, and every Hun of
the hit was blithered at
the time he opened his
head. Therefore in the
interests of the Empire,
and in order that all its
enemies may be discovered
and nabbed, let us, I say.
have an Australia Week
—seven days of foaming
pots ; days on which arch-
bishops and politicians
and Dago fruiterers and
State Governors and
importers and loyal
Shermans and all the
rest would twine vine
leaves round their hats
and argue the point. A
week like this would be
a proper climax to the
war census. The one is
a record of men and
wealth and household fur-
niture; the other would
be a census of ideas and
craft and exposure of
traitors. There is no-
thing like beer for mak-
ing people talk, and the
anxiety to close pubs and
thus assist the enemy to
keei) his mouth shut is
un-Britisli. Besides, think
of the fines ; at £SO a time
the spoiling of the Hun
would be magnificent!
Tamworth (N.S.W.) has had a resurrec-
tion; and the act is so unpopular with the
police that if the Prophet Zechariah was to
apise f rom the dust he would be chased over
the landscape by P.C. ,749. On July 18 one
Wilmott disappeared from the town. The
same day a corpse was arrested in the river,
accused of being YVilmott, and found guilty
by the Coroner. Then it was put into its
wooden overcoat, labelled “Wilmott,” and
planted. 011 September 18 Wilmott turned
up again in Tamworth and had the news
broken to him that he was dead and safelv
under ground. He said it was the first he’d
heard of it, and hinted that he’d never been
dead in his life as far as he knew. Anyhow
he could swear he wasn’t dead during July
That has put the police to the necessitv of
looking for the owner of a cadaver. Anv-
body lost one?

The day was warm, for Spring was here.
He drifted by the shining sea.
Upon a little hillock near
She sift in sweet simplicity.
He chanced an eye,
She made reply—
’Twas ocular, and swift and shy.
The lone youth sat upon the sand,
And idly gazed along the beach,
And presently he pressed her hand —-
She'd left it just within his reach.
The buxom miss
Permitted this,
So Ernest ventured on a kiss.
She turned on him in fierce disdain,
And with a baton hard and trim
She smote him thrice and once again,
Then smartly she arrested him.
Don Juan, stop;
Canoodlings drop—
Your mash may be a Jennie Hoi)!

RETIRED WORLD’S CHAMPION ALL-
ROUND ATHLETE,
praises Ilean’s Essence, the great, money-
saving remedy for Coughs. Colds and Throat
Troubles. He recently wrote : “I had trouble
with my throat of a most painful and chok-
ing kind. It was so bad at times that I
could hardly articulate, and I could not
speak above a whisper. I found a friend in
Mr. Allen Doone, who persuaded me to try
a course of Mean’s Essence. The effect was
almost incredible. Within three days all the
trouble was cleared away. I can also certify,
through having witnessed its speedy effect in
several instances, that Mean’s Essence is
a boon and a blessing in cases of
colds and sore throats among chil-
dren of tender years. In my opinion no home
should be without this economical means for
the preparation of an excellent family cough
mixture. Head’s Essence is such a wonder-
fully good remedy that it should be kept in
every home. It is so economical that a bottle
costing 2s. goes as far as 12s. worth of ordi-
nary cough mixtures. If you cannot obtain
Head’s Essence from your chemist or store-
keeper send 2s. (adding 3d. for postage),
and you will receive a bottle per return mail
from the manufacturer, G. W. Mean,
chemist, ITS Castlereagh Street. Sydney; or
Mean’s Pharmacy, Wanganui, New Zealand.*
PERSONAL
ITEMS
HANNAN, M.H.R. the
elect of Fawkner (Vic.).
A man who took a hand in the destruc-
tion of the Emden went under recently at
the Dardanelles. This was Private W. C.
Falconer, of the first Maoriland expedition-
ary force. He was a telegraphist in the
Eltliam post office, and much interested in
wireless. Aboard the transport he was set
to handle the spark, and while on “listening”
duty had plenty of opportunities to experi-
ment with a detector of his own contrivance.
It was while using this device that he picked
up the hurried call sent out by Cocos Island.
Of all the wireless “listeners” in the big-
fleet he was the only man who caught the
cry. and he probably wouldn’t have got it
with anything but his own receiver.
I’asseil out the other day, near Pitt Town
(N.S.W.), “Tommy” Thompson. “Tommy”
started as a mail carrier when he was about
nine, and stuck to it for just on 70 years.
But general elections were his specialty.
Dead and gone William Morgan never had
a more strenuous supporter. He helped
Morgan to beat Sydney Burdekin for the
Hawkesbury, though Burdekin out of Par-
liament was worth a dozen Morgans in it
when it came to log-rolling and wire-pulling.
At last Hall beat Morgan, and lie has kept
the seat for 15 or 16 years, though he has
scarcely spoken in the House all that time.
In one respect Hall is like George Reid—
he can sleep anywhere and in any position.
A large ability to go to sleep counts for a
lot in old Windsor and other Hawkesbury
townships.
“THE NURSES ARE RUTTING IN
SPLENDID WORK.”
(From the Other Girls’ Point of View.)
Alfred La Nauze, who died lately at Arma-
dale (Vic.), aged 73, was for many years
chief magistrate in Mauritius. One of his
sons, Charles, was killed at the Dardanelles
iu June; another, George, is that member of
the Eastern Telegraph Co.’s staff who sent
from Cocos the wireless message that brought
the Sydney along to meet the Emden.
Ex-Justice Simpson, just dead, had his
political ambitions in the days of long ago,
and started out once after a seat in the
N. S. Vales Assembly. But he fell off his
horse and went to bed in a hospital instead.
He never tried again, but put in several
years in the Council.
Estell, of Mines and Labor, is the present
sick man of the N.S.W. Ministry. A facial
trouble which called for the knife a year or
two ago became troublesome again lately.
The operation has been successful, but the
little man has been allowing his politics to
worry him far too much.
The row in the Tasmanian Public Health
Department is likely to send a billet begging
at a time when doctors are scarce. The
Board appointed to hold the' usual inquiry
has not yet reported, but according to his
friends McClintock has had enough, any-
how. Elkington, now in Queensland, the
original occupant of the post of Chief Health
Officer, was the only one who found the job
tolerable. But, in his way, Elkington was
an artist. If he wanted to shake up a local
board of health he first got a beautiful set
of photographs, taken by his unobtrusive
lieutenant Wadsworth, depicting the pigs
browsing about the chairman’s slaughter-
house, with the cesspit close handy. “If you
don’t, I’ll expose you,” was the formula for
Ids best prescription. Elkington worked on
the principles of painless dentistry.
Jolin Deason, just dead at Moliaaul
(Vic.), aged 85, was the discoverer, with
Richard Oates, of the famous “Welcome
Stranger” nugget, near Dunolly, in 1569.
This little speck weighed in the rough
2801 b., and Was worth £9555.
C. .T. Valentine, dead in Adelaide at SO.
was the type of man who can do just one
thing and does that perfectly. lie came
from England in the old gold-digging days,
but took to the pastoral life almost at once,
and iu 1865 became Chief Inspector of Stock,
during the great drought of that era. In
that position he stayed till retired under the
age-limit 10 years ago. Valentine was a
mine of information on all points connected
with stations or stock, and transparently
honest in everything.
Passed over in his 90th year,
Nicholas Maine, one of the most
energetic of Melbourne’s early
citizens. His days on earth
included time spent in a mer-
chant’s office in Belfast (Ire-
land). in voyages to Brazil as
supercargo, in running a Pata-
gonian guano fleet, in adven-
tures in various South Ameri-
can republics, and in opening
up trade between Brazil and
Russia. After that he was a
stockbroker in Liverpool; then
he went to California : and next,
after a spell in Mexico and
Nicaragua, he naturally rushed
to the Victorian diggings. More
or less settling down, he tried
station life in Queensland, but
not liking it returned to Mel-
bourne, and there applied for
the local secretaryship of the
A.M.P. Society, which he se-
cured in 1868. Hardly any-
body thought the rolling stone
would fit; but it did, and he
continued in the billet for 28
years, his only holiday being-
one trip to England in the early
’9()’s. He married a daughter
of Andrew McCrae. an old-time
warden of flic Victorian gold-
fields.
Alban Gee, manager of the
one co. in Australia which does
best for its shareholders when it
doesn’t make any profits, shifted
into Sydney the other day from
Auburn; and, as he had made
Auburn, the neighborhood fed
him and obliged him to talk.
Gee was only 19 when he was
brought in with the late Wm.
Cordingley by the Australian
Meat Co. That was in 1866.
Gee was the meat packer;
Cordingley the chemist. The
thing was a failure; and, after
a while the youngster Gee set to
work to establish the Sydney
Meat Preserving Co., on a really
brilliant basis, and he has run
it for 44 years—run it so suc-
cessfully that it has never paid a dividend
and hopes it never will. The anomaly is
explained by the fact that it is owned by
squatters, and buys only when prices are
weak. It is always in the market, and its
bidding makes the other purchasers bid
higher. So it goes on bidding till there is
no profit in the purchase; and that is why
it succeeds by always failing to pay a divi-
dend.
It is claimed by her relatives that Mrs.
Letitia Woodward, who died at Toorak
( Vic.) last week, was the oldest living Aus-
tralian-born white woman. She was a
daughter of Colonel Chas. Wall, who arrived
with his regiment (3rd Buffs) at Sydney in
the early ’twenties. The daughter was
born in the Wynyanl Square Barracks on
September 4, 1824, which would make her
91.
One of the two surviving explorers of the
Central Australian “Desert,” W. 11. Tietkens,
lies ill in the Eastwood Cottage Hospital
(N.S.W.). The only other living explorer of
his time is Bigjohn Forrest. Tietkens was
the second in command of Ernest Giles’s
last two trips through the West —the first in
1873, the second a few years later.
There was one less survivor of the old-
time Indian Mutiny when James Bell
answered the last roll-call at Hobart the
other day. He saw some hot times as a
private in the Seaforth Highlanders, and at
the end pleaded guilty to 78.
John McCall, long the Agent-General for
Tasmania, has joined the A.A.M.C. The
foundations of Major McCall’s career were
laid in the fertile Tasmanian nor’-west,
where the farmer usually has enough money
to pay the doctor, and, if the matter were
left to him, would always keep a Conserva-
tive representative in Parliament. The
Agent-Generalship with the tag of knight-
hood seemed to mark the close of McCall’s
public existence, but the war has given him
a chance to do something worth while in
another direction.

The Bulletin grieves for another bright
young contributor lost to the 'Vink. C. R.
L. Ilalloran, who went off with the Light
Horse from Sydney, was wounded on Aug-
ust 14, and died apparently about three
weeks later. Before leaving he got his
bread as a linesman on a Sydney paper,
where he put up a good fight to secure better
treatment for the semi-detached staff-men
who w r ere on the same mark ; and he but-
tered the bread with occasional Bulletin
verse and more frequent prose. After
enlisting lie kept up the family reputation
of the Hallorans by turning out for the Sun
much descriptive stuff which often was a
good deal like literature.
“Effosee”: Somebody (B. 12/8/T5) asked
if there was an equal to the late Arthur
Rosenthal's record of the gold, silver and
bronze medals of the Iloyal Humane
Society. A r es ; Jim Knowles, of Pori
Aburiri (ALL.), has all of them, with extra
bars for the gold and silver, and five certi-
ficates. He is just back from Gallipoli with
four endorsements of his bravery—bullet-
wounds he received in those first wild days
with the pioneer M.L. contingent in the
Turks’ peninsula.

BURNS. M.H.R. of
Hbrcarra.
Thomas Hulse, who shut oft steam at
Albert Park (Vic.) the other day. arrived
in Victoria in ’GO, and was employed as
engine-driver by the Victorian Government
when there were only two railway-engines
iu the State. When the Bendigo line was
opened Hulse was the driver of the famous
express. From the footplate he went to be
chief loco, foreman at the North Melbourne
depot, and stayed there until his retirement
20 years ago. He lived to be 81.
REHABILITATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Professor Stephen Langdon, M.A., Tt.IL, Ph.D., of
Oxford University, has just translated a Sumerian
tablet showing that Noah and not Adam was ap-
pointed Chief Gardener in Eden, and ate of the for-
bidden fruit. —London Times.
Again, again! The hand iconoclastic
Beats down an ancient record in the dust,
And scoffers gibe iu bitter tone sarcastic,
And ask what ancient tale can mortals trust.
This time the fame is cleared of Father Adam
Who lived in Eden with his charming madam.
llow long- has he been blamed! But lo ! to-day
No stain is left on Adam’s reputation,
For fresh discoveries clear the blot away,
And show, in clearest light of revelation,
That neither he nor Eve, our primal mother,
Transgressed about that fruit, or any other.
The Villain in the piece, we learn at last,
Was that old vinous sailor, Father Noah —-
The same Who built an Ark and tethered fast
Within its timbers crocodile and boa ;
It seems that, not content with shameless tippling,
And so corrupting- Ilam, a gentle stripling,
Before the Ark was built or set afloat
To save our race for annals dark and sinful
(From quaint Sumerian tablets do 1 quote),
That mariner consumed a tidy skinful
Of fruits forbid in Eden’s blooming garden—
A sin for which he never craved a pardon ;
But cast the blame (the cunning, crafty knave!)
To rest for aye on Adam's guiltless shoulders,
While he, the culprit, rested in the grave
Along- with other early racial moulders,
And left the grand old Gardener for a guerdon
The carrying of the Skipper's trespass-burden.
And as to Satan —grossly slandered, too—
lias he been wronged about the apple-stealing?
Is it a fact, to give the De’il his due,
He nourished no ungentlemanly feeling,
And, though his antecedents were but shady,
Took no advantage of an honest lady?
A plague upon that low-down Hebrew scribe!
lie must have been a scurvy sort of vandal
Who first traduced with solemn sneer and gibe
That primal pair, and blackened them with scandal.
Perchance he, too, for love of pure variety,
Invented Noah’s lapse in inebriety!
lie, too, no doubt, or someone of his breed,
Begat the yarn how Jonah went a-whaling,
And told in fine imaginative screed
How she-bears ate the children for their railing;
lie was the fountain-head of Olden Mystery,
And wrote the bulk of all “authentic” history.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

The disclosed profits are insufficient to pay
the dividend; and that is a most unusual
position for the Union to be in. Judging
by the general policy of the management
one can feel quite sure that the deficit was
only produced by extraordinary demands
upon the ordinary profits. A very comfort-
ing remark in the chairman’s speech pro-
bably explains it, or part of it: —
You will not be surprised that as regards our in-
vestments I have again to announce a considerable
reduction in values. At the end of the half-year
there was a comparatively small depreciation/-on the
values at which they stood in our books, and this
was provided for from funds already set aside for
the purpose. On a valuation taken recently
the meeting was held at the end of July,
five months after the accounts closed —
further depreciation was disclosed, but this has also
been fully provided for.
There has been still further depreciation
since then, and values may go still lower as
money gets dearer; and presumably these
shrinkages, too, will be provided for,
notwithstanding that by and bye a
recovery is just about as certain as
anything can be. The Union hetsf,
however, such large internal reserves that
unquestionably much further writing down
can be done without any call on the dis-
closed reserves. For instance, the bank
premises in Australia and Maoriland, worth
certainly well on to a million, stand in the
books at only £384,600. If these were
written up to their true value at least half
a million would be available for writing
down investments without touching the pre-
sent visible reserves. Still it is pretty cer-
tain that things will , have to go a lot worse
with the Allies before any such step as that
is taken, if the other internal reserves are
anything like what people who ought to
know believe them to be. A
And. after all. what the bank calls its
investments are only a comparatively small
part of its assets. Its total figures are made
up this way :
Coin and short-call money . . £6.264,953
Investments (public securities) 2,507,759
Drafts in transit, etc... .. 3,074,614
"Advances . . . . . . 17,440,135
Premises .. .. .. 500,330
Total .. ..£29,787,791
So the “investments” amount to only about
8 p.c. of the total assets. The coin and
drafts, etc., represent another 31 p.c. The
Advances are practically all in Australia ana
Maoriland ; and these depend for their prop-
erty values mainly upon the wool clip and
the harvest. The borrowing boom has no doubt
given city property in particular a fictitious
value, and a good deal of that will be lost.
But there are not many Australian banks
which have not been fully aware of the
frothy conditions that have prevailed, and
they have asked for margins accordingly.
As The Bulletin has pointed out before,
there was a vast difference between the
Australian banking position as it was in the
early ’nineties, when trouble happened, and
as it was when the maniac of Potsdam set a
lighted match to the world. In the ’nineties
the Australian banks were overloaded with
British money, deposited by fidgety people
who didn’t know that one drought doesn’t
mean national ruin, and who would have
said that you were, taking liberties with
their leg if you had told them that a drought
was to a great extent a species of beneficial
fallowing. Australia had their money and
they wanted it back, and that was all about
it. They wouldn’t wait for the extra-fat
harvest that followed the fallowing. In a
good many cases they accepted a lot less
than they were entitled to; and that was
all to the good of Australia. For when it
did recover Australia owned millions which
it would otherwise have had to pay away ;
and it is those millions and others which
have been banking up in Australian insti-
tutions. Ilence, when the maniac committed
his crime, and the world was ablaze, the
banks were stocked with Australian money ;
and of course it was left where it was —
what could Australia Jo ivith it if it had
been stupid enough to withdraw its deposits?
There has consequently been no need to send
out urgent head-office memos to managers
to call in overdrafts; there havA been no
forced realisations, no flinging of properties
on the market to bring what they could.
Hundreds of times Australia has been asked
to groan over the flight of foreign capital.
Foreign capital isn’t an unmixed blessing
no matter liow it is invested ; but invested
in bank deposits it is a curse so far as Aus-
tralia is concerned; and our salvation now
is that we had practically none of it. For
that reason the Union’s deposits were actu-
ally larger at the end of February than they
were when the war broke out; and the
chances are they will go on steadily grow-
ing as we ship our wool and our wheat and
our butter and our metals and our meat, and
turn them into money.

The Bulletin .won’t swear positively to all
the figures ifTcite last two lines. The amount
of tlie dividends is, for example, a matter of
computation. ’Still they oughtn’t to be far
out. The co., having put itself on a sound
basis and seeing excellent prospects, decided
early in 1914 to reconstruct itself, primarily
with a view to an increase of capital. At
that time there were 22,500 8 p.c. fixed
cumulative A pref. shares, 30,000 8 p.c. fixed
cumulative B pref. shares, and 22,500 ordi-
naries. There was at that time no good-
will. On the reconstruction, as dividends
in excess, of 10 p.c. are liable to provoke
envy, hatred and uncharitableness, the
ordinaries got two new for each one old
share. That made 45,000 ordinaries issued
to old shareholders ; and 52,500 were offered
for subscription at par. All have been
taken, but as at June 30 there were 32,195
oil which ss. was still unpaid. The position
at that date was that there was a paid
capital of £141,951, of which, however,
£22.500 was represented by goodwill; but
at the same date, after providing for current
dividends, there were accumulated profits to
take the place of all but £1747 of this paper
asset. Of this accumulated profit £13.132
was taken over from the old co. in accord-
ance with the terms of the agreement.
It is a fine start; and the profit for the
14 months is very fine too. As it stands in
the table it looks in the top class; and
actually it was perhaps better than it looks,
because the -expenses included the whole of
the cost of the reconstruction. Also depre-
ciation appears to have been very liberally
provided for. Thus the cost to the new co.
of plant and rolling stock was £42.508, and
already £5565 of it has been written off.
Of the new money got in during the term
about £15,000 has gone into stock, which is
how valued at £74,591; and of course it is
not possible to any check on that —it
may be or it may be overvalued,
and profits may be affected accordingly. But
you ca’n generally- form some idea of a co.'s
policy in this very important matter of
stocktaking; and one would say pretty posi-
tively that the chances are all in favor of a
conservative valuation here. In spite of the
increase of stock and of factory freehold
and book debts (£49,716) the co. is in a
fairly easy financial position. Besides stock
and book debts, which are semi-liquid, it,had
on June 30 about £7OOO of cash and imme-
diately convertible assets: while its liabili-
ties of all kinds totalled £46.070.

But that needs explanation. When E. 11.
Lascelles, the patriarch of the woolbroking
business of Western Victoria, invited his
clients and friends to come in, he guaranteed
a 7 p.c. dividend for five years, the surplus
profits of one year to go towards repaying
him any money that he might have to hand
over on account of this guarantee in any
other year. The first year he had to put his
hand in his pocket; and this money was
refunded in 1914. In the ■ past year—■
probably the worst the Western district ever
had—he has to dip still deeper. Conse-
quently the deficit (that is, the amount of
capital represented by goodwill and flota-
tion exes.) is less than the figures shown in
the table. But it seems just as well to let
it stand as it appears above, because in
subsequent years, unless things go utterly
to pieces, the co. will have to refund the
money now paid (or advanced, if you like)
by the vendor. On the other hand, there
may be a trifle to add to goodwill on account
of an item, “Shares motor business, £5000,”
which stands as an asset. It is highly pro-
bable that some of that is not represented
by tangible assets. This motor business, by
the way, has not been a success during the
last couple of- years, and an antici-
pated loss of £l4OB has been oro-
vided for by this co. Fortunately 1914
was a fair year, so that, in spite of the
drought and the motor-business loss, the
vendor is now tapped for only £ll6l. How-
ever, though disappointed, he probably isn’t
regarding it as gone for ever. During the
six years preceding the birth of the co. the
average annual profit was certified to have
been £13,198: and even if the next two
years are £2500 worse than the average, his
money will come back to him.
During the five years no dividend in
excess of 7 p.c. may be paid: and anv
surplus has to go towards a reserve fund.
Such a fund is certainly very desirable : for,
in addition to the capital which its own
shareholders have risked in the business,
they have £115.0X5 of other people's, most
of it bank overdraft. On the other side there
are. £10(5,477 of sundry debtors —no doubt
largely made up of advances —and it is a
good thing to have a fund apart from capital
to provide for contingencies in a business
of that size and character. On the six
years’ history prior to the dotation of the
co. a vendor wasn’t likely to part with the
business except at a substantial figure; but
even with an average profit of £13,198 there
wasn’t a margin large enough to provide for
a sufficiently-rapid reduction of. so large a
goodwill as- that which actually figured in
the prospectus. Ilad there been no guar-
antee it certainly would have been thought
necessary by this time to have accumulated
£BOOO or £10.000; and that would not have
allowed of an average dividend of any more
than 5 p.c. p.a. on the inflated capital of
£150,000. Things may, of course, change
completely; but on present appearances it
seems highly probably that on the expiry of
the five-year guarantee period the dividend
will have to come down to the figure just,
mentioned —assuming that the business is
f|p a’naged with due caution. Even for the
present the directors, or some of them, doubt
the wisdom of actually distributing a 7 p.c.
dividend, even though the vendor guarantees
it; and a proposal was afoot (what hap-
pened to it The Bulletin doesn’t know)
that of last year’s dividend 2 p.c. (=£3000)
should be deferred.

This extraordinarily successful concern must
feel the coming pinch more than most. At
present it is being maintained in its afflu-
ence by the tail-end of the borrowing boom ;
and when that disastrous beast goes round
the corner, it will rub a good deal of the
gilt off this co.’s profits. Already the output
has suffered considerably: during the latter
half of 1914 the sales amounted to £29,661,
while in the past six months they were only
£25,452. Thus far, however, there has been
no great slump in the building trade in
Sydney, partly because of the extensive
works in progress, when the war started, and
partly because advantage has been taken of
the better value now obtainable to carry out
jobs which had been deferred on account of
previous high costs. But “To Let” boards
are beginning to dot the suburbs ; and that
means an early slackening off of speculative
building. While the boom has lasted this
co. has had a great time. Not only has it
been paying 20 p.c. of late on watered cap-
ital. but it has been laying by a nice bit.
Look back over five years:—
June, ’3 0. June, ’l5.
Liquid assets .. .. £6539 £21,730
Liabilities . . . 547 838
Surplus assets .. £5992 £20,872
So the co.. besides paying its very fine divi-
dend, has laid by £14,880 in five years: and
the capital five years ago was only £40,800.
In addition it has bought more quarry land
at. Guildford and has carried out consider-
able improvements at Emu Plains. Mean-
time, no doubt, there has been some depre-
ciation ; hut it is probable that the improve-
ments more than counterbalance the wast-
age. Nor will the bottom altogether drop
out of the business when the bottom drops
out of the borrowing and building boom:
there will still bo moderate pickings to be
got out of the supply of materials for
patches and repairs.

Three things have told against the company
during the year—drought, war, and the
decreased output from Broken Hill mines,
due of course to the war. Oversea shipping
lias to some extent revived, and so has
Broken Hill, but S. Aus. business is still
slack, and will be until after next harvest.
However, looking back over the years, the
co. has nothing to complain of. Take a
seven years’ comparison :
June,’oB. June,’ls.

Liabilities .. ... .. .. 2,087 1,636
Surplus .. .. £29,934 £14,107
S > in those seven years the co. has cut
£15.827 but of its mostly-liquid assets; but
during Hie same period it has provided
itself with a steam lighter (for the Broken
T T ’ 11 flux business), which cost, ahni.it.
£17.000. and lately it had a tug built in
Britain at a cost of about £15,000. Maybe
the* other plant isn’t worth quite as much as
it was seven years ago; one or two hulks
see-in to have got beyond service even as
hulks. But the shrinkage cannot amount to
much. At any rate, two vessels which seem
to have cost over £30,000 have been pro-
vided in seven years, and approximately half
of the cost has come out of earnings which
have been left over after paying the dividend.
As for the new tug, by the way, at latest
the Admiralty had collared it; so the c-o.
will most likely have to get another one
built. It ought of course to be produced
in Australia; but the dreadful experience of
the Commonwealth in connection with the
building of the Brisbane is enough to make
any co. think hard. And it is enough to
make any barracker for Australian industry
curse hard.

No, don’t envy these people their 15 p.c.
Instead, look at the two or three lines which
introduce the table, and note that the capital
was written down, as an average, from £5
to £l, so that a 15 p.c. dividend is really
one of 3 p.c. As to some part of the capital
it is a lot worse even than that. The prefs.
of course suffered least: hence they get
only 5 p.c., while the rest get the bigger
amount. The ordinary capital was cut down
so horribly that there was little more than
a smell of it left; but it was agreed that, if
the old assets should turn out to be worth
more than their valuation, the surplus should
go to a special reserve for the benefit of
these ordinary and of deferred shares. As
a matter of fact, much better values have
been got for most of the old assets than was
expected ; so the special reserve has grown
like this :—-

Roughly, in 10 years properties of a book
value of £270,000 have been sold for some-
thing like £360,000; and this money lias
been used to pay £lOO,OOO off deposits, to
make £145,000 of advances and add £117,000
to the liquid assets. To the old depositors
£155,000 was still owing on June 30 (it
was included among fixed deposits), and
this lot was payable a few days ago. Hence,
no doubt, the accumulation of liquid assets
during the last year or two. The with-
drawal of this fixed deposit money will leave
the co. with very little besides its ow r n
capital to work on. Yet, if it wants
deposits, there is no reason whatever why it
shouldn’t get them—if people have the
money to lend. The security, at any rate,
is undeniable. The co. has over £300.000 of
its own funds, and with this £155.000 repaid
it won’t have so much as £150,000 of other
people’s money in the business. Profits must
remain small while it has so little outside
money to work with. On the other hand,
the shareholder has the consolation of know-
ing that, if he isn’t making much profit, he
at least hasn’t been taking much risk ; and
there has been a good deal of risk about
advances made on the boom values of the
few years which preceded the war.

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supplv Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cat,tie dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.

Sydney', Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.*'

Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per" cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making- a total cost of 4s. 10d. per cub
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to O’s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is •
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. 10d.— a saving of is rid
per cub. yard. This result was obtained‘by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2
