ROYDS, LTD., Investors’ Advisers, Challis House, Sydney
it they didn’t close up, H. R. Denison, the
chairman of the co. which held the Tele-
funken agency, fixed up an amalgamation
with the Marconi principals in London;
and the table shows the history since the
new concern took things over as from Dec. 1.
1912. Up to that time it is doubtful
whether either concern had made a profit.
For the first six or eight months after the
amalgamation the two organisations had to
be more or less maintained; nevertheless,
competition being past, a loss was now easily
dodged, and a dividend of 4 p.c. on the capi-
tal, equal to about 21 p.c. p.a. for the 19
months, was declared. Things were then
shaping very well ; but in the past six months
profit from the trading account —apparently
in connection with installations —has almost
disappeared, owing, of course, to the sup-
pression of private apparatus. Gross profit
from this branch in the previous half-year
was £6937; in the past six months only £993.
Also traffic revenue —derived from messages
between ships’ passengers and shore-—has
been more or less cut into by various restric-
tions. So the ordinary profit for the term
was only £1673. To that has been added in
the table £2935, received net from the Com-
monwealth Government in an action brought
by the Marconi people. This amount lias
been used to write down patents, etc.
And that brings up the question of re-
serves. When the co. w r as formed £90,000
of the capital was supposed to be represented
by patent rights, licenses and exclusive agen-
cies. Strictly, perhaps, the table ought to
start off showing that amount as a deficit: it
certainly isn’t represented by anything
tangible. At any rate, it must be clearly
understood that the patent rights, like a
lease, are a wasting asset, and that the true
profits can only be shown by writing off each
term something towards extinguishing this
fugitive asset. In the first period £3OOO was
written off. Since then no part of the ordin-
ary revenue has been put aside; wherefore
neither tin* £7SI2 of the one nor the £1673
of the other half-year is the true profit. If
£5OOO in hard-cash profit were put by every
year it would be IS years before the capital
now represented by paper was represented
by money; and if £5OOO were written off the
ordinary profits of last year there wouldn’t
be a great deal left. True, as mentioned
before, the past year was a time of very
exceptional difficulties; nevertheless, this
need to cut off large slices of revenue, in order
to make good the principal, must be kept in
mind. It will seriously affect the profit
even in the best of times.
* * * *
Shareholders in the Colonial Sugar got
a surprise when, opening a circular this
week, they read of a special meeting on the
29th to authorise the directors “to distribute
in specie amongst the members” the pref.
shares in the new co. which has taken over
the Maoriland and Fiji business. But a foot-
note says “the term ‘in specie’ used in the
resolution means ‘in shares,’ not ‘in cash,’ ”
and Heaven and the co.’s counsel only know
why the resolution didn’t say so. Share-
holders who want the new scrip put in
someone else’s name must give notice on or
before the 28th.
* * * *
The negotiations between the Tasmanian
Government and the Hobart Gas Co., for
the purchase by the former’s Hydro-Elec-
tric Department of the Gas Co.’s electrical
plant and undertakings (mentioned B.
9/9/T5), have advanced another stage.
With enthusiasm the Gas Co. shareholders
have ratified the provisional agreement, and
it only remains for the Government to get
the approval of Parliament. The price is
£lOB.OO0 —£8000 in cash and £lOO.OOO in
Treasury bills bearing interest at 5 p.c.-
and the date of the transfer is October 1.
How good the proposal looks to the Gas Co.
may be judged from the fact that a year ago
if was prepared to sell out its electrical
business to the Hobart Corporation for
£BO.OOO, but could not do a deal. Under the
influence of the Government offer the £lB
Gas Co. shares are quoted at £3O. and the
shareholders are suggesting that something
in the way of debentures or extra stock
should be passed out to them on the
strength of such a >srood stroke of business.
B. H. Junction North has been giving its
plant some exercise, and has increased its
stock of lead concentrates from 781 to 1300
tons. Surplus assets are said to be now
worth £120,000 against £99.262 in Septem-
ber, 1914. But most of this value is in
slimes, and so far it has proved a heap easier
to pay a big price for slime than to get any-
one to pay a big price for slime concentrates.
In 31- years New Burragorang (N.S.W.)
has produced 905 tons of silver ore averaging
£2l per ton. In a recent eight weeks 1221 tons
realised £2434, and values range now from
£l7 18s. 6d. to £22 per ton. The half-yearly
meeting in August ended with a comfortable
assurance that New Burragorang was out of
debt, and that there was a surplus of £2519,
of which £l2BO was cash. There is, how-
ever, £l5OO owing to the people who sold the
property to the present co., and this is to be
paid by deducting 25 p.c. from money set
aside for dividends. The directors are
anxious to push on with development, and to
improve ventilation No. 8 west is to be con-
nected with the Yerranderrie next door.
From a man who has been watching
developments from Melbourne :
Who got first cut into the Edna Deeps market
doesn’t matter. What is now certain is that the
Deeps is the surprise pack'et of Westonia next to
the big mine. After it had been turned down by
the Edna on a bore that went away west, mine-
surveyor Macartney had the pluck to shove it off on
to a co. with the testimonial that those who had
th“ grit to back it would find the lode coming into
the claim at about 440 ft. The sniffing over Macart-
ney’s prophecy still echoes in the Perth Club ; but
the fact remains' that in a bore from the Deeps
shaft at 250 ft., just over the Edna boundary, 26ft.
of quartz has been run through from 440 ft. down-
wards. The grade is reported 2oz. for the first
Bft. and loz. for the balance. After that comes
footwall country carrying stringers of quartz, such
footwall being reported in some quarters as granite.
Seemingly manager Stuckey was told that unbeliev-
ing Didymuses were questioning his core values.
He remarks that the doubters are liars, which isn’t
polite but is emphatic. He has “many indepen-
dent witnesses of values,’’ he says—a superfluous
remark, for his own word is good enough. The true
width of the quartz where bored is given as 21ft.
r lhe drill also confirms what the Edna’s own bores
disclosed—that the lode underlies very flat to the
north, and explains how it is that the No. 4
depressed bore put in from the Edna 300 ft. crosscut
to get the lode at 400 ft. furnished only negative
evidence.
Centrals got jumped on heavily because of the
Deeps strike, but the extinguisher is not on the
claim by a long way. The Deeps bore is so close to
the corner peg of the three mines that unless the
shoot makes west steadily the pitch will carry the
lode into the northern section of the Central, if it
does not cut out. Another bore or two on the fan
principle will settle that, if the Deeps directors have
cash and pluck enough to do the work.
Ivanhoe’s latest half-yearly estimate of ore
reserves shows an increase of 3 1,525 tons in
quantity and a decrease of Gd. per ton in
value compared with the figures of six months
ago. The total on the present guess is
1,030,243 tons, worth 365. 9d., and is made
up as follows:—East lode, 698,317 tons,
average 355. 9d. ; middle lode, 3 54,841 tons,
average 38s. Bdr; new lode, 133,886 tons,
average 85s. 2d.; Boulder lode, 23,399 tons,
average 655. lid. Allowing 255. per ton
for loss in residues and cost of production,
this gives a profit in sight of £8 per share,
or nearly a third more than the present
market value.
Another hitherto promising Westralian, the
Sand Queen, has become disabled at depth.
Very little payable ore has been exposed at
500 ft., and only traces are showing at the
No. 6 level. Inroads made by the mill on
the ore reserves have necessitated the stop-
page of half the mill and the sacking of most
of the men. The owners, Frank Sloss and
Co., are hopeful of the shoot righting itself
in deeper ground; but when they once go
wrong in that part of the West they rarely
recover. In an unpretentious way the mine
has produced 60,000-odd ounces of gold, and
paid £lOO,OOO in dividends.
Apparently the new find at Mount Hol-
land. 90 miles south of Southern Cross
(W.A.), is not altogether a duffer. Several
eosteens show the lodes to carry values over
a fair length, and the visible gold area is
expanding. The discovery of water, too,
has improved prospects.
* * * *
Ivookynie (W.A.) is reported to be com-
ing again. At its best it was a one-mine
town, and when that mine went to pieces it
shrivelled up. Now, as a result of a few
encouraging finds in neighboring shows, its
admirers reckon that a revival is imminent.
* * * *
“Oof Bird”: George I). Meudell, of Mel-
bourne, has written the Westralian Mines
Department, “cordially disagreeing” with its
geologist’s opinion regarding the non-exist-
ence of oil in that State. Incidentally the
enthusiast has a dig at every Australian
geologist bar one on this subject. He says : “I
cannot understand why they condemn the
idea that petroleum exists in the Common-
wealth. Australia holds everything else, so
why not oil? Yet the various State geolo-
gists except Twelvetrees, of Tasmania, scorn
the' idea of ever striking it. Why? Are
they paid to stop prospecting for oil, or to
encourage it and help the prospector offici-
ally?” Well, over here in the West they are
paid to protect the public, and so far they
have done their work uncommonly well.
Minister for Mines Collier (W.A.) asked
Parliament the other day to agree to the
appointment of check inspectors, and sub-
mitted the following casualty list in support
of his request:—393o : Fatal, 29 ; serious,
587. 1931 : Fatal, 37 ; serious, 528. 1912 :
Fatal, 35; serious, 491. 3933: Fatal, 26;
serious, 741. 1914: Fatal. 26; serious, 831.
For the first seven months of this year:
Fatal, 20; serious, 487. The proposal
is an old one, previously scorned by
the Leg. (Council. State-subsidised in-
spectors are to be elected by the Miners’
Union; they are to have full power to initi-
ate prosecutions, call and cross-examine
witnesses, Take a hand at inquests, etc. As
further evidence of the need of check inspec-
tors Collier quoted the opinion of Dr.
Cumpston, an admitted authority on the sub-
ject, that he found 33 per cent, of under-
ground mine workers employed in the State
to be suffering from fibrosis. The Assembly
has acceded to Collier’s request, and he has
told the Council that if it doesn’t agree it
will only hurry along the day when the
powers which it now possesses will be trans-
ferred to the Commonwealth Parliament.

Duke Extended, Maryborough (Vic.), still
struggles to get its head up. Just £50,000 of
calls and gold have gone into the mine with-
out a solitary div. to cheer the suckers. The
co. lias a fairly big block of ground round
Nos. 3 and 4 rises to panel, and it has been
getting some fair prospects off No. 5 rise.
But cheaper work— not lower wages, but a
lot more hustle—will have to be available if
the old mine is to pay.

ol a nice dab of gold. The trough is tumbled

Co. the chance to quit 7000 forfeited shares

. Kingston Oil Wells Co. is making a new
issue of shares at 25., evidentlv im-
pelled by the South Australian Oil Wells
Co. s report that it has seen petroleum in
its Robe bore at 1163 ft. The Kingston Co.
holds 20 square miles of oil areas near the
Ocean Oil Co.’s bore, now well down
on its way to where it is believed the
oil sands are.
On the present look of things antimony
mines will soon be almost as common as gold
mines in Victoria. A mostly-Melbourne co.
has just taken off its coat to open up a belt
of country in the bush at Clonbinane, near
Kilmore ; and various other likely patches are
being prospected. L
# #
“Gumholes”: In 1900. 1,5- years ago, gas
was struck in the Roma bore, which gave, on
analysis, a paraffin base; and yet no deter-
mined effort has been made to find the
oil pool from which the gas came. The Ryan
Government . has now promised us an Oil
Monopoly Bill. If that will monopolise to
the Government all prospecting for oil, this
generation can cease to hope for any benefit
from a Bananaland oil industrv. Surely if
there is one activity which might be left to
I rivate Enterprise longer than others it is
prospecting. It is such a wild-cat game, with
success often as not due to ignorance and
faith in one’s own luck, that with a Govern-
ment guided by expert opinion, pulled by poli-
tical strings, clogged and doomed to wasteful-
ness by an impersonal interest, there are a
thousand chances to one it will fritter away
money and find nothing. Let the Govern-
ment, when the oil is found, claim a certain
area and handsomely reward the prospector,
and leave prospecting to the fever-brained
class who have done such valuable work for
Australia in every other class of mining.
We do not want to be exploited by local
monopolists, but we would like, if the oil
is here, to tap it and be saved from the ex-
ploitation of foreign monopolists.

WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, September 20, 1915.
My Dear Arini, —
Mayor Richards travelled up to Gosford to
open its 31st annual flower show. A few
Sydneysiders who also made the journey
came back bursting with enthusiasm for the
lunch provided by the committee and the
gorgeous vision of bush flora in the School
of Arts. The only criticism was that the
show had a prevailing feature which used
to shout for help at Manly wild-flower dis-
plays—the blooms were squeezed so tightly
together that the little pink roses were
almost scarlet with suffocation.
The vice-regals are making a procession
among North Coast butter factories and
musical prodigies, and the trek won't close
till the middle of this week. Miss Strick-
land, who likes these country jaunts with
father, has the Titian-haired Miss Finn to
back up her admiration for the fatted calf
and the bloated sugar-mill.
Captain Segrave, who has been lying in
port for a few weeks while his cruiser Dash
was refurbished, has been recalled to London,
probably for another job. Captain Baillie-
Hamilton, who has been here before, takes
his cabin.
Dr. Ashburton Thompson passed out in
London last week, after a career which Syd-
ney remembers with gratitude. In the days
when Plague stalked the rat-haunted water-
front, and even the squeak of a mouse
sounded like the advance of bubonic legions,
the Board of Health chief put his finger on
ihe spot, and by his campaign established a
reputation that was easily world-wide. The
city has grown older and cleaner since then,
and Thompson was the man who, until his
retirement a couple of years ago, did more
than anybody else to make it wash itself.
Mrs. Thompson was a daughter of the late
Julian Salomons. When the big doctor pro-
posed the lady was a widow, having married
in the first place a son of the late Justice
G. B. Simpson. He had two brothers, who
loved music as he loved it himself—Gerald
Marr, long-time the S. M. Herald’s first-
night critic, and HCmilius, who for many
years mixed his crotchets with the medical
oversight of S. Australia’s one big prison.
The green fly-nets that are going to
Turkey are weird-looking net bags with
elastic bands at each end. The Gallipoli fly
is more numerous and pestilential than the
Australian beast. So the floating mesh-
veils that dangle round a bushman’s beard
would be no fence against the Mahometan.
Enthusiasts in the country who yearn to run
up this millinery are therefore warned not
to copy the local article. The Dardanelles
model fits the hat at the top, while the lower
elastic clutches the soldier’s throat. Where-
upon the baffled enemy squats on the Kan-
garoo’s broad shoulders, and says the brute
would be too tough, anyway.
For a modest penny you can record a vote
for the Queen of Allies’ Day. Several theatri-
cal stars of one million candle-power are
hinted at as competitors. In spite of the
Dreadful Secrecy insisted on it is safe to
guess that there’ll be at least one red head
and one mop of flaxen curls in the Corona-
tion Stakes. The beautiful simplicity of the
voting scheme is that every lady is not only
allowed, but encouraged, to supply her
friends with as many voting papers as she
chooses to pay for —or as she can wheedle
them into paying for. Cash is the thing,
and the votes help to buy bread for Serbs
and Poles and other lean belligerents.
like this make the world seem
a very small ant-hill. Ex-Aide Wallis and
Major Macnaghten, formerly on Governor
Strickland’s staff, were wounded on the same
day at Gallipoli. They were carried down
to the same hospital ship and were able to
inquire about each other’s damages every
morning in Egypt. Now there’s word that
Macnaghten has been badlv hit again. He
was the young man who made such a suc-
cess of his job when he was area-officer in
the Woolloomooloo district.
The linen tea at South Sydney Hospital,
with Matron O’Connor in charge, brought
forth a plentiful supply of visitor’s with
brewn paper parcels under their arms. The
Willie Cooper dame was handed the inevi-
table bouquet when she stepped out of her
petrol chaise. In return she gave the
nurses good value in a 20-guinea cheque.
Miss Stone, who was purred over by the visi-
tors, was the lioness of the tea party.
Formerly a sister in the Zetland Hospital,
this tall grey-eyed nurse is just back with a
batch of wounded, after months of waiting
on the injured Kangaroo. She goes back
shortly to Egypt.
Chaperoned by G; G. Neill, of the Tourist
Department, a party of pressmen had a
recent week-end at Burrinjuck. From
Goondah to the village by the great dam
they travelled iu the tram that bumps along
over a foot gauge. Access to this meeting
of the waters is better than it used to be,
and as many as 300 tourists in a month
have been shown the local exhibit by the
Government guide. But the idea of making
it a wildly-popular resort for ladies who
travel with several Saratogas is still in the
nebulous state.
For the finals of the Manufacturers’ Week
competitions, the Repertory Theatre was
filled with fond relatives who were respon-
sible for gusts of applause. Some of the
talent on exhibition was 6ft. high and wore
tin 1 swallow-tails of an effete civilisation.
Some more of it was large and buxom and
swathed in long skirts. But the best of it
was young and tender, with sweet, chirrupy
throstle calls. Little Thelma Burness, the
star child singer, promises to warble like a
nightingale if her notes are not forced before
she thinks of putting up her golden locks.
Mr. Vicars, of the Chamber of Manufactures,
handed Thelma a medal to go on with. He
passed on a similar award to Minnie Hig-
gins, a flaxen-haired infant whose recitation
was another sparkling circumstance.
In the midst of a dust storm, Minister of
Education Griffith last week laid a founda-
tion-stone. It was a memorial slab for the
new Golden Fleece Free Kindergarten. The
old rookery had been pulled down to make
way for the brick tenement for which an
enthusiastic women’s committee has bor-
rowed about £l3OO. John Mackay, presi-
dent of the Union which barracks for the
children of mean streets, gave out the text.
I think it was: “Blessed is the beggar, for
her bowl removes mountains of mortgages.”
Mr. Griffith having patted the little grey
slab into its niche, the committee put its
gratitude into a trowel brooch for Mrs.
Griffith. Secretary Ellen Desailly (who
now adds up figures for the kindergarten in
an office in the new Education Palace),
Mrs. John Mackay, the redoubtable Mrs.
Francis Anderson, and Mrs. W. G. Boor-
man were foremost in the proceedings.
Mrs. Barff, wife of the Uni. registrar, is
a steady goer on the trail of Good Works.
Her latest hobby is the new League of
Honor, by which some of the ’Varsity
women hope to help the little things that
Tommy leaves behind him. This week’s
gathering was in St. James’s Hall.
Mrs. Louis Barber is the surviving sister
of the late Justice G. B. Simpson. The
old lady, avlio is in her eighties, is, like other
members of the Simpson family. Sydney-
born. She belonged to the days when travel-
ling was set with thorns, and has never been
out of Australia. Till her memory flagged
she used to tell quaint tales of Sydney in
the era when a water-cart dripped round
the streets with supplies.
Papal legate Cerretti paid a visit to the
Jubilee fete for the Kincumber orphans.
Hordes of youngsters swarmed round the
arrival, in the hope that the vdeasaut-looking
Italian would wear his best purple frock.
But be made an unobtrusive entry, and so
far as the color of his clothes went you
couldn’t have told him from an undertaker.
The visitor had a rest from the voice of the
raffler during a long programme dotted with
juvenile performers.
At the fancy-dress carnival for the Ivin-
cnmber kiddies, Mrs. Hugh Ward did the
judging, and handed Miss Amy Yard’s
pupils several prizes. In the infant section
the best effort was made by youngsters who
masqueraded as the pots that said the kettles
were no gentlemen.
The age of miracles is not yet past. In-
stead of the loss that threatened at the open-
ing of the 10 days’ Town Hall raid for the
Kincumber orphans, the St. Joseph’s sister-
hood are joyfully counting their profits. The
drawing of the Art Union on Saturday night
finished this gallant attack on a raffle-tired
world.
The Bank of New Zealand’s chairman, Mr.
Harold Beauchamp, came this way from
Wellington last week.
Sydney will be feeding the monkeys at
the new Zoo next Easter. The Public
Works Department, having money to burn,
has taken the construction over, and is
rushing it forward. The job won’t be com-
plete till June, but they will march most of
the animals two by two across to Bradley’s
Head in April or thereabouts.
I hope that somehow the late Alfred
Usher knows what a beautiful funeral he
had. The Liedertafel was there and sang
its sweetest. Mrs. Hugh Ward by chance
heard of her old friend’s serious condition a
few hours before the light flickered out.
But the last bar was reached so suddenly
that she was the only friend to see the
kindly old gentleman off.
Young Arthur Champion, eldest son of
Canberra’s Anglican shepherd, who was
wounded some time ago, was at latest in a
London Hospital, having an injured knee
massaged. Two younger brothers have thus
far done their bit without reported mishap.
The new flounced skirts will billow round
Randwick lawn for the first time on October
2, weather permitting. On the same crowded
spring afternoon the Cullen lady will open a
patriotic sale or robbery at Gordon.
The Girls’ Realmers who learned first-aid
at the knee of Dr. Emma Buckley swell with
pride at the lady's latest achievements. Dr.
Emma is supposed to know the domestic
habits of most microbes. While waiting to
go to the Front she has been acting as bac-
teriologist in a London hospital with such
success that still another Australian is being-
talked about in the big antheap.
Mrs. Ralph Worrall had a tennis party
the other afternoon. Any lady who had
forgotten to take a Christmas gift for sol-
diers in the Island hospital would have been
assured of a chilly welcome. But nobody
was so absent minded.
The large, handsome Margaret Vinton
continues to wear fascinating frocks as the
dress-designer to the firm of Potash and
Perlmutter at the Criterion. Her black-and-
white creation in Act 1 is a shimmering-
white garb, touched with the raven hue the
magpie flaunts on his waistcoat. The
Criterion was a very full house on Satur-
day night, with a sprinkling of khaki to
remind us of the war —in case Abe Potash
was making us forget any row but his own
guerilla warfare with his partner. Chief
Secretary Black sat in the front row of the
d.c. Inquisitive neighbors were unable to
decide whether he crossed his legs at one
crisis or merely knocked his knees together.
The Assembly adds to its good looks by
the return of George W. Fuller for Woilon-
dilly. There is no more carefully-
groomed man than the ex-Federal Minister-
the first, by the way, to climb so high as a
seat in the Commonwealth Cabinet and then
creep so low as a perch in the State fowl-
house.
The osprey has by no means perished in
the general cataclysm. But it droops nowa-
days like the curve of a crescent moon. I
saw a specimen at the theatre the other
night in its present tamed shape. It is
much better to sit behind the 1915 bit of
fluff than it was to meet one in the days
when it reared itself like a tousled nine-inch
quill upon a frightful porcupine.
The Women Painters have revoked their
intention of holding a show this side of
Christmas. They have postponed the
annual affair till the autumn.
A search for queens is foaming over even
the most unpretentious suburbs. Balmain,
which wants cash for its Civil Ambulance
and hospital, is thrilled with the quest.
Dr. Bob Stopford has stepped aside from
his pet hobby, bull-pups, to work as hon.
sec. of the carnival.
On October 1 and 2 the Repertory
Theatre houses a show for the Red Cross.
Thelma Maddrell, of the large Braidwood
family, is starred iu double-sized letters
in the amateur cast.
William Murdoch, the tennis-playing Aus-
tralian pianist, was here on a return visit to
his native land about three years ago, and
rumor then engaged him to various Sydney
damsels. Now he has really done it. But
on the other side. The girl who wears the
engagement ring is Ellen Tuckfield. She is
a musician, too.
Prof. David’s only son is with the
R.A.M.C. in France. A son-in-law, Dr.
Mclntyre, who recently got his diploma at
Edinburgh ’Varsity, is also off to the Front
—or as near the Front as the surgeons are
allowed to go.
It will be interesting to note whether the
shy Russian pianist Pintel will get a show
in the scramble for billets at the Conserva-
torium. Pintel fell in love with Australia
when he toured it as the pianist of the
Calve co.. so he came “home” to Sydney a
couple of years ago. But he hides his light
behind green baize doors, where lie culti-
vates the Young Person, and gives musical
refreshers to lady amateurs.
In spite of the din of war specials the
Ideal Home Exhibition, fathered by the In-
stitute of N.SAV. Architects, will be held
next month at the Town Hall. Every
woman is invited to send in her own plans,
and show, incidentally, what she thinks of
male architects who design kitchen fixtures
for lady giraffes, and generally build a small
house as if the woman who runs it had 40
legs.
H. J. Preston, of Sydney, who was one
of the first men in Australia to beat his
motor-car into an ambulance, is still driving
the vehicle for the Red Cross in France.
Thea Proctor, the handsome Mosman girl,
is still pegging away at fan designs in her
Chelsea studio. She was a successful exhibi-
tor at the recent International Society’s
show in London.
The Persian Garden Song-Cycle encore
has been pushed further forward into Octo-
ber to avoid a collision with race-week.
There is a notion of getting either the
Little Theatre or the Royal for the repeat.
Little Mrs. Quesnel, who used to teac-h
vocal trills in Sydney, is spending a holiday
in her old haunt. She is on the Elder Con-
servatorium staff in Adelaide now.
Old Lamps for New Vests is the latest cry
of the Sock Fund dames. They vary it by
asking for any old silver goods—thimbles,
brooches, trinkets, bracelets, etc. —for which
you have no further use. Heaven knows
what they mean to do with the oddments
that will pour in to the Sock Depot, Bull’s
Chambers. But they call it “The Silver
Thimble Fund” to provide sheepskin vests
for our soldiers.
The North Shore Hospital sends a wail
for help at a sale of work in the local School
of Arts on Saturday.
Parson John Ferguson, the big Scotcliby-
terian who is renowned for his wedding-
breakfast orations, tied the knot between
W. Scott Fell, of the Mosman family, and
Doris McDonald. The bride’s male parent,
a major, was detained at the Dardanelles
by a circumstance over which he has less
control than he hopes to have presently. It
wears a fez.
There are still quiet hearths where the
din of battle does not drown the hum of
the kettle. Home Reading circles gather
in these nests. This week Mosman, which,
like Lane Cove, is inclined to literary medi-
tation, is going to house a gathering to dis-
cuss the writing of Richard Sheridan. It
is a far cry to Tipperary! At Woolwich
these circles occasionally rub each other up
the wrong way by meeting to discuss the
ethics of Marie Corelli. Even knitting is
liable to be suspended when “The Sorrows
of Satan” is attacked with a gimlet.
The committee that packs up comforts for
the Army Service Corps—(lie hewers of
wood and drawers of water for the fighting
men—occupies an upper chamber at IS
Barrack-street. It has just sent off three
cases of garments mixed with chocolate and
cigarettes. On September 27 a matinee to
achieve Christmas presents is booked for
the Palace Theatre. The J.C.W. firm is
letting some of its mummers off the chain
for the occasion.
Dr. Ercole, one of the best-known of
back-country surgeons, passed in from Gren-
fell the other day on the way to the Front.
The story goes that the last time he was
in his native Italy he had to run for it. An
impulsive youngster, lie got mixed up with
political scheming of which the Government
didn’t approve, and, being at the time in
the Army, was put under arrest. The
guard consisted of men whom he had com-
manded. Seizing a favorable chance, lie
simply ordered his captors to do something
or other, and skipped across to Switzerland.
But liis Government forgave him a year or
two ago.
Another Italian doctor, Sisca, who has
fallen on more evil days, was removed from
the roll last week for “infamous conduct.”
After years of ill health this unfortunate
was driven to lend his name to a Sydney
“health parlor.” One watches the descent
with sorrow. Sisca was the first medical
man to try to arouse the public of Victoria
to the seriousness of “miners’ disease” —a
subject which has since had much profes-
sional attention there and in Westralia, and
especially in South Africa.
French Consul Chayet has just come back
to the dizzy round of patriotic shows after
a month’s holiday in Queensland,
Amongst the minor horrors of wartime is
the Theatre League’s threat to sponsor a
missing-line playlet at the Queen Victoria
Club. The guests, of course, are to fill the
void.
The Arts and Crafters are having a rocky
time. The society keeps its spirits up, how-
ever, with regular doses of mental food.
This takes the form of lecturettes on such
subjects as native timbers and thoughts on
furniture. This month’s snack consists of
architect John Sulman’s remarks on the
Renaissance and Modern Architecture.
The Frencli-Australian League of Help
iu Sydney is scouring town and country for
warm garments wherewith to meet the com-
ing winter in Europe. The end of our own
alleged cold season fills the wardrobes of
the well-to-do with surplus clothes. There
never was a better use to which they might
be put than the League can find for them.
Miss Soubeiran is hon. sec.
Prophets who predict that ostrich plume's
are “off” this season don’t know the eternal
feminine. The woman who will look the
other way when she sees a feathered hat
that fits hubby’s purse is not born. In spile
of the promised orgy of floral millinery, any
knowledgajble Petticoat will swear that race
week and fowl fluff will run in double
harness. The wider brim which is creeping
in with warmer sunshine takes very kindly
to the graceful plume. Thank goodness, the
tragic toques of Easter week, rising like the
profile of North Head on the feminine brow,
are only memories.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, September 20, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Lady Helen in her spirited defence of
Dr. Barrett pins discussion down to the
question of lied Cross stores or no Red
Cross stores on the Ballarat. She severely
ignores the most vital charge made by Dr.
Springthorpe, namely, that there was no
Red Cross depot nearer the Front than
Alexandria for fully 11 days after fighting
began. There were no supplies of Red
Cross comforts —or very few —on the hos-
pital ships which carried the first fearfully
wounded Australians back to Egypt. The
journey in some cases took four days. There
were no pyjamas, air pillows, sheets or other
comforts of the sort. The men reached the hos-
pitals in Egypt in the shot-torn, mud-stained,
blood-covered clothes in which they fought.
Surgeons, operating full speed ahead for 10
hours at a stretch, could only have the
actual wounds bound up. Doctors at the
Front were crying out for milk-foods for
the wounded, while there were hundreds of
tins of the stuff held up at distant Alex-
andria. All these things have been estab-
lished, also that there was money in plenty,
-besides stores of everv kind of Red Cross
comfort in Egypt long before that first
fighting. Whoever neglected to prepare
for the big emergency should not need
any other Hell than the thought of the boys
who “died of wounds.”
At Stonington, where the Victorian State
Governor is housed, Yates the butler still
reigns in the pantry, even as he did in the
days of coach-proprietor Wagner, who built
the mansion. After the Wagner executors
sold the place to the Government. Yates
became a public servant, and as such has
stood at the elbow of 5| Governors —Syden-
ham Clarke, Talbot, Carmichael, the late
John Fuller, Madden and Stanley. Yates
is a beautifully round, pink, mellow speci-
men of his kind, with a ripe voice and a
sympathetic manner at table.
Walter “Coomete” Bayles, a grazier in
the fatted Caramut region of the Western
District, runs about 2000 head of poultry in
addition to the sheep which pay his land
Year after year Bayles enters a pen
or two at the Burnley egg-laying competi-
tions. Last time his hens came 50th in
an entry of 50. Was Walter down-hearted?
Not a bit! He had a heart-to-heart talk
with the ladies, and the figures for this year’s
competition show the “Coomete” birds to be
only a few cackles behind the leaders, run-
ning third, with a good chance of being “in
the money.” Walter Bayles is a brother of
Norman, who is M.L.A. for Toorak, where
the society hens roost high.
The fashionable Macoboy sisters gave
their threatened big concert at the Audi-
torium last week. Outwardly the two girls
looked Dreams, but inwardly, they were vol-
canoes owing to several maddening hitches
in the glorious arrangements. For one thing
not a single Governor turned up, though
they had pretty well all promised they
would. The result was that the carefully-
upholstered semi-circle reserved for them was
occupied only by the John Maddens, the
Dame for once in severe black instead of the
customary royal red plush cloak. She sat
up very straight and clasped a dainty little
bouquet of spring flowers hard and tight
in her hand. The second tragedy concerned
the programmes. Very elaborate books had
been ordered, full of portraits of the stars.
These artistic treasures went astray, and
hurried slips were printed for the informa-
tion of the people who were to provide the
applause. These were doled out at sixpence
each by girl sellers, and the victims paid up
cheerfully enough, though they had been
waylaid at the door by collectors for some-
thing or other. When the concert was half-
way through, the lost programmes turned up
and the vampire girls (tulle and ribbons
and all) came smiling with them and their
collecting boxes! But this time the audi-
ence drew back with agonised moans, and
the rattled management tried to reassure
everybody by giving the things away at the
door as the listeners left for home.
Molly Macoboy was the prize frocker of
the family. She was in black. Wynne
came a good second; her lot was in black
and white that somehow had a grey effect.
The long programme was made longer by
the performers’ determination to give en-
cores if the audience so much as sneezed.
Mary Conly, plump and portly, was
wrapped in a flame-colored gown with black
net clinging tightly to the generous curves.
It carried my mind back to the well-filled
grates of winter. Rita Hope wore flame-
color, but it was a different tinted flame,
worn very, very short and side-splitting.
Natalie Dawson sported her blue satin cor-
sets over her fluffy petticoat —or that’s how
it appeared to the puzzled costume-spies.
Marie Noski looked like a Mrs. Humphry
Ward heroine in simple white, and Mollie
Taylor gleamed and glowed in white satin
with a pink rose.
There were loads of enthusiastic lads in
khaki present, and flags and decorations
helped to give a martial air to the function.
Thirty of the soldiers belonged to the 14th
battalion, whose number p: or young Mick
Macoboy also wore till he was killed at
Gallipoli. Some of his wounded mates who
had been granted special leave from the
base hospital hobbled in.
_ Mary Conly sang “To a Hero” exqui-
sitely. She is becoming so plump, however,
that she finds difficulty in bowing. Mrs.
Fryer, the Maeoboys’ teacher, who came on
at the last to help with “Rule Britannia,”
wore a black velvet and silk arrangement
that resembled pa’s very elaborate dressing-
gown.
On Saturday the Quamby Club seethed with
bridge, song and commerce to provide a room
in the heart of the city where wounded
soldiers who have medical appointments in
town may go for rest and nourishment. A
luxurious produce stall faced the lift in the
corridor, and matrons of great social tonnage
amazed their cooks, who didn’t know where
they had been, by landing home at unearthly
hours with baskets of vegetables. Mrs.
Alawa Buchanan won two dressed <-honks
and a bottle of champagne for a shilling.
For the milder type of gambler there was
roulette, a soldier with one arm in a sling-
acting as croupier. Mrs. Leslie Stuart pre-
sented two bridge tables out-and-out, and
then invited her own chosen plungers. Miss
Hume Black’s orchestra played, and Maggie
Stirling sang. Daughter Mary (Mrs. Croom),
in purple velvet, dimples and pearl earrings,
adorned the programme in both a musical
and decorative way.
From the official recorder in Adelaide:—
C. .T. Valentine, the ex-Chief Inspector of Stock,
died at North Adelaide on Sunday. When T. T.
Morris decided to exchange the billet of Chief
Inspector of Sheep for that of manager of Anlaby
station in ’65, Mr. Valentine stepped into his shoes
and almost immediately was appointed with M’s.L.C.
Bonney and Cavenagh to inquire into the great
drought of ’G4-’66. .They inquired very thoroughly ;
but it would have been better for Australia if they
had abolished the thing.
Another old colonist to pass out during the week
was Mrs. Douglas Gooch, of Cooke's Plains, who
arrived in the State in ’55. Her father, the late
J. Loudon, took up a pastoral lease in the Far
North that afterwards became known as Yadla-
malka, and while there • opened ’ up and workgd>
Cariewerloo station, out-back from Port Augusta.
From there Miss Loudon married Mr. Gooch, who
was her father’s partner, and when the drought of
’64 drove the settlers from the north, Mr. Gooch
took charge of various stations, finally going to the
Burnside estate, near Strathalbyn, where he died
about 12 years ago.
T. B. Harrington, the Adelaide manager of the
National Bank, has received news of the death of
his son Keith at the Dardanelles, in the early part
of August. Tlis other boy is reported ill, and in
hospital at Malta.
The temerity of N. S. AVales in launching a Uni-
versal Service League has stirred up the local Peace
Society, which passed a hurried resolution expressing
its entire disapproval and abhorrence of the whole
affair. Up till about a year ago there were people
like that in Belgium. But the Huns soon con-
verted them.
The howling north wind played over the Outer
Harbor on Monday, with the result that the Mol-
davia had to hang about doing nothing till things
calmed down a little. This is the second time in
a few months that the delay has occurred. Which
looks as if hostile critics of the Outer Harbor
scheme knew what they were talking about.
The Cheer-Up Society gave a good-bye lunch to
750 Expeditionaries on Saturday, and a couple of
days afterwards the latest batch of returned wounded
were given a reception at the Town Hall. There
was a Gift Afternoon for Army Nurses at Austral
Gardens on Thursday, and on the same afternoon a
start was made with the building of the Cheer-Up
Soldiers’ “Hut,” at the back of the City Baths, by
the turning of the first sod. The land has been lent
by the Government for as long as the society wants
it, unless railway extensions complicate matters, in
which case the “hut” will have to be transported
elsewhere. The whole thing is being made as plain
and bright and airy as possible. There are no
“decorations” —for which may the Heavens be
praised!—and the general idea has been to get as
much comfort as possible for the money— £15,000.
The Dickens Fair next week is being relied on
to bring a good cheque for the building fund.
J. W. Searcy, a cousin of the clerk-assistant of
the Assembly, has been lifted from sergeant to lieu-
tenant in the Australian Engineers at Gallipoli.
He was in the landing of April 25 and has been
slightly damaged twice.
A motor smash at a level crossing on the recently-
opened Willunga railway resulted in the death of
R. H. Trezise, who was driving the car, and Mrs.
Luke, the wife of the manager of Tatachilla vine-
yard. Mr. Luke is on his way back from Marseilles.
The three-year-old Arts and Crafts Society held an
exhibition on Monday, which the Mayoress opened
without casualties. Of course the war- has affected
the society, but for all that the show of exhibits
was good. The committee has purchased a spin-
ning-wheel, and intends to give lessons in the home-
industry our foremothers knew so well.
A long-dated after-death honor has come to
Nathaniel Whight, a baker, of Edwardstown. Nearly
two years ago he was fatally burned while saving his
mother in a household outbreak of fire. A Iloval
Humane Society's certificate has just been awarded
to him!
Will Ashton’s picture-exhibition was the most sue-
eesiful thing of the kind, in Adelaide for years.
People not only attended, but bought, G.-G. Ferguson
setting a good example by acquiring a view of
Adelaide as seen from the Hills, and Hans Ileysen, a
fellow-artist, following suit. During the week
nearly 40 pictures were sold.
Mr. Allen, of this city—the same who in
his peaceful moments works a patent dye
which makes flappers of grandmothers and
restores faded fashionables —is going to the
same old spot. There is wailing amongst
the Touched Up who depended on the per-
sonal administrations of the Restorer. This
war is brutal.
The patriotic auction has done'its dash.
A special feature of the cafe chantant at
the Oriental on the 12th prox. has been thus
advertised : “There will be no auctions, with
the exception of one flag.” Once the initial
dollar has been paid, the patriot’s only
remaining duty will be to sit down to a
beautiful afternoon full of tea and cakes
and Red Cross talk.
Captain Broad returned this week to
home and wife. The latter was Miss Beat-
rice Best. He was badly wounded in the
landing at Gallipoli.
Mr. Black, of Tarwin Aieadows, the
rich Western District bachelor who ran a
little milking herd of 1000 cows, is the latest
eligible to join the army.
Artist B. E. Minns, who returned to Aus-
tralia last week with a view to getting among
the blacks again and painting them in their
habits as they live, left his wife in England.
She is absorbed holding up an umbrella or
something to shield the Minns establishment
near Hendon from bombs. Their place is
close to a big aerodrome which the Germs
would dearly like to burst up. Minns dis-
closes the fact that a colony of Australian
artists is springing up at Golder’s Green.
Hilda Spong’s brother (the scenic artist) is
there, and George Ashton —in addition to
the Minuses. The village has only cropped up
since the advent of the big Flying School,
and now everyone wonders why it didn’t
arrive before. It stands high over Hamp-
stead Heath, and the scenery is beautiful.
The Minuses have also a mia-mia at Folke-
stone, which is opposite Boulogne; and all
the summer the occupants heard the crack —
rumble —boom of artillery. Kipling, stay-
ing at Brighton—farther along stilt —also
confessed to being troubled by the artillery
racket.
The George Fairbairns’ son Clive cables
the family headquarters that he lias"-got a
commission in the Scots’ Guards.
Norman Bayles last week lost his wife,
who had been an invalid for two or three
years. Before her marriage she was
Marion Clarke, of Hobart.
Buried at Boroondara a few days
ago, Mrs. J. S. Armstrong, whose name
woke up a lot of Victorian Law Court his-
tory. For very many years John Simpson
Armstrong was Crown Prosecutor at Gen-
eral Sessions at Geelong, but he lias been
so long dead that this generation hardly
remembers anything about him.
There was a rumor that Willy,.Lawson,
who used to flutter round Melbourne some
years ago, had gone to the hideous length
of enlisting in the French army. As a
piou-piou he would have got |d. a day (less
separation allowance for dependants), while
the use of his motor-car and his man-
servant would have been withheld from him.
The dreadful story turns out to be apocry-
phal. In point of fact he has taken a large
house in England, and is living in sylvan
quietude with George Walker, one of Syd-
ney’s imperishable twins. London is fre-
quently refreshed by the appearance of
William and George in such costumes as
Solomon in all his glory would have been
disqualified from the Tabernacle for wear-
ing.
Old W. B. Cumming, of Mount Fyans,
who had made one of a sheepish half-dozen
in the Mortlake shire council for the past
33 years, has at last given up local politics
and taken to his armchair and slippers.
There are various ways of getting immortal
fame, and you might win it even in a shire
council. But please don’t think that “W. 8.”
was out after anything so unsubstantial.
You see, there are several Gumming estates
in the Darlington region, and the family
has always found it worth while to keep
one representative in the council.
Colonel Rhodes fled through Melbourne
the other day on his way to Egypt, where
he will be the commissioner representing the
M.L. Red Cross. Sister Mrs. Allister Clarke
came down from her beautiful country
garden to see him off, and trade-commis-
sioner Manson and other male Maorilanders
edged m a farewell dinner. “Bill” Crow
flapped through the city in the wake of his
principal, to whom he will play private sec-
retary. Crow says he has not had time to
breathe or brush his hat since he made one
in the Andrew Fisher expedition to Eng-
land. There was a zoological tinge about
that passenger-list—Fisher, Lamb, Shep-
herd and Crow.
Tommy Cochrane is acting as town guide
to a brother in khaki, who looks like a mag-
nified image of Tommy himself. The
warrior was through the African campaign
under Botha, and was also concerned in the
far-back Boer war. Pleased at still being
whole in wind and limb, he came this way
to see his mother before trying his luck
again.
Despite the Zeppelins, Mrs. Frankie
Stewart Murray is busy jumping on the lids
of her various Saratogas, which are all lab-
elled “London.” The flaxen-haired kidlet
Mary, who is one of Jennie Brenan’s prize
pupils, goes with her; and Mr. Murray (I’d
nearly forgotten him) will be at hand to see
to the luggage.
One of the late Francis Ormond’s
adopted daughters (he had two, and an
adopted son) is now the widow of a Western
District Austen. She has been devoting her
time and money to the care of our soldiers
in the Egyptian hospitals. The part of
ministering angel should suit her, for she is
a pretty blonde woman with an endearing
manner. I hear the wounded Australians
are devoted to her. The hot climate, how-
ever, interrupted her good work, and she is
down here for a brief rest.
Oakleigh Hall, the Melbourne home of
Prime Minister- Fisher, was the scene of a
festive but subdued gathering on Saturday
afternoon, when Elsie Irvine, sister of Mrs.
Fisher, promised to obey Leonard Motteram,
of. Port Melbourne. There was only one
bridesmaid, Miss Annie Irvine, and the quiet
affair was stage-managed by the Rev. C.
Strong.
Edward Bromby, an old identity of his-
toric Heidelberg, who was one of the scholars
present on the day when Melbourne Gram-
mar School was opened in 1858, was also on
the spot, as a member of the school council,
at the installation of headmaster R. P.
Franklin the other day. Edward Bromby’s
headmaster, in that first school year, was
his own father, the late Dr. Bromby, one-
time Dean of Melbourne.
Lorna Cooke, a Frankston girl, lias gone
to Egypt to lend a hand with the wounded.
Auntie Estelle Leinpriere, who is in touch
with the nurses, will teach her how to do
real work.
The handsome Mein widow who, owing to
ill health, has not shouldered the house-
keeping burden since her return from her
last overseas trip, is back in her St. Kilda
mansion. She has changed its former label
Falkenstein to Hazel mere, and feels a little
better for it already. Son Douglas, who
enlisted in England, has received the usual
commission and started for the Turkish
trouble in charge of a machine-gun.
Anne Williams and Harold Smith are
giving a vocal and piano recital in conjunc-
tion on Thursday evening, September 23.
The G.-G. and Lady Helen have promised
to toot up, the University has lent the Melba
Hall for the occasion, and the proceeds are
going to the Red Cross.
Florence Hull (Mrs. Arthur Kenny, of
Tasmania) has published a recruiting song
for which her parson-brother, Tremlett Hull,
of Skipton, supplied the words. Dufault
made a success of one of her songs, and she
is now writing him another. By the way.
the Frencli-Canadian, who is now in the
U.S.A., is due back in Australia next
August.
One recently-chronicled engagement has
ended in a hurry. The bride-elect has left on
a trip to England with her mother; the for-
saken swain is hurrying towards one of the
Fronts; and the parson who might have got
the job has climbed a tree.
Mansion holders in the most exclusive
Domain-road portion of South Yarra (let’s
hope there will be “exclusive” spots in
Heaven!) are fiercely opposing the conver-
sion of the George Cliirnsides’ palace into a
private hospital. Confound the sick! Blow
the afflicted ! Let ’em go and get well some-
where else!
Miss Lucy Rowe is handing out buckets-
ful of encouragement to budding musical
talent, and spraying the tender shoots to
kill the eodlin moth of criticism. There is
to be a party at the Reps’ Club on October
1, when some pale, new stars are to flicker.
Stella Tower, a soprano Conservatorium.
pupil specially favored by Melba, will
strengthen the programme. The little
Scapini from Maoriland will make her first,
bow in Melbourne at this show. —The ex-
perienced Hal Elvins is to attend to the
accompaniments and play some of Fritz
I lart’s compositions.
At Jellalabad, the Tom Dowlings’ ancient
Western District squattage, a bunch of
local sheep-owners gathered around the
old honeysuckle arbor the other afternoon,
and by earnestly selling one another a
variety of pincushions, Victoria sandwiches,
bottled fruits and so on raised a hundred
pounds for the Red Cross. Mrs. Gerald
Gumming w T as the organiser.
Young and Jackson’s ancient nooker.v,
opposite the Central Station, changed hands
18 months ago, the Young boys having been
warned in a dream of the dry wrath to
come. Norman Young, who had for some
years found a uniform of use at evening
parties, is now Major Young, of Gallipoli.
The tall wife of an absent-in-Egypt
medico lately devised an entertainment for
soldiers’ wives. She roped in a girl who
owns a pretty voice as sole performer. When
the soprano presented herself at the hall
where the entertainment was to take place
an Officious Person who was acting as door-
keeper greeted her with “Are you a mother?”
The dazed singer .murmured, “Er —no —not
yet!” “Unless you are a mother,” the O.P.
continued, “you casb’t come in.” “Well,” fal-
tered the soprahsQ| ratlier short notice,
isn’t it? I’m afraid they’ll have to postpone
the entertainment.” Then someone rushed
to the rescue, and all was peace.
Lady Juliet Duff has been laying down
the principles of her new Economy League
in London. She “strictly avoids” latest
fashions in dress and pre-war fashions in
costly feeding. She entertains only one or
two intimate friends at lunch instead of a
“catty” mob, and she often “frugally” feeds
off “chicken and pudding, or even meat and
cheese.” These economies are touching
suggestions of “top-hole” patriotism. The
fifth pledge of Lady Duff’s League binds the
League to employ “no men-servants except
ineligibles.” Presumably when footmen
Jeames and Chawles plead ineligibility, the
League doctor is sent for to put his stetho-
scope to their fatted calves.
Yours sincerely,
JIHABBAL
lou Ward's proposal to tax petrol and
kerosene has raised a growl of protest from
fishermen and settlers in the Sounds districts.
Kerosene, the main illuininant for country
people and the poorer folk in the towns, and
petrol, the motive power of the boats on
which the settlers who live in the bays and
sounds depend to reach market, are absurd
things to tax, anyhow, unless there is a local
product crying for use.

ALITIES
Pooh Boon” : I’d like to inform “Pick”
(b. 1w 8/ 15) that eels make the headwaters of
the North Tableland, Monaro (N.S.W.), and other
streams the terminus of their wanderings, but they
certainly do not ascend the waterfalls. If they can’t
get round them they go up other branches, devoid of
cataracts, whose headwaters are not far re-
moved from the sources of the streams in which the
waterfalls are located. Then they make a land trip
across. No baby eels are ever found above these
cataracts, which goes to prove that eels do not breed
in such streams. Has anyone ever discovered an cel
m N. S. Wales western waters? I’ve fished in most
or the streams between Longreach (Q.) and Moama
(N.S.W.), and invariably failed to land one.
* * * *
To M hare”: A curious and patient in-
vestigafor found recently fully-formed eels, micro-
seopic in size, but quite lively, in the
float of female eels taken from creeks
in the New England (N.S.W). district.
These young things would in due season
leave their parent’s body to face the
world on their own account, in direct
contradiction of previously-accepted scien-
tific opinion, which avers that all fresh-
water eels, when mature, descend to the
sea to spawn and milt. The female hav-
ing disburdened herself of some trifling
10,000,000 ova, or eggs (the number
being estimated by cutting off a small
section of the roe, counting the eggs in
it, and calculating the remainder), and
the male having fertilised this enormous
provision for posterity, both give up the
ghost. They never return
to the fresh water, but the
young eels, on hatching
out, invariably work up-
stream. Thus says science ;
but the recent discovery casts suspicion
on this theory. Another case in point.
In the damp mud at the bottom of a
dried-up waterhole over a mile from the
sea I found a large eel lying under a flat
stone, and beside it a wriggling bundle
of young eels from about 4in. to 12in. in
length. If these young eels were not the
fry of the large one, but members of an
enormous family hatched from ova de-
posited by some other eel or eels in salt
water, then they must have worked their
way up a partially-dry watercourse, and
eventually arrived at the residence of the
large eel—an unreasonable assumption.
The breeding arrangements of the eel
have puzzled the brains of humanity ever
since the days of Izak Walton, who
wrote: “Some say they [eels] breed by
generation, as other fish do, and others
that they breed, as some worms do, of
mud ; or as rats and mice and many other
living creatures are bred in Egypt by the sun's beat,
or out of the putrefaction of the earth. - ’
David G. Stead, the Ma State Fisheries
expert, added a sft. 7in. banded sea snake to his
vast collection of marine objects the other week,
it is the largest of this venomous species yet hauled
in this part of the world, and was picked up at
Farqubar’s inlet, Manning River.
“To Babarantiko”: Compared with the
brown men of the low-lying coral islands of the
Pacific, the fellah of Egypt and Black Brother of
Queensland (B. 20/8/T5) are the veriest lubbers in
the matter of climbing. The men of the Coral
Islands would scorn the use of the rope grummet—-if
they knew about the thing, which they don't. Grasp-
ing the stem with both hands, and partly grasping,
partly pressing and stepping with the soles and sides
of the foot, they walk up a perpendicular vegetable
«oft. to 80ft. high (double, and more, the height of
the date palm) with ease and celerity. Should the
climber require a rest, which is seldom, he hangs on
in squatting position, helping to clasp the stem with
his knees. The way up is travelled with little frog-
leaps, giving to the looker-on rapid flashes of
quickly-moving yellow-white foot-soles. When nuts
are “pulled,” i.e., cut down, for copra-making, each
man climbs a considerable number of palm trees in
a day. Even women do not hesitate to climb the
“toddy” trees, in which, however, notches are cut
for the prehensile toes. Farther west, down the
Archipelago, when any of the mighty forest trees
are climbed to rob the wild bees of their honey, a
few stout pegs are driven into the trunk about three
feet apart, as the man mounts, and pliable rattan is
tied to the pegs in such a manner that if any peg
breaks or comes oiit the pegs above and below carry
the extra weight. In this way it takes these climb-
ers but a few minutes to reach the first branches of
the great trees.
* * * *
“Ben Sun”: Asa twister swamp red-
gum can beat jarrah every time and give points to a
politician. Some of my neighbors built houses with
the timber, and now they wish they’d fallen down a
bottomless well first. The windows are twisted out
of action, the doors are out of plumb, the roofs look
ashamed of themselves, and the township wilhelm-
goat makes incoherent remarks through the floor
openings at night.
">r #
“Alamon”: Warn “Vigilans” (B.
24/6/’ls) against erecting a fence of Northern Ter-
ritory paper A ark. It’s splendid timber for building
purposes, so long as it does not touch the ground,
but it always rots at that level. For rafters it is
unsurpassed. Cypress pine, after seasoning - , is prac-
tically indestructible, but if used in the green state
is liable to dry-rot. White mangrove, cabbage-gum
and pandanus are utterly useless for building pur-
poses, being the favorite items on the menu of
white ants and borers; yet red mangrove, ironwood,
and what we Territorians call fanpalm are nearly
as good as cypress pine, especially if seasoned. “Vig-
ilans” is correct when he says that as a mattress it
is unequalled. It is also unsurpassed as a cradle
for baby Binghi during the first few weeks of his life.

“Kairuku”: My Papuan house-boys
reckon we’re going to fix the Germans for good arid
all. I was showing them a photograph of my son

he go fight?” asked one of them. “Oh,” I replied,

on alonga big Government to fight till all Germans

“Hay”: That cook of “Milo’s” who used
to paralyse plain turkey's by flying a kite over them
(B. 12/8/’l5) was an expert leg-puller. In the far
northern parts of S. Aus. I have seen droves of plain
turkeys feeding quite unconcerned while kite-hawks
hovered over their heads. By the way, kite-hawks
used to be known to bushmen as Babbage’s chickens,
because so many of these scavengers accompanied the
explorer on his return from Australia’s interior. The
kite is a great camp-follower ; but all the turkey he
ever eats is what the cook throws out, and plain
turkey is such good eating that there is very little of
him wasted. Our way at that time when out after
turkeys was to head a team of loose working bul-
locks in their direction and advance under cover of
the animals till near enough to get a shot. They
appeared to take little notice of horses or bullocks
approaching them. I remember a fine plump bird
141 b. weight from which the cook took two pickle-
bottles of fat in the course of the roasting process.
“Barcoo”: “Lou,” in a recent Bulletin,
describes barcoo as the summer sickness of western
cattle camps. This is penned on the Barcoo River
(W.Q.), which gave the illness its name. Cattle
have given place to the jumbuck, yet the infliction is
as persistent as ever, summer or winter, providing
the flies are numerous. If the grass is green and
abundant the common fly generates in countless and
unbelievable myriads, and hands on barcoo to the
sheep-men. The only way of escape is to take no
food containing sugar and no sweetened tea. Do
that, and you’re immune.
“Ben Sim”: “Pick's” assertion (B.
19/8/’l5) that “furnace stokers yell for stringy-bark
every time” is moonshine. Some years ago, when
the N.S.W. coal-miners’ strike compelled the use of
wood fuel on the Vic. railways, various kinds were
tried as steam-raisers, and stringy-bark got the
unanimous boot off the engine. The medal went to
the closer-grained and heavier box, ironbark, etc. As
to fire-kindlers I’ll back Murray pine every time.
Once started, the sparks will kindle any judiciously-
insured building; in the open, they will kindle the
adjacent country if the time is ripe.
“Pay C.”: I can go one better than
“T.F.’s” 21ft. shark, caught by engineer Walker in
Trial Bay (N.S.W.). Melbourne Museum possesses
two specimens of the Charclrarodon rondoletii breed
that when they stopped growing- touched the 22ft.
mark with their hind tail. Each possessed a diges-
tive cavity that would accommodate The Bulletin's
largest Fatrnan with ease, and if he had got his
deserts a row of molars that would make a
circular-saw look cheap would have been used in the
process. They were deep-sea rovers, and it was dur-
ing an ill-timed visit to Fort Phillip Bay that a
full stop was put to their careers. Fishermen oil
Brighton were responsible for their introduction to
Melbourne's collection of man-eaters. Sea-tigers of
the Rondoletii family have been caught that could
extend the tape 40ft. between tail and snout, and
they bear almost as bad a reputation amongst fisher-
men as a German does in Louvain.
* * * ■»
“Billy Bung” : Twenty-two years of bush
life entitle me to a vote in the best-timber-for-fuel
election. I plump for boree—easy. It is essentially
a good heater, being hard, compact and durable.
Experienced men say that the heat of a boree fire is
too intense for most engines, and lighter woods have
to be used with it. As proof of the durability of
the wood I have seen some posts which were . split
from it and put down 30 years ago. They are still
standing, and show little sign of decay.
“Barcoo” : Being on the Leichhardt River
(Q-) "hen a mob of travelling cows was put on
camp, I was initiated into an act that was new to
me. Eight or 10 animals were cut out, thrown and
hobbled by the innd legs. The bloke-in-charge
explained that these cows had dropped on the
journey calves which had to be killed, and the
hobbling was to prevent the mothers meandering
back at night to look for a lost child. The hind-
leg hobbling safely anchored them and saved many
a weary mile’s ride in the morning.
*•“('. J.”: About one farmer in live gives
his cattle sufficient salt. The local storekeeper
threatened reprisals when I asked him to haul out
2c-wt. on each of his fortnightly trips, but between
us we’ve been teaching the surrounding cockies some-
thing, and the storekeeper has since offered to make
me a present of his grandmother. The daily allow-
ance for stall-fed cattle is four ounces each. As
mine never see a stall I give them one ounce each.
I have known cattle craving for salt to eat anything,
even old iron. One fourdegged lady of my acquaint-
ance puts in part of her time daily li king a rusty
fence.

BY MIXTA GONG CREEK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
1 rom mountain heights hoary the waterfall dashes,
And down on the foam-crested granite it smashes,
Swift under the driftwood. Swift back from the
boulder
It pelts a white spray on the silver!op's shoulder;
I hen, coursing serene where the ferntrees a-spread
(■ive shade to the dear, dainty maidenhair bed,
It rolls o’er the pebbles. It purls o’er the sands
Beside the clear pool where the wayfarer stands
With eyes to the east as the first crimson streak
Of morning comes gilding the Mittagong Creek.
Louder than thunder it lets loose a song
1 n wild, rugged haunts where the cockatoos throng ;
An anthem majestic o’er Bushland it flings
" here, proud o’er his eyrie, the eaglehawk wings ;
'1 hen soft o’er the white beach it ripples along,
A song in its trill like a butcher-bird’s song ;
And laden with plunder of wattle,perfume
And tribute from gardens with rose-beds a bloom,
With pillage of valley and spoil of the peak,
The dawn-winds are playing by Mittagong Creek.
The song of the bell-bird rings clear from the pool,
M here shades of the lightwood fall pleasant and
cool;
Between sky and water the perch leaps agleam,
And groups of black bream are at play in the stream.
Though heavy the hand that the drought fiend has
laid
On homestead and flock of the plains where I strayed,
1 he billows of green on the uplands to-day,
Are sweet in the eyes of the pilgrim from Hay
Who drives in his tent-pegs in sight of the Peak,
His walkabout ended by Mittagong Creek.
N.B.W.

“Bad Australian”: In some of the out-
back S. American mining districts the methods of
working are still about as primitive as when Manco
Capac started out from Titicaca with the golden
wedge which located the holy city of Cuzco. Speak-
ing more particularly of the copper country, in
many places you find a little crowd bandicooting the
better-grade ore, and reducing it in the old Inca
“horno,” a concern something like the burnt-out
white-ants’ nest in which I used to bake my damper
and ceratodus steak (yes, steak) and fresh-water
mullet in the bad old days on the Upper Burnett
(Q* ), what time the dingo howled around my humble
dwelling and the flying-foxes squawked and
hummed in the leafy bloodwood overhead.
The horno is usually run under difficulties
as to fuel, the only combustibles on hand
being dry llama dung and a curious vegetable called
jaretta. Jaretta looks exactly like a gigantic billy-
button, growing out of the ground without a stalk.
It has the pungent, aromatic smell of a Chow joss-
house, and spilits like a free mountain ash. There
is, or was, a vegetable of very similar appearance
to be found on the tableland between Mole Creek
and the Great Lake (Tas.). Jaretta sometimes attains
a diameter of upwards of two meters, and contains
more resin than any other vegetable I have bumped
against. After the first fierce ebullition it burns
exactly like white box or red-gum bark. The slag-
heaps from the liornos, both ancient and modern,
being in every case the refuse from 2.5 p.c. to 40 p.c.
ore, contain very high values of metal. The bandi-
cooters, like the old Inca, always sink on the under-
lay, and their lifting-gear consists of a series of
poles of the giant cactus fished- together and laid on
the footwall with cleats across at intervals. The
bunch generally hire, beat or bluff some Indian labor
into their service, and on a fine May morning, when
the poor Indian is painfully shinning up the impro-
vised fowl-escape with a greenhide bag of ore on his
shoulders, remarking, “Eaigh-eaigh” at every step,
it is brought home vividly to the onlooker how these
unthinkable, half-breed Dagoes tortured the Indian
away back in the uncivilised days before the good
and holy institution of “grub-staking” was invented.
* * * *
Somewhere in Australia at this moment
a wearisome person all the way from U.S.A. is trudg-
ing along with a yarn about having, for a huge wager,
set out to walk round the earth and accumulate a large
quantity of. dallars by the sweat of his chin. A
queer push these boot-tourists, and their tales are of
infinite variety. The latest of the tribe has a pictur-
esque story about terrible experiences in the Orinoco
country (South America), where vampires seized on
him, it “being a peculiarity of these creatures that
they make two punctures in the big toe, through
which they suck the blood.” Why the gentleman
didn’t keep his boots on is not explained. The
Orinoco vampire is, however, a relatively harmless
animal compared with his brothers whom the travel-
ler will meet when he gets to Melbourne. The
peculiarity of these brutes is that they make 15
holes in their victim’s pocket, and by the time,
gorged like leeches, they abandon the unhappy
wretch he couldn’t walk along- Bourke-street without
being run in for having no lawful visible means.
The explorer now on the Australian landscape re-
ports that in the West he walked three stages of 240,
252 and 273 miles respectively without seeing any
water except what he carried. So he seems either a
mighty near relation of the camel or a heavyweight
carrier of some circumstance. Possibly he learned
a trick from the Orinoco vampire, and lived on his
own big toe.

“Outcast”: The paper bark ti-tree men-
tioned by “Vigilans” (B. 24/6/’l5) is also well
known in Papua, especially west of the Fly River.
It grows plentifully in the low open country border-
ing along the Paho, Oriomo, Morehead and Bensback
rivers, and attains considerable girth. The country
in this district is extremely like the northern part of
Australia, dried up in summer and a swamp in the
rains. All the tribes west of the Paho use the paper
bark for house-roofing, and also for making women’s
petticoats. The wood is very durable; and, as far
; I have used it, I have found it resists white ants.
Moreover, to a very great extent it'defies the cobra
worm ( Teredo nrnalis). There’s any quantity of the
timber in the west of Papua, and it’s a wonder some-
body has not taken it up for milling, especially as
most of the rivers are navigable.
A GENTLE HINT.
BILL: "Yer dorg 's bloomin’ thin, Jachy. Looks as if yer starve ’im.”
JACKY ( hinting ): “No blurry pfear, Boss. That pfeller gettem tueker every time I
gettem bacca.”
“ Warradulla”: I used to think that
“Barcoo” knew everything or even more, but I was
wrong. He asserted (B. 19/8/’l5). that the charges
of cruelty laid against the Afghan are over-done,
and intimated that the Abo. who said he saw ’Ghans
splicing goat-skin into a broken patch on a camel’s
back “is verging on lunacy.” Now, I have lived
for over eight years in Broken Hill, almost next door
to a camel camp, and have seen Abdullah “dealing”
it to his ships of the desert scores of times. When
loading up and the beast has commenced to rise
prior to the burden being securely roped, I have seen
the owner rush at and fell the poor brute with a
sapling. And when the long strings of dromedaries
lope in from the distant stations Outback with their
swaying burdens of wool, I have seen some with
almost raw backs and others with blood squashing
out from under the pack-saddle. On one occasion I
saw a piece of old boot sewn with twine across a
hole in a camel’s rump, and maggots were dropping
out from behind the leather. That particularly un-
fortunate beast had lumbered beneath four great
bales of wool from away beyond Tibooburra. If all
this is not shocking cruelty—well, then I’m insane.
“Peri Winkle”: Further attention has
been given to that quaint water insect that “Risque”
(B. 12/8/’l5) reported from northern Victorian irri-
gation country, and as to whose iijnocuous habits
doubts were expressed. Specimens were sent to
Jas. A. Kershaw, director of the National Museum,
Melbourne, and he reports that the water insects are
small, perfectly harmless crustaceans, often termed
“fresh-water shrimps,” which occur at times in large
numbers in shallow water, such as ponds and pools
formed during the wet season. They always swim
back-do wnwards, and feed upon minute organic
matter. Large numbers of eggs are produced, which,
when the pools dry up in summer, remain quiescent
in the mud, and immediately hatch out when rain
oomt i. “Risque” and his settler-friends will have
to blame something else for the mysterious loss of
stock. The so-called “fresh-water shrimps” with the
blue shell must not be confounded with the water-
s’ sects of the rivers that we bait our hooks with to
Lird cod and his relations. They are quite different,'
rc.-mbling more a limpet with a tail like a scorpion.
* * * *
“Poon Boon”: The controversy or dog-
fight as to how marsupials reproduce themselves has
been again aroused by a bush naturalist, whq re-
cently discovered in the pouch of a wallaby a young-
ster the size of a grain of No. 16 shot. The speci-
men has been forwarded to the Australian Museum
for examination. The bushmen’s belief that the
young are born on the teat seems strengthened by
this discovery. The scientists’ contention is that the
young are born in the ordinary way, and, after
birth, are placed on the teat by the mother. Well, if
she can handle a mite as infinitesimal as a grain of
No. 16 shot, she should be an adept at extracting
splinters.
“McNortli”: Last year an epidemic .or
something in that line happened to the citizens of
the deep along the western shores of the Gulf of
Carpentaria. From Biowen Strait (N.T.) to a point
400 miles down the Gulf millions of fish of all kinds,
sharks, dugongs and trepang died suddenly and were
washed up on the beaches. The weather was fine,
and there was nothing to indicate the cause of
the sudden rise in the death-rate. Fish in various
stages of deadness were strewn in some places knee-
deep. Trepang-fishers and others were unable to
anchor their vessels close to the shore on account of
the smell, and for months afterwards live
was practically non-existent. The niggers of Coburg
Peninsula told me that many snakes, lizards and
other cold-blooded creatures also died suddenly at
the same time as the fishes. Anybody got an ex-
planation?

“Private 445” : “Fiinderino” (B. 10/6/T5),
alluding to the occasional outbursts of cannibalism
amongst boo-book owls, mentions their slaughter of
young birds. The laughing-jack has to plead
guilty to a similar charge. Out shooting a couple
of seasons ago, and hearing the squawking of a
young bird in a dead gum some distance away, I
hurried up in time to see (and hear) the whack
against a limb with which a Johnny stopped the
wails of a half-fledged starling. Stiil holding the
remains in his beak the murderer glided slowly to
another tree and entered a hollow, from which issued
an uproarious welcome.from the missus and kids.
“Billy T.”: All the bull-makers are not
Irish. It was a young Australian of Cockney descent
who explained to me the other day, “Mother isn’t in
—she’s getting ready to go out.” And it was one of
the native-born, and bush-bred, who in reply to a
question about the size of Wright’s place replied:
“My oath! It’s so big he don’t reach home till
half an hour after he gets there.” Ho was a Ballarat-
born Australian, too, with no Irish in his pedigree,
who explained when I went to examine the teeth of
the horse we were talking about: “No good lookin’
at them upper teeth. They ain’t there.”

“J.R.K.”: Messmate or bastard stringy
are fair fuel, but I’ll defy “Pick” (B. 29/8/’l5) to
burn the real stringy unless lie drops it down the
chimney of the Devil's restaurant. In the Mt.
Garnet (Q.) district, where thousands of tons of
timber were burnt for charcoal, stringy is the only
thing left standing by the Italians who supplied the
Mt. Garnet Co. The only practical user of it is
Binghi, either io smoke a bandicoot out or to pass
the time o’ day to his mother-in-law.

A party named Pullman (Bi
26/8/’l5) wants to know the meaning of the native
word “Kooyong,” I beg to refer him to “Cole’s
Family Almanac,” page 145, where he will learn
that “Cooyong,” a word said to have been used by
certain Victorian tribes, means “bandy-coot.” It is
allegedly taken from vocabularies assembled by that
earnest curio-chaser, A. C. MacDonald; but the
“bandy-coot’ part of it hardly reads like Mac-
Donald.
“S. S. Severn”; Tbe Northern Territory
has its “hatters.” One of him put into Pine Creek
not so long since driving’ a donkey at the end
of a pair of buggy reins. “Where to?” drawled Pinq
Creek. “Somewheres.” “Where from?” “TIT
Barrier.” Two thousand miles of sand and stones
and his own company ! He pushed off at daybreak
heading for God-knows-where.

“Devilfish”: To fishermen only. Some
years ago the kingfish was as common in Tasmanian
waters as the barracouta is now. Kingfish then was
used as manure. Line fishermen have been known
to catch 45 dozen of the sea animal, averaging 121 b!
or 141 b. a-piece. That was in the good old days;
Now you’d hardly know a kingfish if you met him in
Tassy and he asked you to come in and have a wet.
* * * *
“Alamon” : Judging by Abo-liars’ reports,
the dingos of the southern States differ remarkably
from those of the N.T., since to kill the N.T. species
is a very simple matter. We lay a poison-bait along
one of the tracks that the dog is known to pass and
place a tin of water close by. Next sunrise gener-
ally illuminates a very dead dingo. Strychnine
is the usual poison, being quickest in action. In
laying the bait no endeavor is made to get rid of
the man-taint. Dingos have taken baits that had been
carried in a box in constant use as a seat.
They do not, however, always pick up baits that are
laid in the bush. A N.T. dingo usually comes and
goes along the one track, especially when foraging
around farms, etc., and baits decorating that track
are invariably taken._ None the’ less, the brutes
show a diabolical ingenuity in avoiding traps of
every description.

“Mulga”: The scrub tick mentioned in a
recent Bulletin by “Zamia” is responsible for some
awful Australian slanguage, but as a curse-producing
agency it is not in the same catalogue as the green
caterpillars which do business in the brigalow scrub.
A few minutes after this pest gets to you a rash
breaks out over the body and the interior feels as if
it were on fire. Such itching and pain couldn’t be
produced by a whole paddockful of stinging nettles.
The sensations last in varying degree for two or
three days. Perhaps somebody lias- a remedy.
SCARCE FISH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He sought my humble door to-day,
His poor, supply of fish to, sell;
Up through his hat were seen to stray
His auburn locks; he had a smell
Suggestive of a factory wing
For double-salting British ling.
He asked for whiting ninepence each,
And “Garfish four a bob,’’ he said ;
Whereat the wife began to preach
And heap derision on his head.
She thought he was a robber bold,
And chided: “Am I made of gold!”
He smiled a sad, sweet smile, and sighed:
“Yes, lady, fish t&..scaic&! I swear
All up and down the bay I’ve tried,
And cannot catch ’em anywhere.
There’s few that’s left, -and-Aha't’s the truth —-
It’s all along, mum, of the drouth !”
VICTORIA.

“Hamer”: Australia is making many dis-
coveries about itself. The big upheaval lias
created a demand for dyes—especially khaki
dyes—and the German output being cut off
the supply is short and precarious. Under
that spur it has been discovered that myti-
colorin, which exactly fills the bill for khaki,
can be obtained from the leaves of the red
stringy-bark. It looks as if there is an
opening for another fair-sized industry—if
Private Enterprise has any enterprise.
