“Chemicus”: Is not “Tenaculum” (P».
2/9/T5) a trifle late with his complaint re-
garding the British mode of prescribing
medicines V The 1914 British Pharmacopeia,
which came into force in Victoria on Sept. 1,
1915, uses for all its preparations the
gramme and millilitre (practically a cubic
centimetre). Already a few doctors are
dashing off their scrips on the metric decimal
system. No doubt the older generation of
doctors and chemists will cling as long as
possible to the old system of grains, minims
and ounces, but the new generation will be
educated on the metric system alone. The
next edition of the “8.P.” will undoubtedly
contain metric weights and measures only.
The only reference to Imperial measure in
the 1914 edition is the dosages of the various
preparations: these are given in both
systems.

“Risque” : Vic. Police Commissioner Sains-
bury’s back-to-the-blackfellow style of deliv-
ering police missives from hand to hand is
only in keeping with things. For the first
time in 45 years I have had to eat brown
sugar, and the missus has had to rake out
the old candle-mould and cadge some tallow.
Also we have eaten honey in the comb as a
luxury; the first crop of nettles after the rain
made up the first dish of greens for many a
day. Birds have gone towards the menu in
the absence of rabbit and sheep. Mush-
rooms have been a godsend, and pumpkins
left over in the paddocks from last summer
carried many families through the winter.
Why, Sainsbury is a mere amateur in
economy compared with some of us where
the drought was.
* -X- * *
“Hermes”: When the war is over
.7. Bull ought to present the Stan-
dard Oil Trust with a souvenir, for it did
a great deal to save Britain. True, what it
did was quite accidental —an awful amount
of the good done in this world is accidental.
In this matter the Trust charged such a Ned-
Kelly price for motor spirit that Bull
angrily took to making benzole from his coal
deposits. Now, one of the by-products of
benzole is tri-nitro-toluene, stuff which is
necessary for the making of high-explosive
shells. Hitherto, all the tri-nitro-toluene
Bull used he had bought from Germany, and
when the war broke out he found himself in
a hole. He could not get the article from
Germany, and U.S.A. did not make it. Bull’s
own manufacture was enough to keep a Boor
war or a mere local disturbance of the sort
going, but this flare-up was a different propo-
sition. In one week around La Bassee the
British artillery fired more shells than it did
all through the Boer war, and anything like
a sustained advance was only possible if the
British artillery could, as Ben Tillett said,
“rain shells for 40 days and 40 nights.” For-
tunately, our father Bull makes a good deal of
the benzole he uses, and when he started to
make tri-nitro-toluene in a hurry he had not
to start altogether at the bedrock. Incident-
ally, there is a chance hereabouts for Austra-
lia to help the Empire considerably. There is
more than a suspicion that coal from the Mait-
land district (N.S.W.) is particularly rich in
t.-n.-t. It is up to either the State or the
Australian Government, or both, to investi-
gate, and, if the charge is proved, to. start the
manufacture of the shell-food and ship it with
all speed to where it will do the most good.
* * * *
“W.C.M.” : I am continually handling mer-
chants’ catalogues, American and Australian,
and I notice the difference. The American
brands himself “Manufacturer and Ex-
porter” ; the Australian, “Merchant and Im-
porter.” While the American invariably
talks of the number of hands he employs
and the size of his works, the Australian
carefully conceals the fact that he employs
only an office boy and a typist.
“Berowra”: “Boronia” (B. 19/S/’l5)
touches on a subject of interest to all Aus-
tralians who consider our woods equal to
any. I cannot back any other timber to
shrink and twist more than jarrah, but I
would point out that all our native hard-
woods have the tendency to “give” when,
even after years of seasoning, a new face
is put on them. The glory of blackwood
does not appeal to the better class of cabinet-
makers on account of these shrinking quali-
ties. A matter that should be sifted is
whether our timbers are felled at the proper
season. “Cut when the sap is down” has
always been the rule, but we apparently do
not adopt it, as it is much easier to work
the forests in summer. Also, scientific study
should bo made as to when our hardwood
saps are down. Old-world timbers show no
inclination to “move” each time they are re-
faced ; why. then, should Australian timbers,
if they are handled properly?
“Masarcus”: They’re after the Ford
Motor Co. in the land of the wooden nut-
meg. and a press campaign is in full scorch.
This sort of piffle: “Why is a Ford like a
rich woman’s baby?” “Because it has a
new rattle every day.” The eel behind the
rock is that the Ford co. is a near approach
to wholehog Sosh. No employee is paid less
than five dollars a day, and a pyramid of
the nrofits is whacked up amongst all hands.
Fellow-manufacturers have for Ford the
same benevolent feelings that the unionist
has for the scab. He’s a puzzle to them:
they can’t make out for sure whether ho is
a crank or a longer-headed business man
than all the rest of them. Considering the
terms he offers compared with what they
offer, they are sure he’s a crank (he
mixes some religion with it) ; and when
they look at the money he is making some
of them reckon lie’s merely on to the best
advertising scheme of his day.

“Warre,”. after going through the wheat
belt.: —-
Though the N.S.W. Government is out again with
the same old tale of making preparations for the re-
moval of wheat from country stations to the sea-
board, wayback bookmakers are offering a quid to a
quondong that the same old breakdown will arrive at
the same old address, and hundreds of thousands of
bags of wheat will have to be dumped just anywhere.
A record yield is a practical certainty, and it’s up to
someone to find out from the Minister of Railsvays
just how many locomotives are out of action, and
why. Nothing so far has been done at country
stations to meet the inevitable rush of grain just
after the New Year, the stacking sites are quite
inadequate, and there is nothing like sufficient dun-
nage to meet requirements.
* * * *
“Ritasun” : Hundreds of bundles offruit-
treelings have been coming into the irriga-
tion country of Northern Victoria, and there
is a golden promise that the Australian
housewife will soon no longer have to look
upon dried fruits as a luxury. At Is. 4d.
per lb. these goods are twice too dear, and
the “corner” in the Mildura and Renmark
output is doomed. Thousands of citrus
fruit trees are already in bearing—slightly
only, since the strength of the trees is being
wisely nursed —and the question already
arises as to where all the Washington Navel
oranges will go when the full harvest is
ready.
“W.C.M.”: About co-operative flour-mill-
ing (B. 19/8/M5). A tremendous amount of
prejudice will have to be overcome if the
wheat cockv is built on the same lines as his
brother who minds the sheep. A few years
ago, in a sheep district, I found that each
farmer shore his own little lot, baled his
wool, and had it sold separately. I preached
co-operation—one large shed, one lot of cart-
age, one big lot sold in place of a dozen small
lots. I spent a good deal of time over the
scheme, and every farmer could see its
advantages. But they remembered. Some
years before there had been a scheme for a co-
operative freezing works. It wasn’t a success,
because each shareholder wanted to get Svd-
ney prices in the country. So the concern
closed down, and each man lost a tenner or
so. That hurt, and co-operation has been
shied at ever since. The cocky can’t see fur-
ther ahead than yesterday, and sometimes it
is tlie day before.
"P.X.”: Writing about the Post Office
The Bulletin said the other day that pub-
lic enterprises wouldn’t run themselves any
more than private enterprises. In a small
way Bundaberg has just provided another
example. Private Enterprise having proved
a cormorant in the local meat trade, a Citi-
zens Defence Committee was formed to buy
carcases and sell them. After trading tor
six weeks with apparent success this com-
mittee transferred the business to the Bunda-
berg Co-operative Butchering Co., Ltd.
Now. after about 10 months, this co. finds
that it has used up all its capital and has
to go into liquidation. When it was too
late an auditor was called in, and lie found
that instead of making a profit the Defence
Committee had actually made a loss of !•>
a week. Subsequently things went on m the
same old slipshod way, and nobody seems to
have known that the concern was rushing to
the Devil until it had actually got there.

Sydney Banco Court heard another dis-
pute about onions the other day, the quarrel
concerning a consignment shipped from
Japan in a German boat. The transaction
was interrupted bv the war, and the cargo
had to be landed and stored in Japan until
another ship could be found to briiv it
along. The vegetables were alleged by the
defendants to have arrived “unfit for human
consumption”—in fact, “quite unfit tor
human consumption.” They ought to have
been good enough to bring tears to the eyes
of patriotic Australians, anyhow.
The promoters of a “patriotic” Art Lnion
in Adelaide have just played a grim joke on
the subscribers. The lucky holder _of the
first-prize ticket received a large Marie Tuck
painting, valued at a complimentary figure.
The poor unfortunate who took second place
had to be content with a mere Argyle motor-

NEAR BEER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne publicans are asking for a new drink
to serve after hours. They want something “near
beer” —something that will meet the need of their
ordinary customers, and yet be in strict accord with
the new Licensing Act.
There is a feverish demand
For some good, simple drink to-day;
'Tis felt throughout the thirsting land.
Oh, have you such a liquor, say—
A soothing cup that maketh gay
The heart of man, and gives him cheer;
A drink that is, and isn’t, beer?
A booze we want possessing nip—■
A new, exalted, temp’ranee swipe
That passes o’er the eager lip
Like velvet rum to make us ripe.
Yet keeps us from the Lemon’s gripe;
A drink for dinner, supper, lunch,
Which, though teetotal, has a punch.
So near to beer it ought to be
That it will warm the bosom’s core
And not disgrace the devotee.
The three big moons that shone of yore
’Twill to all merry men restore,
While they roll home with roaring song,
A sedulously sober throng.
We want a drink with which, in fact,
We can absorb the spirit quite,
Yet keep the letter of the- Act —
A lotion that is Itechabite,
Yet prone to make us think we’re tight
When we are not; preserving, hence,
A saving legal difference.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.
THE GAMBLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The gamble that we christen Life
Plays many a curious prank.
Thus of it spoke a careworn man
Of aspect drear and dank:
“Our life is but a lottery,
And I have drawn a blank.”
But there’s another man I know,
Who languishes for naught,
In whom this tale of discontent
Impatient anger wrought:
“The prizes that the fool would win
Are scarcely worth a thought !”
But I am in this gamble, too,
And neither fat nor thin:
I never think that I have lost.
But stay, with cheerful grin.
To gamble for the gamble's sake
Without a hope to win.
x.s.w.

“Blanky” : The Egyptian coinage is
guaranteed to box the Australian for a
while although it is so simple. There are
10 millimes to a piastre, and one hundred
piastres to the Egyptian pound. A piastre
is worth 2-id., and is a nickel coin about
the size of ~ a bob or deener, and the half
piastre is a little smaller. Then the two-
piastre is silver, about as big as sixpence,
The next is five, then 10, then 20. The
twenty is like the old ss. piece, It takes a
while to remember that a piastre is 2*d.
I not . ld - and when you do remember
• to rea J. lse that t this _ being a col-
s country, living is naturally
< ei here than in Australia. As a set-off
lkn 7 !'V”- l V l „ dlsa | te J- w 7 ch IS , Austra ;
t' i-n 01 •F l^nf ie ’-ri a ® d tbe natives have got
to know it. The English pound is worth 97*
disasters and the Egyptian pound counts out
, s * Cano Bom the camp
• tram costs a disaster or two n you are
caught there without a leave pass.
m . . ~ . , „
1 raining as it is done now m Egypt:
Our camp is a well-arranged turn-out. We have
a lar «e wooden mess-room for each company, so we
don t have to eat our meals in the tents as we did
m Australia. Each battalion has a wet canteen, a
dry canteen, tobacconist and barber. We do all our
drill from 6 till «o a.m. and from 5 till 7 p.m., and
we have a lecture in the mess-room from 11 a.m.
to 12.15 p.m., so we are not worked during the hot
part of the day. The parade ground is a desert.
Bill this desert isn't all sand. There’s nldre gravel
and dust than anything else, and ambling over it is
no joke; but taking everything into consideiation
we’re not badly treated, and are getting well trained
without being overworked. We’ve got out of the
clothes we landed in. Our everyday dress is boots,
puttees, shorts, light shirt and felt hat. Shorts are
pants reaching a little above the knees, and are
made of light khaki drill. There are pith helmets
ready for us, and soon we will all be wearing them
instead of felt hats.
“E.N.M.”: Some weeks ago, between Ben-
rith and Emu Plains (N.S.W.), 1 counted
n i lle men putting up a. small telegraph-post.
It took them the best part of two “work-
ing” days. Now, in the Brisbane Courier,
an indignant taxpayer writes concerning
another telegraph-post—this time outside
hN house His storv •
Six nieri> ' two horses, * a'dray and a wheelbarrow
were recently engaged in replacing a telephone-post
(two wires) with an inferior one. I know of no
reason for the substitution. The time occupied was
over two days, and in the aggregate 104 hours, and
the expense, at Is. per hour, £5 4s. Two men
could easily do the job in half an hotir.
Yet the Postal Department faces half-mil-
lion deficits not only without If'.tremor, but
without murmuring an apology. Is it any
wonder that, with leeches dragging at its
veins, such as Cockatoo Dock. Canberra
and the awful Postal Department, the
young Commonwealth is becoming finan-
ciallv anaemic?
“Corporal Slump”: The rifle clubs are one
of the few humorous features of the war. Being
a married man with a flock of young children
1 enrolled myself amongst the. stay-home
heroes shortly after the outbreak of trouble.
One of the first things that struck me about
the parades was the number of hale, single
young men who were standing shoulder to
shoulder with us broken-winded suburbanites
and ignoring the call for recruits. However,
most of these young men gradually became
aware of the national clamor for real work-
ing soldiers and faded away to camp amid
applause from the club, which cheerfully took
credit to itself for discovering a few more
expeditionaries for the Front, whereas all it
had really done was to provide several young
men with a harbor of refuge for a few weeks.
After the clearance, some too-enthusiastie
fusilier started to worry his elubmates into a
uniform which cost 255. complete with hat,
and looked shocking. One or two objected to
the slopshop-sweating indicated by the low
price, and a heap more stormed against the
utter shapelessness of the outfit. Just about
then the membership rot set in, and the bi-
weekly roll-cqll became a sad function. The
position at present is that the young shirkers
(who now regard the refuge as useless,
and are determined to shirk to the end) can-
not resign, and the clubs haven’t the power to
throw them out. No doubt the regulations
will be amended in time and the moral wreck-
age pushed off the rolls; but for the present
the wheezing veterans on parade have to
throw out their stomachs and listen indig-
nantly while the corporal under the lamp
yelps the hated names of the wilful missing.
“E.N.M.”: Perhaps it is the enervating
Northern climate, but even if it is only the
Northern beer the fact remains that the
Light Horse camp at Chermside (Q.) can
show, relatively, an extraordinary number
of big fellows in khaki. It is ridiculous to
call these sons of Anak “Light Horse.”
Some of them run up to 6ft. 4in. and 6ft.
Sin. in their socks, and ride at 15 or 16
stone. Why don’t the Defence authorities
draft out these giants and form the Aus-
tralian Lifeguards? A couple of thousand
picked men and horses would help to para-
lyse the gentle Uhlans in the European
spring of 31)16. And how they would make
the name of Australia ring!
Brisbane Bung, who fought several Home
Secretaries bitterly for years on the
Sunday-trading question, is now the strict-
est of Sabbatarian observers. Indeed, the
Brisbane police report that a Sunday sl.v-
grog traffic has sprung into existence ns a
result of Boniface’s bigoted objection to wet
even a legitimate pewter on Sunuay, and
they begin to wish he wouldn’t be so par-
ticular.


THE FOLEY OF TAKING DIGESTIVE
PILLS.
A WARNING TO DYSPEPTICS.
The habit of taking digestive pills after
meals makes chronic? dyspeptics of many
thousands of men and women because arti-
ficial digestants, drugs and medicines have
practically no influence upon the excessively
acid condition of the stomach contents which
alone is the cause of most forms of indiges-
tion and dyspepsia. The after-dinner pill
merely lessens the sensitiveness of the
stomach nerves, and thus gives a false sense
of freedom from pain, whereas if those who
are subject to flatulence, heartburn, wind,
etc., were to obtain a little pure bis lira ted
magnesia from the chemist and take half a
teaspoonful in a little water after meals
there would be no necessity for drugs or medi-
cines because Msurated magnesia instantly
neutralises acidity, stops food fermentation,
and thus ensures normal painless digestion
by enabling the stomach to do its work with-
out hindrance.*

THE SOFT ANSWER.
REJECTED CANDIDATE: “Well, by heavens,
I'll never allow myself to be nominated
again without first submitting myself to
two brain specialists.”
BRUTAL FRIEND: “But why delay?”
everything will be all right. Haven’t Massey
and Ward put a 50 per cent, tax on German
goods to make the doddering Dominion self-
reliant? They might as well have put a
mustard plaster on the local volcano.
"Harrison O.” : Imagine my astonishment:
•he other day when, drowsing over the
Argus, I came across the following:—
The present discontent in the ranks of the State
Liberal party is clue chiefly to the irrational and anti-
quated method bv which the Cabinet is constituted.
The idea of entrusting to one man—the leader of the
party-—the task of deciding who shall conduct tlie
business of the country, and denying- to the party as
a whole any voice in the matter, is essentially un-
democratic in theory and has never been justified in
practice. Tlie reasonable method is for the party to
meet in conference and select from among its mem-
bers those whom it considers best fitted and most
entitled to Ministerial rank. The time is ripe for
tlie abandonment of the system at present in vogue,
which places too much power in the hands of a single
individual, and lias the effect of causing dissatisfac-
tion amongst the rank and file of the party.
Upon looking again I find that the above
were not the Argus’s exact words. Still
•hey are near enough. What the old lady
actually did say was: —
There is a strong feeling in the party that the
best method of establishing a Ministry which will
possess the confidence of the party is for the party to
choose the members who are to administer the vari-
ous departments. Such a method, although not in
accordance with Parliamentary traditions, would at
any rate, etc., etc. Neither is it a violation of the
party system of government, because Ministers would
he chosen only by and from members of the penlomi-
nant party in the House.
Now that tlie Argus has bestowed her bless-
ing upon the caucus system, it is no doubt
only a matter of time before she will demand
that no non-unionist shall be allowed to
hold office, and that any measure thrown.out
by the Legislative Council shall at once
automatically become law.
ENLIGHTENMENT.
HIM: "Gad, that Census card's got me beat!
What else coaid a fellow do, the johnny
wants to know.”
HER: “Well, you could say that you might
get work, couldn’t you?”
“No compulsion!” Professor Ronald
Mclntyre, of the Sydney Scotclibyterians,
cackles. "Shall it be said that we can’t
defend our country of our own free will?”
Shall it lie said that we had to make a tax
to pay our war debts’? Shall it be said
that, when our country wanted money, it
had to compel people to pay? Away with
such nonsense ! Let Andrew Fisher merely
announce that the Treasury will oe open
from 10 a.m. to 3 p.fh. on Mondays and
Thursdays, and all this stupid system ox
postage-stamps, Customs Houses, land and
income taxes, etc., can safely be swept away.
The dreadful result of bringing the parson
up on a collection plate seems to be that he
thinks everybody else ought to depend on
the same deplorable system of cadge.
“E.N.M.” : Where are we going? To say
that butter shall be sold at Is. sd. per lb.
when it is costing Is. 9d. to produce, and
when there are thousands of people able and
willing and even anxious to pay 2s. Gd. for
it, is a folly so stupendous that only a corpse
couldn’t see it. The making of a law or
regulation to achieve this maniacal scheme
proves that the legislators responsible are
either the prize fools of civilisation or the
most dishonest gang of political frauds ever
spawned by the devil of Party. Such a law
—and the same thing applies to wheat or
milk or meat or anything else—is a direct
incitement to the public to co-operate
promptly in breaking it. And accordingly
broken it is. amid the enthusiastic silence of
all concerned, and the Law passes further
into contempt. Which is bad for the Law
and worse for the people, since disrespect for
Law means in the end defiance of authority
—and anarchy lies along that road.
TURKEY’S “S.O.S.”
“Somewhere a voice is calling.”
To the German mind the position in Eng-
land must be beyond all understanding. The
Trade Union Congress resolved that “Prus-
sian militarism must be destroyed” ; also it
recorded “hearty appreciation of the magni-
ficent response to the call for volunteers to
light Prussian militarism.” Yet, while
enthusiastically in favor of voluntary mili-
tary effort, it unanimously carried a reso-
lution against eonscriptive effort to do the
thing that it is so much in favor or naving
done. What the trade-union worker wants
is freedom—freedom to encourage the bolder
to go and do what ought to be done, while
the other sort stays at home and gets the
benefit of higher wages. In industrial mat-
ters they call this sort “scabs” and have a
contempt for him. But in this national
matter they are prepared to go out on strike
for tlie l ight of every man to be a scab.”
On the principle of rectifying a quarrel
with Brown by hitting Smith, the night-
shift miners in several South Coast
(N.S.W.) collieries struck work recently—
and won. The grievance was that the
miners, many of whom live at Wollongong,
left work at 10.30 pan., but the railways
only provided a leisurely train which fre-
quently did not get them home till 3 a.m.
They had, they alleged, no means of shaking-
up Brown, the Railway Commissioner, so
they took it out of Smith, the mine-
owner, by refusing to work. And" Smith,
having influence with Brown, promptly
got the grievance redressed; the Commis-
sioners announced, almost immediately, that
a suitable train would be run for the miners’
benefit. The incident serves to show that
the idea of hitting Smith for Brown's
offence is not a had one in a partly-civilised
community. In a wholly-civilised commu-
nity the idea would of course he prepos-
terous. And the idea of knocking off work
to get a little grievance like this put right
ought to be more preposterous than ever
with the Government in the hands of the
workers.
Having undertaken to spend £400.000 in
making that picturesque ditch, the Tamar,
navigable for ships of the Ceramic class or
thereabouts, the Launceston (Tas.) Marine
Board now finds itself in a financial moss,
and has asked the State Government to give
it £lOO.OOO towards what it calls a national
work. The Government is in very low
water itself these days, but it is going to ask
Parliament to put up £40,000 to clear the
rocks away from the mouth of the ditch, on
condition that the Marine Board drops as
much of its scheme as it can. and goes slow
on the rest.
The power of the Federal Parliament to
insist upon fair labor conditions in return
for the payment of bounty for the encourage-
ment of an industry is to be tested. Doubt
as to the existence -of the power having-
arisen in consequence of a judgment of the
Full Court in Rex. v. Barger, Minister for
Customs Tudor asked Justice Higgins in the
Arbitration Court to say what wages and
conditions were reasonable to be paid by
Hoskins, Ltd., who had claimed a bonus
under the Iron Bounty Act. Higgins for-
mally refused to make a decision, remarking
that, though lie had dissented from the
judgment in the Barger matter, he was
hound by it. So the matter goes to the Full
Court; and if the Full Court says the Com-
monwealth hasn’t the power to fix wages,
labor conditions, etc., in bonused industries,
then the principle of bonuses must go over-
hoard—-until the Constitution is altered. A
Constitution which strikes at the very root
of what is called the New Protection, but is
really The Bulletin’s old Protection, would
bo no good for-the year 1915.
By the way members are talking it look.t
a sure thing that the .Maori la ml picture show
and theatre ticket will have to bear a war
tax. The man who is getting fat on the
unearned increment of land values cordially
approves of this. Any rotten old port in a
storm, so far as he is concerned.
While the N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor
party was writhing in agony over the Minis-
terial dole of £40.(100 to the Great Cobar Co.,
Speaker "Dick" Meagher entered his protest
—that “the deal savored of giving assistance
to Private ■ Enterprise.” It must have been
a moving moment.

The steamer Clan McTavish lias arrived with 4000

An extra service has been inaugurated with Japan
and the first of the steamers arrived in Sydney with

comprising cement, salmon, sulphur, cane-ware,
silk, etc.
In adjoining columns were details about
(11 thousands of Newcastle miners threaten-
ing a strike; (2) a project for spending
public money to buy out landlords in order
.that work may be “available” for returned
soldiers; (3) the spectre of unemployment;
(4) loans to relieve threatened distress. All
of which moves The Bulletin to propose
three cheers on general principles and that
everybody take a day off.
Whilst Lloyd George last week was ex-
horting Britain to realise the necessity for
national service and show “grip, resolu-
tion and prompt readiness in every sphere.”
Prime Minister Asquith was assuring the
Commons that “the matter of compulsion
had not escaped the attention of the Gov-
ernment.” When the Zeppelins begin to
drop bombs around Westminster, Asquilh
will arise and say: “Hr. Speaker, the atten-
tion of the Government having been drawn
to the fact that hos'ile airships are drop-
ping explosives in the vicinity of this
House, I beg to move that this House do
now .adjourn.” Then the roof will fall in.
and Mr. Asquith, immediately previous to
his decease from asphyxiation, will murmur
a few words of regret that i’arliamentary
forms have not been observed.

FITZG[?]ZZLE. M.P.: HIS APOTHEOSIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The f'inih stood around the bed.
And nil the usual things they said
Ju whispers, as they mostly do
When brave King Death litis work to do.
They watched as, whimpering in his sloop,
Fitz Guzzle died. They didn’t weep.
Reporters wrote, 'mid noise and chaff,
Each his obsequious paragraph :
But folks said, "It's no loss, maybe ;
He was an average M.P.,”
And with regrets no tears were blent
lly anyone in Parliament.
(“It would he fine,” one cynic sighed,
“If all the fat Fitz Guzzles died!")
The widow had a slight attack
Of nerves, then robed herself in black ;
The mourning, in the latest style,
Gave her great comfort all the while.
And all the while, discarded clay,
FitzGuzzle’s empty body lay,
And did not seem to care a jot
For any mourner of the lot;
And so, with ritual void of wit,
They took it up and buried it.
They buried it one afternoon
With solemn pomp, and not too soon.
The farce was played, the game was done,
FitzGuzzle's earthly race was run.
Released from mortal toys and jars
I (is soul sped 'mid contemptuous stars
Into the vasts of outer space,
Which is a very lonely place.
His soul—that sort of man was he—
Was half the size of half a pea,
A very tiny, shapeless thing—-
You’d not expect the-stars to sing.-
Tlie soul sped through the dark, and whined
Its anguish sore, dismayed to find
Itself, a thing so mean and wee,
Adrift in cold eternity.
And then, with eyes of lambent flame,
A splendid pitying angel came,
Took up the soul and stopped its wails,
Cracked it between his finger-nails,
And said: “It's queer, without a doubt,
llow such poor insects get about.”
The angel, pondering this puzzle,
To nothingness reduced Fitz Guzzle.
He’s gone. Let men take heart, because
Ho is as if he never was.
lie’s gone. The guilt's on you and me,
117/ o made Fitz Guzzle an M.P.
x.s.w.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Undeterred by the cropper they came over
him in the Chelmsford Stakes at Randwick, punters
rushed the Hooks for Woorak in the Spring Stakes
at Rosehill on Saturday like hungry schnapper after
a prawn. It came off all right at 2 to 1 on, the
unreliable chestnut winning from end to end by half
a dozen lengths. In consequence of this win, in a
rather moderate-quality field, Woorak was pushed
back again into place of favorite for the Epsom Han-
dicap at a price which acquits the Chosen of deliber-
ate intention to squander their substance before the
war is over. Woorak is, of course, a brilliant track
performer—one of the most perfect gallopers seen in
Australia for many a year. But he doesn’t like
moving under pressure. If there had been anything
to tackle him at Rosehill the good thing would, in
all probability, have gone down with the same old
thyd as it has before ; and if there is a dispute in
progress at the business end of the Epsom, then the
rich mile is not for Woorak. At Rosehill he was
allowed to run on at his own clip—the only way to
ride a horse of this type—and he stayed in front the
whole way. He is a horse of quite exceptional pace,
but to back him at a short price in first-class com-
pany is to take a risk which nothing- in his record
will justify. The running at Rosehill of Lardner
in the Rydalmere Mile and First Principle in the
Camellia Stakes—won by the unbacked Orrtato with
a featherweight up—was quite as impressive as
Woorak’s exercise gallop in the Stakes.
•
The Derby favorite, Wallace Isinglass, has fur-
nished into a fine, handsome colt who will gallon
generously over a distance. He took the Rosehill
Guineas like a tradesman after a desperate finish
with Cetinge. On this running the son of Wallace
will be desperately hard to beat in the race for
three-year-olds next month. Blocked by Cetinge on the
rails, the colt had to be pulled out into the course
a furlong from home, thus losing a good two lengths.
To win under those circumstances was a remarkably
good performance, especially after getting nearly the
worst of a scrambling start. But on the strength
of it to chase the odds merchant for a share in
closely-clipped quotations' for the Melbourne Cup, as
many deluded punters have done, requires amazing-
faith.
The Bulletin was directing- attention the other-
week to William the Silent (6st. 121 b.) as an un-
obtrusive .animal which might be found taking part
in the argument about the Metropolitan Handicap.
It doesn’t feel called upon to make any further-
remark about the excessively quiet prad "just now
beyond observing that in the Rosehill Handicap with
fst. 41b. nothing- had a chance with him except
St. Cgrwynne, who turned up the job in disgust and
reckoned second money quite good enough, to the
great satisfaction of the Bar Vorrs.
Punters who got the long prices about
William the Silent for the Metrop. are likely to be
err good terms with themselves when the barrier rises,
though they are not bound to have to collect when
the numbers go up. After his Rosehill Cup victory
William looks a pretty good thing for the 12 furlongs
race. Not as good a thing as Wallace Isinglass for
the A.J.C. Derby, but a splendidly healthy proposi-
tion for anyone who got 50 to 1 about the imported
horse five weeks ago.
“Evens”: As the A.J.C. meeting draws
nearer the sporting columns of the Sydney news-
papers make more frantic efforts to indicate to the
punting community the betting odds obtainable
against probable starters for the big handicaps and
Derby, while keeping- clear of the law which pro-
hibits the publication of the odds. T suggest to
them the algebraic system— -'a system of equine equa-
tions, so to speak. F’rinstanee, “Carlin’s price- for
the Metrepsom is a third of that of First Principle’s.
When each is three points shorter the latter’s figure
will be six times the former’s. This may seem
abstruse, but any schoolboy could answer it in half
a minute. Besides showing due respect for the law
1 his system would provide gentle mental exercise for
a very deserving class prone to neglect its mathe-
matics, and at the same time afford them invaluable
information about their fancies.
An unusual thing happened at Tattersall’s
race-meeting in Adelaide. The third money in a
steeplechase was not claimed. There were only three
starters, and one of them, at the first fence, explained
courteously but firmly that there had been some mis-
take- he understood he had been engaged for a flat
race. He argued the point until the race had been
finished for some time, and then he was ridden off.

lhere has been much talk in Maoriland as
to how C. G. Dalgety’s Martian-Lady Disdain three
year-old gelding Snub would shape when tried in a
real race. It was known that he is regarded by his
owner as being the next string in the stable to War-
step ; and the fact that lie’s half-brother to the
wonderful mare that shook' all Maoriland up las-
season added interest to the speculation. Snub made
his first appearance the other day in the Braemar
Handicap at Ashburton, and landed the bacon by a
neck. He looks like a money-gatherer, and a lot
ot people who know nothing about it are beginnim-
to say “I told you so.”

“Moana”: Apropos recent jockey-scales
swindles. ' There is a scale-manufacturing firm in
Christchurch (M.L.) controlling a valuable local
patent under which lever-scales without any springs
ear, he made. This firm has for years been turning
out a machine that hangs from the ceiling- of the
weighing-room, and gives the weight of the horse-
pjot on a dial. As the chair is open all round and
underneath, the scale .cannot be rigged by getting at
it through floor or walls. The improvement is in
use in different parts of Loan Land, but it isn’t
being rushed as it should be, probably because it is
a local product, and hasn't been dragged from Berlin
or Yokohama or some such place.
N.S.W. Trotting Club is expanding. Last
year £4987 was given in prize-money at 14 meet-
ings in the Metropolis, a rise of £lB4l on the pre-
vious year’s figures. Throughout the State 147
affiliated clubs keep the pastime, business, felony,
or whatever it is, moving.
* * * *
’Murkan Jimmy Clabby blew into Sydney
again the other day, and after announcing himself
fit and well, rushed off to get fitter. ITis first
engagement will be with Darcy, and if James has
brought along the assorted biffs and blocks he
carried with him on previous visits to this part
the argument between the two should be strenuous.
The more recent disturbances in which he took part
in the States didn’t put him any nearer the middle-
weight championship than he was before. But
James is a very accomplished boxer, all the same.
* * * *
The punchings that Billy Murray has re-
ceived in Australia have done him no good, lie has
probably deteriorated 50 per cent, since leaving his
Yankee home, and the hiding he got, from McGoorty
at Sydney Stadium on Saturday night was not the
sort to assist rejuvenation. In the first round he lost
his head, in the third his powers of resistance, in the
fourth his senses. McGoorty completed the massacre
with a clean knock-out to the chin. Murray had
climbed to the perpendicular as the count ended, but
it was quite a. while before lie knew what had hap-
pened. The man’s courage is wonderful, his indiffer-
ence to punishment amazing, his judgment deplorable,
lie fought the worst fight that could have been
imagined, completely reversing the methods be had
employed against Darcy a, couple of weeks before, and
playing right into McGoorty’s bands. He was as
wide open as a gate, and his hits were as slow as a
sermon. McGoorty was in rare good form, and had
an easy job. Against a slow boxer McGoorty is
deadly.
* * * *
A youth with a hefty light and a left of
direful possibilities has unearthed himself up in the
district Darcy comes from, and having’ scattered the
local talent and snuffed Les Gleeson out in a round
is to be stood up against Arthur Everdeu. If the
young gentleman makes good in that little un-
pleasantness he is expected to do further damage
shortly with more difficult material. Good judges
wiio have seen him speak of a very promising lad.

dashed against each other at Brisbane on Saturday,

The towel saved him from oblivion. Stated that

to Yankeeland in the train of “Fighting Billy’’
Murray. He should do well in the short bouts popu-
lar thereabouts.

McGoorty being a middleweight going to survive? lie

7ilb.-r-a pound and a half over the' Australian middle-

at 8.30 p.m. every pound of 12st:5f5atB£h probably
a bit over—his real fighting weight, and one he cannot

The absolute, last, decisive, grand final of
lie. League football for this season, oil the Metro-
politan Cricket Ground, Melbourne, was a moment-
ous circumstance. In brilliant weather Carlton and
Colling wood faced each other with their best teams
Bines winning the toss had the advantage of the
breeze, and at the first change they had to credit
23 points to ’Wood’s 12. In the second term they
stuck to their lead, and it looked as if ’Wood's sys-
tematic play would not prevail against Carlton’s
well-planned rushes. But the third quarter brought
a change. The Magpies took full control of the
game, and methodically piled on points till, just after
entering the last quarter, a hard-earned goal gave
them the lead. Unfortunately for their hopes the
continued effort had been too great; Carlton’s coun-
ter-attack yielded two goals and then the Magpies
collapsed, and the Blues ran out winners by 78
points to ’Wood’s 45. Thus Carlton retained the
premiership and gained their eleventh pennant. The
attendance (39,211) was ahead of last year’s 30,495,
though both crowd and “gate,” £1409, were far
below the “grand final” record of ’l3, when the
meeting of Fitzroy and St. Kilda attracted 59,550
spectators, equal to a £2308 “house.”
“The Recruiting Sergeant”: The fine-old
traditions of our forefathers are not to be suffered
to die out! Waterloo was won on the playing fields
of Eton, and if Gallipoli is not lost on the Melbourne
Cricket Ground it will be no fault of those who
bellow and blurt round an arena far more glorious
in their eyes than that in which , red-eyed, sleepless
men in dirty trenches stand up for a greater tradition
than sport, till maybe a Turkish bullet puts them out
of the great game for ever. On the morning of the
day when Carlton was to contest the football pre-
miership with Collingwood, the Magpies were nearly
knocked off their perch because two players, Rowan
and Seddon, in camp at Broadnteadows, had been
refused leave. The club secretary whirled out to
the camp in a motor-car. What he said is not
known, but the military potentate who had failed to
bow down to King Football relaxed his iron hand,
and the two players, who had taken-part in a 10-mile
march that morning, were rushed back to town in
time to take their places in the playing field. The
result of the match was cheered to the echo, and.
shook the Sultan on his throne. Over in Gallipoli
tired soldiers “spat buttons” from parched mouths,
grunted wearily, and took a fresh grip of their
scalding rifles as they settled anew to repel a charge
of ignorant heathens who had never heard of Colling-
wood, an 4 would probably burn it to the ground if
they were givT r fl a"Chance to enter it.

Rugby League football came to an end for
the season in Sydney on Saturday at the Agricultural
Ground yyith a duel between teams representing the
Metropolitan' and Northern districts. It was a fast
game, ajpt&hjk scores show —30 points to 26. The
home players won; but more by good luck than good
managemeift A wild pass by a Newcastle man in
the last few minutes lobbed into the arms of Frank
Burge, and with characteristic expedition that speed
machine was off. Nobody could pace with him, and
he plunked down the try between the goal posts, and
that try was all liis side needed. Only about 3000
spectators were on the ground to cheer his dash —a
poor outlook for the Newcastle League’s ground-pur-
chasing fund, for which the match was specially
arranged. On the other hand, whether a football
ground as a football ground will be an asset or an
alabaster elephant when the season of 1916 comes
round is a problem which may best be solved with
the two-up kip.
During the afternoon the Agricultural Ground
flags marked the loss of Major. Jim McManamey,
killed with so many other-tine Australians at Galli-
poli. No man ever had«the interests of football,
particularly the Rugby Union brand, more at heart
than he. A noted forward in his day, a referee keen,
quick and utterly impartial, and a sound adviser in
the councils of delegates, Janies McManamey’s name
will live in the traditions of the g'anie, at any rate,
on the east coast of Australia. lie couldn’t have
been more than a week or so in the great trouble
when death found him. Before the war, in his work-
ing hours, he was a barrister.
* * * *
“Wally” Messenger’s scoring ability put
him in a class by himself in N.S.W. Rugby League
this season. In premiership and City Cup matches
he got 88 points (six tries and 35 goals). A.
Bolewski (77), H. liorder (73), and F. Burge (72)
were next on the list. F. Burge’s collection of 24
tries, and A. Bolewski’s 37 goals were something to
set to music. W. Messenger is a brother of the
famous “Dally,” who made mighty piles of points
under both Union and League, bawigrs in two hemi-
spheres.
* -X- * *
The most untamed game in N. S. Wales
on Saturday raged under the auspices and patronage
of the Rugby League at Bathurst. The joust,'shindy
or gunpowder-plot was between Bathurst and Hartley.
The legions of the Citj r of the Plains powdered the
miners in the western dust until their own points
were 36 while the stricken enemy’s were still nil.
But Hartley, like the Russian bear, was getting some
satisfaction for its run. Six of its men at that stage
had been ordered off the field for stoush and such
like, and the other seven then retired in a body to
lick their wounds and despatch a communique to the
effect that the fall of Hartley on the western front
would not permanently affect the campaign in that
theatre, and that ultimate victory was assured.
The final for the Gardiner Challenge Cup
drew a big crowd of Soccer enthusiasts to Sydney
University Oval on Saturday in the expectation of
an unusually strenuous battle between Balmain and
West Wallsend. Seven times the men of the water-
side have booted the State championship
home since the Cup was made its sign and manual
in 188 S ; but this year the strong play of the coun-
try team, particularly in the past couple of weeks,
had put the fear of Adam into Balmainiacs. They
might have spared themselves, however, for their
deputies won without exhausting their resources by
two goals to nil, one scored in each spell. In 27 finals
city teams have won 22, the remainder being gath-
ered by' Northern district reps. Southern district
clubs have yet to break the monotonj’ of defeat.
Paddington, winner in the Australian
Rulqs section of N.S.W. football, had a fine record
this season. In 17 matches (one lost) it scored 125
goals and 195 behinds (945 points) against 61 goals
56 behinds (422 points). It is Paddington’s first
premiership, and it made the thing quite decisive.
The Australian Rules League at Adelaide
has been playing off its semi-finals, and the surprise
packet has been Sturt, which walloped out South
Adelaide and West Adelaide on successive Saturdays.
The former team was cleaned up first by nine goals
six behinds (60 points) to four goals 12 behinds (36
points), and last Saturday West Adelaide went down
at the Oval by seven goals three behinds (45 points)
to two goals 13 behinds (25 points). Sturt, the
youngest team in the competition, will now meet
Port Adelaide in the challenge final, and great should
be the excitement thereof.
* * * *
The first Maoriland toeballer to earn a
war decoration is Corporal C. W. Saunders, of
Dunedin. He is well remembered as five-eighths in
the first-grade team of the famous Pirates Club in
the Scawtch city in 1903. Being in Fogland when
the trouble with Bill Sauercabbage started he
promptly got to the Front, where he has been fight-
ing ever since. Now word drifts through that he
has been handed the D.C.M. for gallantry. He has a
brother with the M.L. Field Artillery at Gallipoli.
* * * *
The Dunlop Rubber Co. puts the widest
of Australian commercial questions in narrow advt.
form: “Are foreign-made tyres to predominate in the
Commonwealth?” Just now the answer of. the right-
thinking Australian citizen is emphatically “No”;
but the question in the future will be answered only
by the tariff. No foreign anything that can be effici-
ently manufactured here will predominate in the local
market if the tariff gives patriotic encouragement to
the local article; and competition can be left to
ensure that the local producer will give the purchaser
a fair deal. It is just a questiou for the tariff.
Mariner Edward Jones, of Melbourne, dis-
claims credit for inventing the motor-car tyre now
manufactured by the Emergency Tyre Co. Captain
Pearse (of the Pastoralists’ Review) struck the idea
of making tyres with coeoanut fibre, and on the strength
thereof formed a co. in Sydney. But Capt. Pearse’s
invention was lacking in one essential, and eventually,
after 10 months’ experimenting, Jones invented the
joint, or splice, which was needed to make a commer-
cial success of it. lie then formed a co. to make and
sell the goods in Australia on a royalty to the Homing
Tyre Co., which has the disposal of the manufactur-
ing rights for foreign countries.
* * * *
Tlie gluttonous crowds of the U.S.A. lick
their lips over the risks of motor-car speed races;
and one mob had a beautiful gorg-e last month at
lies Moines (Iowa) when in a 300-mile race Joe
Cooper was killed and his mechanist terribly in-
jured. Cooper was raging round the mile circuit
past thousands of spectators at the rate of 95 miles
an hour when one of his tyres blew out. The ear
leapt like chain-lightning over the outer edge of the
track and smashed in smithereens 20ft. away. The
same day, at the same show, another hell-fired car
came to grief and..killed , its driver, Morris Keeler,
and close-up settled his mechanist. “The accident
cast a gloom over the race,” says the New York
Times. A gloom ! ’Struth 1
The suggestion that Victoria and N.S.W.
, should carry out an inter-State cricket programme
this season for patriotic -purposes received scanty
support in N. S. Wales. The Vic. Association is
credited with fathering the proposal ; lint as it had
already been agreed that- inter-State cricket should
he flung up the idea had to he dropped. The trouble
is that the proposal came too late —like the immor-
talised pardon.
Cricket in N. S. Wales, which officially
commences on October 2, is likely to be at a very*
low ebb in all grades, particularly amongst the
juniors, wherein some districts have notified that they
will have the honor not to be represented at all owing
to heavy enlistment from their ranks. Amongst the
seniors University has informed the Association that
after the November examinations it may have to
withdraw entirely from the season’s programme.
Other clubs have appealed to old members who have
passed the military age to get into flannels again
and keep things moving until better days dawn and
the call to the suppler athlete is no longer from the
field of battle. As for the Association itself, it may
be confidently expected that if it finds the engage-
ments of the 1915-16 season interfering with recruit-
ing it will put the shutters up altogether. The posi-
tion is such that three grounds will not be of any use
to the Assoc, this season and four only partially so ;
many junior teams will be missing as well as third-
grade elevens from University, Leichhardt, Redfern,
North Sydney, Middle Harbor and Central Cumber-
land. So the cricketer appears to be doing his little
bit. Incidentally, in such play as takes place eight
balls to the over will be the order.
The first sailing race of the season took
place in Port Jackson on Saturday in the form of
an open-boat event jointly managed by Flying
Squadron and Sailing Club. Of the 14 boats that
spread themselves to the rattling S.S.E. breeze only
six were' thrashing along with it after the first round
of the triangle. Then there was boatmanship that
might well have brought old man Neptune out from
the cave where the three-star rum is pannikined out
by the mermaid with the coral in her hair. The six
were Golding (R. Beashel), Australia (Chris. .Webb),
Kismet (W. Dunn), Evelyn (IT. Green), Mavis (F.
W. Moppett) and Advance (IT. E. Thompson). They
finished in that order, Golding beating the Australian
champion boat by 25see., 32sec. separating second
and third proas, and one minute the third and fourth.
X.S.W. Great Public School champion-
ships brought out a string of excellent perform-
ances. The episode of the gathering was the wing-
clipping- of Sydney Grammar (which had held the
championship for an unbroken 19 years) by Sydney
C. of E. Grammar, although it was only by the
narrowest of margins—29 points to 28. It was a
rare tussle for the points between the sprinting
of S.G. and the hurdling, jumping and distance-
running of C.E.G. The nippiest individual perform-
ers were R. F. Cripps (Newington College), who
won 440yds. teams race, 220yds. championship, and
was second in 100yds. and 440vds. ; and R. S. Hol-
combe (C.E.G.), who defeated the striding Ludowici
and a host of others in 880yds. and one mile.
Another fine effort was a high jump of sft. Tjin.
by McKellar (T.S.C.) ; and the running of 100yds.
by Leadley (S.G.), in 10 2-sscc. —4yds. worse than
evens.
* * * *
Two more notable N. S. Wales sports
joined the., khaki army last week in Dave McAlister
(winner of two “King’s” in Australia and third in
a Bislev “King’s”) and Harry Ilauestein, who has
pulled a blade in five inter-State eight-oar events, and
was No. 5 of the Australian crew that, won the
Grand Challenge Clip at Henley in 1912. Also,
George Kefford, another great rifle shot of Bathurst,
Stepped up. On Saturday at his home range he
plugged 16 consecutive bull’s-eyes from the 500yds.
mound. There’ll be some more vacant chairs in old
Stamboul when a few others of that stamp join their
comrades already in the business.
* * • * -X- *
“Bias’’: The Sydney bowling world is
tackling an ancient problem again. Most of the
suburban clubs are numerically weak, and the
annual subs, aren’t enough to meet the heavy ex-
penses of a tip-top green. For a start the skilled
person who looks after the rinks is cut down to a
microscopic wage, and a screw is made up for him
from the shilling which every visitor on the green is
required by custom to pay for his game. The system
of cadge is peculiar to bowls. The obvious remedy
is to raise subs., but members won't stand this, as
.they foolishly prefer to pay 30s. on other greens than
another £1 a year on their own. The main objec-
tion is that in no game that wasn’t of Scotch origin
would a guest be asked for a bob a time, and as a
solution it is proposed that its collection be made
from the hosts and not the visitors. Though an
improvement, this will not be really satisfactory
cither to the clubs which will tax their match-players
to pay the wages of the caretaker, or to the caretaker
himself, who would much prefer a fixed salary
instead of the weekly collection of shillings. The
caretakers could form a union and appeal to the
Arbitration Court, but the small clubs threaten to
disband if saddled with an attendant at a living
wage.
ROMANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her eyes were dark and subtle, as the night
is dark when shifting' clouds blot out the stars ;
In the dim room her mouth’s red flame shone I wight,
Tempting my blood that burned to leap its bars.
Close, close 1 clasped her, and she turned her head
Away to avoid the menace of my kisses.^
All maidenly she strove with me, and sa|d:
“Oh, let me go, or else I’ll tell the Missus !”
N.S.W.
JOS. Q. H.

Are You a Victim of
CATARRH!
You know what Catarrh is, with its humiliating and discomfort-
ing symptoms.
A slight cold develops into a stubborn and protracted one, while
the discharges from the nose are most offensive and annoying.
As the inflammation extends further up into the bead and the
delicate mechanism of the ear is attacked, there is a continual
buzzing and ringing in the ears, and frequently the hearing is
partially or totally lost. Headaches are almost constant, with
sometimes acute pains in the eyes, and the nose becomes so
plugged up that the patient must breathe through the mouth,
while the slimy secretions are forced back into the throat,
requiring almost continual hawkingand spitting to dislodge them.
At certain stages of the disease the odor of the breath becomes
very bad, to the mortification and embarrassment of the sufferers,
and the disgust of all who come near them. Gradually the
mucous discharge finds its way into the internal organs, and a
most miserable form of Dyspepsia, or Catarrh of the Stomach, is
the inevitable consequence. The blood becomes contaminated by
catarrhal poisons and unhealthy matter, and these are
distributed through the body.
Catarrh is then a deep-seated, dangerous, constitutional or
systemic disease, and its effect upon the general health and
system is soon apparent —appetite and strength are gone, weight
is lost, despondency takes the place of confidence, and the disease
frequently settles in some vital organ or terminates in Bronchitis,
Asthma, Neuritis, or dread Consumption.

“E.N.M.” : A new version of an old play
is in preparation. It is to be called “Our
American Cousin-German.’’
A while ago the Independent Theatre,
London, offered a prize for an “All British”
light- or comic-opera libretto. The compe-
titors were requested to send their manu-
scripts to •). T. Groin, dramatic critic, who
is one of live judges chosen for the competi-
tion. Another of the five is Herman Klein,
also musical critic. Groin and Klein, all-
British !
“Harrison O.”: Excusing Australian
managers for glutting the market with
Yankee dramatic goods, “C. Ockney” (B.
9/9/’lb) remarks: “I don’t think the man-
agements are blamable. The English drama
was dead long before the war, and by latest
reports it is still dead and likely to remain
so.” He proceeds to mention several Eng-
lish plays that failed in London during the
last theatrical season. Apart from the obvious
unfairness of quoting war-time failures, the
writer calmly assumes that the voice of the
Cockney is the voice of God, and that the
reception of a play here will merely dupli-
cate its London experience. This, of
course,.is not so. But, even accepting
“Ockney’s” assumption tlial what goes in the
Fog will go here, consider the number of
London successes of recent years that are
practically unknown in these parts. “His
House In Order” was Pinero’s biggest hit
since “The Second Mrs. Tanqueray” (and a
better play) : but apart from a production
by Hugh Buckler Australia knows it not;
and the same applies (putting aside Reper-
tory productions) to Arnold Bennett’s “The
Great Adventure.” “Jack Straw,” “John
Chilcote, M.P.,” “Drake” and “Hindle
Wakes” are London booms that have been
enthusiastically ignored by leading Austra-
lian managements. “Ockney” is a poor
apologist for these pushers of Yankee “lines”
when he can urge nothing more convincing
in their justification than the old gag that
English drama is dead. From what I have
seen of Amurkan productions, a Yankee
dramatist worth Considering has not yet
been born.
“Kodak,” after feeding his Soul: —
A friend took me to a really high-grade recital the
other evening. Like everybody else I always tell
people I am fond of good music; but I notice 1
never spend coin to hear it, and that makes me
wonder whether I really am musical. Immediately
we got inside we found ourselves amongst the worried
sort of people who pay real money to frequent these
things. There wasn't one jolly-looking person in
sight. The chief performer scorned to saw off any-
thing which had a suspicion of melody about it. 1
was told that he was not out to supply vulgar enter-
tainment, but to pump up Souls and raise the artis-
tic hair of Sydney. If the good work can be accom-
plished by playing things like Parliamentary debates
and mathematical problems, that missionary is going
to accomplish the trick some time. All I know is I
listened to him until he stopped work for the night,
and then came away with disconnected piano-notes
(those high, clear ones like ice chips) sliding coldly
down my neck and my pocket full of violin screams.
Maybe it is only a sign that my neglected Soul is
stirring, but I invariably emerge from these func-
tions feeling as if I had been thrown from the top
of an iceberg, and had my teeth filed, and been
hosed with red-hot needles and forced to swim three
miles through a wind-lashed sea of old feather dust-
ers. If it is correct to find no fun in these bleak
entertainments, but to feel as above, then I am really
musical, and my Soul is O.IC.
“0.K." : What a shapely, sprightly “pan-
tomime boy” is Carrie Moore since her re-
generation ! She sings well, acts well, dances
gracefully, looks pretty, and lias any amount
of “way” with her. But because Carrie is
an Australian girl whose name and talent
have been familiar to us since she was a
mere kiddie, and also because she has been
knocking around in vaudeville here for the
past year or two, the J.C.W. firm never
thought, seemingly, of engaging her for the
next panto. The “boy” for the grand
annual custom will be imported. She is
usually an imported lady, and she never is
a patch upon the Carrie Moore who lately
appeared in “Babes in the Wood” at Mar-
low’s theatre at Melbourne. As far as I
can remember the Carrie Moore of other
days, who did play principal boy in at least
one J.C.W. panto, was a lean, unfurnished
stripling. She went to London and made a
hit in two or three of Courtneidge’s produc-
tions. Then the firm —seven years ago-
imported its long-lost Carrie to star as the
Merry Widow, a character for which she
hadn’t the right temperament. Now that
she is at her very best she figures in a
three-two-one show, and cuts no ice in the
newspapers that are always prepared to
grovel over a third-class performer from
Britain or America.
“Theorbo” : Laver’s orchestral programme
at Melbourne Auditorium last week in-
cluded the overture to Ambroise Thomas’s
"Mignon.” This was the work which was
performed in the Paris Opera Comique on
the night the house was burnt down with
loss of 150 lives. One scene is the burning
of a chateau. The mock fire turned into a
real one. The people could have got out,
but they thought the outbreak was in the
play until flames began to lick the prosce-
nium and roof. Then there was a rush.
The site remained vacant many days. It
was proposed to put up an expiatory church,
but funds failed to come in, and the Opera
Comique rose up instead, and “Mignon”
was performed again.
“Star” : An English illustrated weekly has
been discussing the creations of Pinero, ask-
ing which is the most vivid of bis characters,
and hitting upon Richard Phenyl in “Sweet
Lavender” as the party most alive. This
does not quite suit me. To my mind Pinero
created only one personality so actual that
I find myself continually coming up against
it in “real life.” This woman does not
appear in the plays at all. and owes her
undeniable existence to a phrase. She is
the first Mrs. Tanqueray, the creature “all
white marble and black velvet, who keeps a
thermometer in her stays that always
registers two degrees below zero.” The
second Mrs. Tank, is Pinero’s favorite, but
to me she is a passionate automaton while
her predecessor is a vital fact.


NEEDLESS FEAR OF
HEART TROUBLE
Many people have needless fears about the
state of their hearts. Sharp, shooting pains
that are sometimes present round the heart
worry the sufferer into dread of heart dis-
ease. As a matter of fact, pain in the heart
is scarcely ever present: in organic heart dis-
ease. The pain is nearly always from the
stomach.
When your digestion is out of order it often
affects the heart, and even causes an irregu-
larity in its beat. The gas produced by
undigested and fermented food may cause
pressure that affects the heart unpleasantly.
Hut these pains do not mean that you have
heart disease, nor need fear sudden death.
Tone up the stomach, correct any errors of
diet. Eat the right things, and net too much
of them. Don’t pamper your stomach with
pre digested food and artificial aids to diges-
tion. Tone up your stomach so that it will
do its own proper work. Don’t worry about
your heart; worry causes and aggravates in-
digestion.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Fills are the best diges-
tive tonic. First you notice an increase in
your appetite. Then you see that the food
does not distress you ; that you are no longer
troubled with wind, sour risings in the
throat, and those misleading pains round the
heart. Strength and energy return. A use-
ful booklet on “What to Eat and How to
Eat” will lie sent to anyone sending name
and address to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
( 1 0., Sydney.*

USHER’S NEW METROPOLITAN HOTEL, Castlereagh Street, Sydnsy,
which was opened last Thursday, September 16.
Special Notice to Country
Visitors to Sydney.
The illustration on this page depicts the new Metropolitan Hotel, built under the
supervision of Host Usher, who for i->0 odd years has made a special feature of attend-
ing to the comfort and well-being of country people.
This new building—which is now open for guests—is fireproof from roof-tree to
foundation. It will not be possible to burn it down or to burn any pari; of it. The
staircases, internal Avails, floors and ceilings are wholly proof against tire. Every
bedroom, in fact every room in the house, is connected direct to the central offices of
the lire brigade, so that even the smallest conflagration will have no terror for the
occupant of any room. All that it will be necessary to do in case of lire is to walk
out of the room, close the door, and be in immediate safety.
Two main passenger elevators traverse the building through fireproof chambers.
Other elevators have been installed for moving goods, luggage, etc.
Throughout the whole building a unique system of Exhaust Tubing l a ; been
installed for the removal of dust and impure air. A great many of the bedroo; • are
proxuded with luxurious bathrooms; hot and cold water being available instantly.
Apart from the natural ventilation, the air will be silently removed from the
bedrooms, day and night, by means of an extensive method of power ventilation, and
not only is the air removed from the public rooms, but pure, dust free air. drawn from
a height of 150 ft. above the pavement is returned into the rooms continuously.
Host Usher has always been noted for the manner in xvheli he caters for the
comforts of the inner man, and the chef’s department in his new hotel is second to
nothing in any part of the world. It xvdl provide a cuisine on a par xvith the best
obtainable in London or America.
It is completely fitted with self-contained refrigerating plant, larders, storerooms,
bakery, etc.
On the ground floor are two entrances, one communicating to the main vestibule,
the second communicating direct to the Saloon and Lounge Bars.
Upon (he first and second floors are the I lining. Smoking, Lounge and Readiug
Rooms ; the third floor contains the Ladies’ Sitting Room.
The furniture consists of hand-made articles of chaste design, supplied by Beard,
Watson Ltd., and the quality and finish of their productions and fittings is fully
consistent with an hotel of the highest class.
Country and inter-State visitors to Sydney will find Host Usher ready to take
them on a visit of inspection over the new building, and will always he pleased to do
his utmost to make the Metropolitan a real home for those aa t lio decide to make his
hotel their toxvn residence.
In passing, it xvould bo as xvell to mention that the Pastoralist, Grazier and
Station Holder can still obtain from Host Usher his famous O.S.L. (“Old Special
Liqueur”) Whisky. This whisky is imported direct from the Distillers. It is
thoroughly matured, mellow, high-class whisky, guaranteed 12 years old before leaving
Scotland. It is trebly distilled, every care being made to preserve its unique quality
and flavor.
The shipments never vary in quality in the slightest way, so that when O.S.L. is
ordered a whisky of uniform character, purity and excellence is assured.
O.S.L. Whisky can be obtained either in bottle, 5-gallon demijohns, 5- to 10-gallon
kegs, octaves, and quarter casks at wholesale rates. ,

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long us possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-

BEFORE THE MIRROR.
By “JEANNETTE.”
“Jeannette,” the well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of “The Book of Beauty,”
1111l 111 he pleased to help and advise any reader on matters of the toilet. Queries should be accom-
panied by a stamped addressed envelope, and directed to “Jeannette,” c/o “Before the Mirror,”
4o Gray s Inn Road, London, W.C., England.
■These are undoubtedly days of exceptional nervous strain for one and all of
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow over the
lives of many of us women, and while, perhaps, a few —the more stoical among
us- can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, we may all, with a little
care and forethought, succeed in preventing the results of worry from being notice-
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat will, however, be
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real need of help in toilet
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown
method of retaining, or regaining, that beauty of face and figure which is the birth-
right of every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are at present
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
A Beautiful Complexion by Natural Means. —
The secret of a perfect complexion lies in the
continual renewing of the outer cuticle of the
skin. This is Nature’s own method. The
outer skin as it becomes coarse or shrivelled
must be removed, and an opportunity given to
the liner one beneath to show itself. It is
because the old. dead skin is allowed to remain
on the face that so many women, and even
young girls, suffer from pimples, blotches and
sallow dull skins, To remove, by absorption,
the dead outer skin and with it ail blemishes,
the use of mercolised wax is universally re-
commended. ordinary face creams being
powerless for this purpose. Smear the wax
over the face and neck, rub it gently into the
skin, and leave it on all night, in the morning
wash' •it off. using Pilenta soap and warm
water, when all the dead skin will be removed
with the wax. Then apply a lotion to prevent
any trace of greasiness being noticeable and
to give a peach-like bloom to the skin. A
lotion to do this can be made up quite cheaply
from the following recipe. Take one ounce of
cleminite and dissolve it in four tablespoonfuls
of hot water. Place in a bottle, and when cool
apply to the face with the finger tips, allowing
it to dry on the skin.
To Increase the Growth of the Hair. —Few
women give enough attention to the hair,
which, if it is to be kept in good condition,
requires systematic brushing, regular sham-
pooing, and a tonic for at least two or three
.months in each year. The hair should be
brushed every night for from five to ten
minutes, and the scalp massaged. When a
tonic is to be applied it should be dabbed into
the roots immediately after the massage, while
the scalp is still warm and glowing. To make
a simple and inexpensive lotion which will in-
crease the growth of the hair take a quarter
of a pint of bay rum, and add to it one ounce
of boranium. Shake well and allow it to stand
for half an hour, then strain, and add suffi-
cient water to make half a pint.
Superfluous Hair, and How to Remove It. —
Superfluous hair is a real trial to those who
daily see the growth which disfigures their
whole face steadily increasing, for nothing so
completely destroys a woman’s claim to good
looks. To remove it quickly and permanently
take a little pheminol and mix with a little
cold water. Dust the skin* from which the
liair is to he removed, with some powdered
alum, then apply the plieminol paste with a
hone knife'. Leave it on for two minutes, then
wash it off. If the growth he very strong two
or more applications may he necessary to re-
move it. hut this treatment will eventually so
weaken and destroy the roots that the trouble
will be banished for all time,
To Remove Blackheads. — ,Dissolve one effer-
vescing sty mol tablet in a glass Of hot Water,
allow the effervescence to subside, then difi it
small sponge into the liquid and mop over
the face. Allow it to remain on for a few
minutes, then wipe off with a towel, and the
blackheads will he entirely removed. To pre-
vent them from reappearing use a stymol tab-
let in the manner described twice a week.
How to Shampoo.—Before shampooing the
hair dip the fingers into a little pure olive or
almond oil. and thoroughly massage the scalp.
Then dissolve a teaspoonful of stallax granules
in a cup of hot water. Fill a basin with warm
water into which the ends of the hair can fall,
and shampoo the stallax mixture into the
roots in the usual way. Then rinse and dry
by fanning with a palm fan.
To Wave the Hair.—To wave the hair do not
use waving irons, as these are most injurious
to the hair, causing it to fade and turn grey,
and to become harsh and brittle. Get two
ounces of silmerine at any chemist’s and damp
the hair with it, then either plait tightly,
tying the ends, or arrange it in waving pins.
The Trials of the Stout Woman.—The stout
woman is not only at a disadvantage in ap-
pearance, but she is always uncomfortable
physically and seldom in really good health
while she remains too fat. To remove obesity,
without injuring the general health or alter-
ing the usual diet, a few ciyhoi berries should
be eaten occasionally, and a glass of hot water
sipped after each meal. This combined treat-
ment will work Wonders in a very short tittle,
and is a most pleasant as well as effective
remedy.
Grey Hair. —To restore the Color of grey or
fading hair take one ounce of concentrated
tammalite and mix it with four ounces of bav
rum, apply to the hair for several nights,
using a small sponge, and the hair will gradu-
ally resume its original color. When the
desired shade has been obtained, the tamma-
lite lotion should be used about twice a week.
Apply it to the roots with an old toothbrush. [1

innocent, blue-eyed poem that we hesitate to send it
home by post. It should be sent home by tram in
chaige of a serious elderly person with unimpeach-
able references Quiz: Like most first attempts it
fails to stop the traffic. .. .Nevvy: Oh, yes, there is
always hope. But you must supply it. We remain
in charge of the acid.. 1 . S.D.E.: Spring has arrived,
and this staff has to tie itself in its various chairs in
order to keep on reading poems about wattle and
blue seas and clouds instead of going Out to look at
these things in person. So please don’t babble more
than you can help. . . .F.S.T.Y.: You arc earning-
encouragement in small doses.... Trench: Do you
remember the verses:—
Ho! the Ballad-writer rose,
Snorting slaughter down his nose,
And he drew his pen as though it were a sword?
That s what you remind us of when you whet your
beak on the corrugated-iron roof of Australian litera-
ture Mena: Not much interested in the dreadful
state of things in Egypt five months ago. No doubt
the Red Cross people were taken unawares. But, if
it comes to that, so was everybody else—except,
apparently, the Turk. He was the only one who
had much beforehand information about what was
going to happen; and even he didn’t know every-
thing.
TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
C.S.P.: The joke on the brown-paper bag has been
thrown at the enemy. That shows how mad we are
with the enemy W.J.: Two perfectly flat people
making a doubtful joke....M. Anderson: Some
humor, but the anatomy is out. . . .Torrie: Your lady
angels are too chesty F.M.L.: Thanks; handed
on Frong: Very little use for photos. But they
ought to interest one of the weeklies that illustrate
that way.
In order that they may have the privilege
of pulling cows’ teats, Maorilanders have
lately paid the staggering price of £135 an
acre for dairying land near Gisborne. Now
what does the Cold Hash Government think
is going to be the end of this sort of thing?
George Meudell has flung at Victoria a
statistical brick which ought to make that
State blink its sleepy eyes, even if it doesn’t
wake right up. In five years, he shows, the
number of Victorian public servants, outside
the Railways, has increased 45 per cent.,
while salaries have increased 50 per cent.
But that wouldn’t matter if these public
servants were earning their keep. That
neither they nor the money which has been
borrowed. has done anything of the sort is
shown by the further fact that Australian
taxation (State and Commonwealth) went
from £11,580,964 in 1901-2 to £22.892,742 in
1913-14. In other words, while the ..popula-
tion of Australia has increased only 27 per
cent., the excess of expenditure over earn-
ings. which has had to be made good by
taxation, has increased just on 100 per cent.


G.E.
FLOW METERS

: VP' ! ..7V /fj
IN THE
POWER PLANT
The largest steam plants in the world use G.E. Flow Meters.
The smallest plants need them even more them the big ones, because the savings are greater in
proportion to the investment.
In welLmanaged plants the steam Flow Heter is a necessity and not a luxury. On Boilers it shows
the output in 8.H.P., so that the Engineer can see when the Boiler is loafing, needs cleaning
or overhauling.
Losses which waste coal will be quickly shown up. *'
G.E. Flow Meters are also made for measuring the flow of water, air or gas.
