IN BANANA LAND
Bananaland’s just-dead Clerk of Parlia-
ments, C. W. Costin, won his way to the
Brisbane Grammar School on a State
scholarship ; and so did his schoolmate and
chum, T. J. Byimes, the brilliant, lazy man
who was afterwards Premier of Queensland.
A courteous, able official, who looked after
budding Parliamentarians like a father,
Costin helped many a now well-known poli-
tician on the way to eminence.
The sudden death at sea on September 16
of Capt. Chas. Reis, R.N.R., aboard the
Uupara, recalls the fact that in 1878 when
Russia was a bogey which threw Australia
into convulsions Reis was put in charge of
a little gunboat called the Pippo to patrol
the coast of Moreton Bay and so frighten
off the enemy. The deceased mariner was
30 years in the employ of the Adelaide S.S.'
Co., and latterly was pilot for its fleet
through the Ilinchinbrook Channel on the
Queensland coast. Somebody must have
killed a Chinaman on the Ruparj> She
caught fire just before Reis died, and the
vessel reached Port Jackson with a dead
pilot and a live conflagration on board.
It lives still, up at Rockhampton—the
“Australian Freedom League,” for the aboli-
tion of Compulsory Training. It throws
literature over the back fence for the citizen
to read, and- then hurriedly runs away to
the sandheap and sticks its head in. A
sample from the latest pamphlet:—
The time lias arrived when the civil population of
an invaded country has little to fear from a properly
organised and disciplined army if it does not resist
and uses reasonable care to keep out of the way of
bursting shells.
Of course everyone knows that, especially
the people who got out of Louvain, and those
others who dodged Jack Johnsons while they
were munching turnips outside Brussels.
Likewise the people of Galicia and Poland,
who are wandering' about looking for crusts
and a dug-out. This illuminative pamphlet
carries the information that it is issued by
Edward Foreman, and it appears to be the
fifteenth of a series. Foreman is a grocer
at Rockhampton, and The Bulletin is sure
that his scales are better balanced than one,
other important part of the outfit.
“Norbell” : If this isn’t a world’s record
for shearing it ought to be. At Wanta-
laynia, out Winton. way, over 10,000 jum-
bucks have just been operated on, and five
•shearers (sit tight!) in one day averaged
260 sheep per man,
“5.8.”: Drought-stricken Longreach has
sent 355 men to the Front, besides others
who enlisted in Brisbane. And those who
couldn’t go and argue the point with Abdul
Bill dropped £3OOO into the liat on the
latest Patriotic “Day.” Considering how
small and sun-dried Longreach is, this is
a better record even than Gliarleville’s; and
we are not saying that Charleville’s isn’t
good.
“Milo” : While oysters- are found along the
coast of North Queensland in considerable
quantities, of good flavor, and much larger
than those dredged from the south coast of
N. S. Wales, growers declare that they can-
not cultivate them in beds. The other day
I talked to an oyster man about his failure
to grow crops of the fish, and tried
to= persuade him that he was neglect-
ing a great primary industry and
turning his hack on one of the coun-
try’s chief natural resources. He, in
THE COMPLIMENT.
“Oh, my Lord Bishop, to decide an argument, toe agreed to leave it to your decision-of
all the improper books ever written, which do you consider 'the worst?"
turn, blamed the big black and white crab
of the North, and made affidavit that he
had lost capital in every effort to raise a
crop in beds. When the oysters are within
easy reach of the crab they are unable to
feed, because the watchfulness of the leggy
crustacean enables him to detect the first
sign of opening, and he”promptly places a
small pebble in the doorway, thereby pre-
venting the house being shut up again. Then
the crab banquets at will on the helpless
oyster, and the bivalve birthrate is, none the
better for it.
“Townsville’”; While the politicians are
saying that it is the duty of every fit and
free man to enlist, other people are stamp-
ing out enthusiasm with both feet. Many
men of the North have enlisted, and after
passing the local medical /test have been
feted and cheered and gone away to Bris-
bane only to be turned down. Then they
have crept home again to try to get back what
they sold, and to find their friends’ welcome
as cheerful to them as flat beer. This sort
of thing is no good. Either there is no
standard -of medical fitness, or, if there is,
the. pied ical men don’t know how to apply it,
with the result that some splendid, willing
fellows are made fools of and humiliated.
“Dick” Sumner, Queensland’s Board for
the Control of Prices, finds his job no Dorcas
meeting. He lowered the price of milk
lately from 7d. a quart to sid., and was in-
terviewed by a crowd of injured milk-mer-
chants who successfully demonstrated that,
with the present price of fodder, pure juice
at sld. wasn’t a business proposition. Sum-
ner now proposes to tackle the fodder ques-
tion as the basis of the milk problem, and is
likely to do no better with it than Davy Hall
did in N. S. Wales. So far, something has
gone wrong with every Queensland attempt
at official market-rigging.
All experts know how fruitful a cause of
swine disease skim-milk can be; hence, to
please the pigs, the Ryan Government re-
cently issued a regulation which requires
“milk fed to calves or pigs at any dairy ol-
factory to be heated to 180 degrees Fahr. for
10 minutes.” Then, to please the hogs, who
might be a little inconvenienced if they had
to light a fire and keep it going for 10
minutes, it was next announced that “there
is reason to believe the new regulation will
not be enforced.” The liog-pen is mightier
than the sword. In fact, according to this
latest report, Ryan is so ashamed of his
sword that he proceeds to swallow it.
Last year Liberalism in the Queensland
Assembly was “putting on the gag,” and
Labor Avas strenuously objecting to the
“murder of free speech.” To-day Labor is
doing the gagging and Liberalism is raising
the howl about the murder. A beautiful
thing this Party Government!.
t “Viking”: The moss-covered complaint of
N. S. Wales stock owners might disappear
if Ma took lessons from her Northern daugh-
ter. In Queensland, when drought forced
the graziers to seek green fields for their
starving stock, instructions were issued that
the supply of sheep-vans should be aug-
mented by the conversion of cattle trucks.
Within 10 days five-score stock waggons
had been converted into double-deckers. These
were commissioned as they were ready, and
large numbers of sheep were soon being
dragged from the dry west to the green coast.
The work is still going on, for the drought is
not yet “broken,” though some of the smaller
pastoralists are.
“Milo” on a perpetual grievance:—
The Cane Price Boards promised by the Queensland
Government as a protection to growers will be only
another abortive effort to overcome an evil which
curses the sugar industry because it does not touch
the root. All the evils are traceable to the existence
of only one buyer and one seller of Australian sugar.
The sugar content is a controlling - factor in assessing
the value of cane. It ranges from 5 per cent, to 15
per cent, of available sugar, according to the district
and the variety of cane grown. Thousands of tons
running from 5 per cent, to 6 per cent, “density” are
milled at Central (co-operative) Mills. But the
C.S.R. Co. will not mill any of a less density than
8 p.0.c.5., because low-grade cane costs as much to
crush as the better sort. If the Boards fix a price
for 6 per cent, cane, then the C.S.R. Co. can refuse
to crush it. While the bonus was in existence the
co. crushed everything sent along, but when the
bonus was abolished that policy was altered. The
Board may fix the price, but what is the use of that
if the grower cannot get the co. to take the stuff?
The C.S.R. Co. has spared neither trouble nor ex-
pense in improving the most profitable sorts for
farmers to grow, but the cussedness of the sugar
farmer is as persistent as the cussedness of the cow
cocky who knows that he has. a beast testing less than
3 pei - cent, of butter fat, and yet keeps the wastrel
in his herd. Should it be made compulsory for the
co. to crush unprofitable cane, then the grower of
high-density cane can bet his socks that it is he who
in the end will carry the indifferent grower. If the
Boards are given power to fix the price, what
is wrong with giving them power to fix the mini-
mum percentage of sugar which the miller shall be
required to crush?
His Popularity.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Kaiser is as popular as ever with his people.
—Recent cable.
Our Kaiser is der finest Kerl
Undt ruler in der Welt,
He has der Russian Jay der scalp,
He vears der Belgian’s pelt;
He dakes a million soldiers oudt
On Moscow’s veary track,
Unfit no von knows when Vinter blows
If efer von comes back.
Vlien oop der Linden Vilhelm rides,
Der yubilating crowd
How do dey sing “Hurrah!” unfit “Hoch!”
Mit Voices glad undt loudt ?
Der Kronprinz is not in it mit
His brave undt gritty Dad,
Who treads der Frenchmen in der dirt
Undt drives der Briton mad.
Somevon of course muss haf to pay
At no far-distant date,
Undt schon a million Deutschers schieep
In death to make him great;
But eferyvhere you get der news
“Der Feind ist going down ! ”
Undt Berlin flies her Flags so dick
You gannot see der Town.
Yoost vait till dot indemnitee
Is raised in London rich,
Yoost vait oontil dot Petrograd
Is level mit her Ditch;
Yoost vait oontil der Sultan drives
Dose Allies in der Sea,
Undt den you’ll shtart to-realise
How big der Deutschers be.
Vot does our noble Kaiser care
If Yarman millions lie
Unburied far on Poland’s plains—
Der Menscli is born to die;
Der flood of Deutschland over all
Kann not verhindert be;
Undt dis is vhy he shtand so hoch
In popularity.
Mit dot Moustache so proud up-curled
(Dey say ’tis grizzled now)
He gallops drough der frightened Vorld
Mit Sieg upon his brow ;
Und Ve bis Volk to Himmel high
His praises lift undt svell,
Undt care for Nichts if ofer us
He scharge avay as veil!
QUEENSLAND.
P. F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 18. 1915.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
His Ex. is at present sampling the good
salt breeze at Southport, whither he went
yesterday to open a Show. Lady Goold-
Adams put in her Saturday uncorking two
fetes. One happened at Rosalie, ana was in
aid of the wounded soldiers’ fund ; the other
was at Wooloowin State School, and was to
help the seminary cultivate some of its side-
lines.
Dr. Sharpe, Bishop of New Guinea, who
was thrown off his horse last Monday while
riding round One Tree Hill, has put' in the
past week at St. Helen’s Hospital. He is
patching up nicely.
C. W. Costin, Clerk of Parliaments,
crossed over on Monday. After leaving the
Grammar School, where he shone, young
Costin first joined the Courier and after-
wards the Telegraph. Then lie went to
the Toowoomba Chronicle ; from there to
Hansard. Mrs. Costin was a Miss Sutton.
Both the dead man’s parents are still alive.
Costin, sen., was a chemist in the early
days. In the son the Rifle Club movement,
the A.N.A., the Royal Society, and the
Grammar School Old Boys’ Association
have lost a helper, and the City Rifle Club
has lost its captain. Lieutenant “Joe” Cos-
tin, who stuck to his machine-gun on April
25 at the Dardanelles until he was killed,
was the deceased good citizen’s only boy.
The Minister for Public Instruction gave
a river trip on Friday afternoon in honor
of Vic. Director-of-Education Tate. Since
he arrived Mr. Tate has put in a good deal
of his time visiting schools. If he wasn’t
pleased with what he saw he made a good
jolt of hiding his annoyance.
The movement to appoint Jane Hops has
been wrapped in a cold blanket of disap-
proval by the Inspector-General of Police
and others in authority. A compromise is
suggested whereby the appointment of an
additional inspectress for keeping an eye
or two on State children and factories where
women and girls are employed may be made.
Anyhow,-the spectacle of one of the, gentler
sex, Thargomindah, putting the hammer-
lock on to the homing midnight reveller of
Brisbane is unlikely. These are unpic-
turesque times.
Brisbane lias taken a line from Sydney,
and is starting various regimental comforts
funds. Mrs. Spencer Browne has another
scheme in hand for providing Christmas
etceteras for all Queensland troops.
The fourth batch of Queensland wounded
arrived on Wednesday night. His Ex. and
Lady Goold-Adams were present to meet the
men, and the former made an unnecessary
speech, and J. M. Hunter, M.L.A., supplied
another. Aid. Jenkinson, more humane,
contented himself and the crowd by merely
remarking “Good luck and welcome home,”
or words- to that effect.
There is a wounded Queenslander who
badly-wants to know how a youngster with
a N. S. Wales landing party at Gallipoli got
on The Queenslander does not know the
name of his brother Australian. All he
knows is that when he was making his way
back to the beach with a shattered arm, a
warrior with the N.S'.W. badge on his sleeve
and an ugly hole in his leg bandaged the
wounded wing and passed on with a cheer-
ful “So-long!” I wish he would, tell me
who and where he is, for I want to gladden
the heart of a sick Bananalander.
Jack Coakes, ' son of the managing-
director of Finney, Isles, has gone into camp
with the Motor Transport. He is very
young, but his anxiety to get away to the
war and his frieiidly relations with motor
power cleared the way for him.
Corporal Merchant is merely winding
himself up to have another whiz at the
enemy, for already he has had varied and
hard-boiled experiences with the Navy. He
served with the Flying Squadron and the
Channel Fleet, and was instructor on the
Australian Station in gunnery and torpedoes.
He took part in an expedition to Morocco,
and has seen service in the South Seas, and
at Burmah. By way of upholding the
navy’s reputation for usefulness he has now
joined the 6th Field Engineers.
A marriage of interest to Westerners will
happen at the end of this month, when
Peter Ferrier, of Surat, one of a family
noted for its outsize in height and hospi-
tality, will place a wedding-ring on the
finger of Essie Smith, of Portland, Roma.
Marjorie Cuppaidge, youngest chick of
Dr. and Mrs. Cuppaidge, Gympie, has
accepted the half-hoop of imnending matri-
mony from Jack Meehan, eldest son of the
J. K. Meehans, now of Sydney, once of
Townsville.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
When John Moffat and A. T. Linedale,
of the Irvinebank Mining 00., called on
Treasurer Theodore the other day to tell
him what a good time it would be for the
Government to build a sugar-mill at
Bailey’s Creek (a few miles above Cairns)
—they are versatile fellows these Irvinebank
mining-men, and don’t keep all their eggs in
one basket —they were not exactly among
strangers. They were introduced by Harry
Ryan, who previous to last elections was
union secretary at Wolfram, where his
union had a bitter struggle with “old
John,” which ended in some kind' of
a draw after the mines had been hung up
for a year or so. About the same time or a
little earlier Theodore was earning a crust
around the mines at Irvinebank and Stan-
nary Hills, where he and McCormack (now
Speaker) brought forth the fighting A.W.A.
and were promptly n .ked—and not for
the State Trea'surership and Speaker'swdiair
either.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f I HE ordinary crowd which gathers in
front of the Central Court littered the
steps as I mounted them. There were
the excited groups of people who make the
occasion of even a remote connection with “a
case in court” a memorable day’s excite-
ment. There were the seedy men with
anxious, strained faces, half ashamed of
being seen, and fearfully anxious about the
ordeals they had to face—most of them there
about summonses, which, had they known
it, need not have troubled them. There were
people who fidgeted uneasily; others who
stoically waited, used to the surroundings,
for judgment on possibly serious matters; all
sorts and conditions of men. I*olice-court
solicitors and solicitors’ clerks, on the look-
out for old clients for the purpose of intro-
ductions to other clients, dodged in and out.
I was a little ahead of the time when
there would be a sudden shuffling towards
the various courts on the stroke of 10 o’clock
and self-important officers of the law would
call “Silence!” in the court-rooms.
At the top of the steps I met Lane.
“I say, old chap,” he said, hurriedly,
“could you oblige me with a couple of quid
till later? I came down with just a little
silver because of a ’phone message from a
country friend, and I find that what lie
wants is this cash, and I can’t get it unless
I go back to Balmain. A friend and fellow-
townsman of his wants it to help a friend's
son through some trouble ; and he wants it
quick and lively.”
“I’m sorry, 1 haven’t got two quid,” I
said. “I would gladly”
"iou see, the money’s wanted to pay
some solicitor’s costs, and if it’s not forth-
coming he won’t act. It means business to
me if I can fix up my friend : I'll get his
friend’s business, anyway, and some others.
1 don’t know a soul here. You know some
of these lawyer-johnnies, don’t you?”
I admitted I did; and then, seeing Dan
Itiley, the solicitor, told Lane to hang on
for a minute. I wanted to oblige Lane just
as badly as he wanted to oblige liis friends.
“I’d let you have the couple in a second,
if I had it,” said Riley, heartily, when I
asked him. “Wait a bit, tuough ; I’ll have
some money in a minute or two, and then
you can have it. Look out for me.”
“How long?” I asked.
“Oh, only a few minutes —not more than
five, certainly,” said Riley, as he bustled
away.
I went back and told Lane that he could
have the cash within five minutes ; and Lane
took me over and introduced me to two
obviously “financial” countrymen, Green
and Falconer.
“It’s lucky we met you, then,” said Green.
“I didn’t like to mention the particulars on
the ’phone to Lane, making sure that he’d
have a couple of notes about him. I had
no need to bring any coin with me when I
came in this morning; so when I met Fal-
coner here I couldn’t fix him up.”
“They cleared me out at the races yester-
day,” said young Falconer. “I’ve wired
home for some dough, but I can’t expect it
for an hour or two. Ttie chap who wants
the money is a fellow I’d like to oblige, if I
can—it’s a son of a great triend of his
who’s in the mess. If the lawyer doesn’t
get his four guineas he won’t appear.”
“Oh, it’s jour guineas!” I cried in dis-
may.
"Oh, two quid has been fixed up already,”
he answered. He looked, at Green. “We’d
better tell them it’s arranged,” he added.
Lane and I remained talking together for
a while, until Green and Falconer returned
with another man. They signalled Lane.
Presently he came back to me, perturbed.
“See if you can get the cash straight
away,” he said. “The sharks are waiting
for it.”
- lii turn I pursued the benevolent Itiley,
catching him just as he was entering the
door of No. 1 Court.
“Can’t do it just now,” he said. “But I will
in a jiffy. Wait somewhere about here for me.”
I hurried away with my news to Lane,
and then returned. The jiffy was a fairly
long one; but finally Itiley appeared, quite
suddenly, in a desperate hurry.
“I’ve got a cheque here for a couple of
quid you can have. It’s not my own, but
it’s good enough,” lie explained swiftly.
I duly handed the slip of paper to Lane,
and then drifted away to attend to my own
affairs, which kept 'me till lunch-time. I
met Itiley as I passed out.
“Come and ■Jiave a taste,” he said; and
while we took our ease in the lounge-bar he
passed a scrap of paper over to me. “Seen
that before?” he asked.
“Why. it’s the cheque you handed me this
morning,” I exclaimed. “How the”
“Exactly,” returned Itiley, laughing. “That
little piece of paper for a couple of quid has
paid me four quids’ worth of costs this
morning.”
“Then you were the skinflint solicitor who
wouldn’t appertftill he had received his four
guineas costs'?” ~
same who accepted a
cheque for t \\V quid -to go on with, then
passed on the cheque to a friend, and duly
received the same two quid back as payment
foi the balance. The nett result, by the way,
seems to be that you owe me two pounds:”
“And somebody else, who is owed the-
money by somebody else, who also has some-
one else to collect from, owes me two
pounds,” I added.
But the nett result was worse than that.
Riley still waits, good-naturedly, for his
money. Lane hasn’t paid me yet—he says
he hasn’t been reimbursed himself. And the
father of the young man about whom all
the trouble was taken, was mad that anyone
should have taken any trouble at all, and
won’t part a cent.
As for the two-pound cheqlie itself, that-
very interesting document proved to be worth
nothing at all when presented at the bank.
The cheque being worthless, Riley did not
lend me anything, nor did I let Lane have
anything. Riley was innocently guilty of
passing valueless paper, and I repeated his
offence.
Riley admits that the case is a peculiar
one. He suggests “a quid” as a compromise.

IN THE SUPREME COURT OF NEW
SOUTH WALES. PROBATE
JURISDICTION.
In the Estate of JAMES RYAN, formerly
of Canowindra, in the State of New
South Wales Farmer believed to be
deceased.
Notice is hereby given that the Public
Trustee of the said State is administering
the Estate of the above-named JAMES
RYAN on the grounds that he died intestate
the year One thousand nine hundred
and four. NOW THEREFORE the
said James Ryan or ‘(if dead) his
children or legal personal representa-
tives is (or are) requested to come in and
prove his (or their) identity on or before
the FIRST DAY OF JANUARY, 1916,
otherwise an application will be made to the
said Supreme Court for an order declaring
that the said James Ryan shall be presumed
to have died intestate and unmarried since
the year 1904, and that his Estate be dis-
tributed amongst his next of kin. The said
JAMES RYAN was last heard of from
Queensland and is believed to have gone to
New Zealand from that State. Any person
possessed of any information as to the where-
abouts of the said JAMES RYAN is kindly
invited to communicate with the undersigned.
T. W. GARRETT,
Public Trustee,
Culwulla Chambers,
Castlereagh Street, Sydney, N.S.W.
PURSUANT TO A DECRETAL ORDER
of the Supreme Court of New South
Wales in Equity, made in the suit intituled
No. 5960 of 1915, in the matter of the Estate
of MARY THERESA FARRELL, deceased,
THE PUBLIC TRUSTEE v. ELLEN
JOWSEY, the persons claiming to be the
next of kin of the said MARY THERESA
FARRELL, late of Bay View House, Tempe,
in the State of New South Wales, who died
on the 31st day of May, 1914, intestate and
unmarried, living at the time of her death, or
the legal representatives of such of the said
persons as have since died, are hereby re-
quired to forward particulars of their claimt
verified by affidavit to THE PUBLIC TRUS
TEE for the State of New South Wales, al
his office, Culwulla Chambers, Castlereagh
Street, Sydney, New South Wales, or to
his Solicitor, LEOPOLD BENJAMIN
BERTRAM, of Macquarie Chambers, 153
Elizabeth Street, Sydney, on or before the
First day of March, One thousand nine
hundred and sixteen, or in default thereof
they will after the expiration of the time so
limited be bound by the proceedings in the
said suit as if they had been served with the
said Decretal Order.
Friday the Tenth day of September, One
thousand nine hundred and fifteen.
H. P. OWEN, Master in Equity.
The said Mary Theresa Farrell was the
last-surviving child of Patrick Farrell and
Margaret Mary Farrell (formerly McAul-
iffe). Patrick Farrell died in New South
Wales on the 22nd October, 1870. It is
believed that he was born at Portumna,
County Galway, Ireland, in or about 1816,
and that he emigrated to New South Wales
about 1841. Margaret Mary Farrell died
in New South Wales on the 24th August,
1893. The Defendant Ellen Jowsey claim-
ing to be a first cousin on the maternal side
of the said Mary Theresa Farrell claims to
be entitled to the whole estate as the sole
next of kin of the said intestate. The
father of Ellen Jowsey, James Enright
(also called JAMES TROY), is believed to
have gone to the United States about 1857 ;
her mother, Mary Enright (formerly
McAuliffe), is believed to have died in
Limerick in 1857. The said Ellen Jowsey is
believed to have had (among others) three
brothers, of whom the names and material
particulars are believed to be as follows:
JAMES ENRIGHT (also called JAMES
TROY) at one time in the S9th Regiment
Infantry ; subsequently in the 60th Regiment
stationed _in Limerick; after leaving the
army resided at Bouden, near Manchester.
DANIEL ENRIGHT (also called DANIEL
TROY) at one time in the 86th Regiment
Infantry ; subsequently in some other Regi-
ment stationed in Limerick. MICHAEL
ENRIGHT, enlisted in the Royal Navy; on
leaving the Navy resided at Portsmouth.

Co.’s bore, Robe, South Australia, dated 11th:

“Pipe down 1181 feet. Fairly definite
showing of oil in blue shale at 1163 feet.”
This is the first report of the finding of
petroleum in Australia.
This company has 5000 Is. shares to self
at 2s. net. We hold the only four oil leases-
issued in South Australia, and four licenses,,
all covering a total of 20 square miles, next
the Ocean Oil Co.’s (Whaley’s) bore at
Kingston. At Latrobe, Tasmania, the com-
pany holds 2591 acres of shale-bearing land 1 .-
As John D. Rockefeller once- advised a
friend: “Don’t delay; get in while you can.”
J. Pierpont Morgan said: “The founda-
tion of wealth is the first hundred dollars
invested.”
Chauncey M. Depew advised : “Buy when
the oil stock is first offered.”

rile Bunbury Forrest has written to the
lerth papers expressing grief at the dis-
appearance of black swans from the Swan
Kiver. Years ago, when Bigjohn ruled the
roost in the West, he made the river, from
h remantle to Perth and beyond, a sanctuary
tor native game, and stocked it with several
hundred pinioned swans. Provision was
made for feeding them, and in a little while
the number increased to thousands. Then
feeding was discontinued, and the flock has
gradually diminished until now only a few
are left. Those that didn’t die of starvation
were devoured by dingoes which haunt the
upper reaches of the river. If Seaddan
doesn t mind we’ll call it Wild Dog Creek
for the future. _ It seems more appropriate,
from the financial point of view, anyhow.
“Dinkum Oil”: After all, the talk about
the preponderance of Germany’s ammuni-
tion supplies over those of the Allies seems
to have been overdone. The latest cable
news indicates that Hans the gunnel
couldn’t hit a flock of haystacks sitting on a
fence. During liis recent visit to French’s
headquarters, George Beid, Australia’s
Broad Commissioner, was twice under
shrapnel fire, and not a shot struck him.
Ir the Hun cannot scupper a target like
that his marksmanship is hopeless.
The Commonwealth is shortly to have a
visit from Louis de Rougemont, whose tall
Nor’-West Australian yarns mesmerised the
Wide World magazine a few years ago.
With him comes a new Mrs. Louis, who
describes her husband—the third she has
tried —as a “delightful child of nature,” who
“will yet startle the world with the revela-
tion of a great truth.” Whether the great
truth will out during his impending visit
she does not say.
A Bill to strangle the “red plague” is now
before the Westralian Parliament. Any
person, male or female, suffering from
syphilis must within three days consult a
doctor, who must report to the Health Com-
missioner. Penalty: £2O. Chemists are
prohibited from treating cases, but will be
allowed to sell certain proprietary medicines
approved by the authorities. The disease is
to be treated by doctors only, and quack
advts. are to be stopped. Afflicted women
are to be detained till cured, free of charge 7
and, apparently, afflicted men are to be per-
mitted to roam around and maintain the
supply.
“0.K.”: What do thoughtful people think
of Chief Justice John Madden, of Victoria,
and what does that notable citizen think
about himself? On his -etara to Melbourne
from England three or four months ago he dis-
couraged recruiting by giving a lecturette on
the war to the effect that Germany would
be satisfactorily beaten before the end of
August. Obviously there was no need for
preparing further Australian forces for the
Front if the allegations, suggestions and
theories of the professedly well-informed
Madden were correct. Then came the
“naturalised”-German attempt to get the in-
ternment of Wallach, of the Australian (Ger-
man) Metal Co., declared illegal. John
Madden, asf Chief Justice, led' the Court in
pronouncing the internment illegal, and Wal-
lach was released. Now the High Court bas
unanimously squelched the Chief
The two most serious errors, both of them
calculated to have very evil effects, suggest
such a grave lack of judgment in this vital
matter that Mr. Madden might almost be
expected to notice it and silence himself
accordingly.
“Party-Hack” writes :—-
The time is budding for a clear exposition or some-
thing with regard to the position Allen Taylor occu-
pies in the N. S. Wales body politic. The other
evening a proposed railway made a sudden appearance
in the Assembly. It was to run from Regent’s Park to
Cabramatta, in the suburban area. Even the Minister
who introduced the Bill was mystifild. It was always
understood that the Bankstown linfVwas to be con-
tinued to cut the Southern line at Liverpool, and
that has always been referred to as a sensible plan ;
but the proposed line to Cabramatta would render
this other proposal unnecessary. The Railway Com-
missioners reported that the new line would be run
at an annual loss of £7510. Some people would
say that this isn’t the time to want to lose £7510 a
year. But that apparently was neither here nor there.
And, anyhow, it wasn’t the point that had attention:
Opposition-leader Wade openly said: “Here we have
Allen Taylor s brickyard again,” and hinted at worse
things. Another member remarked that it was'funny
that the “same old crowd were always getting in,”
and added that he had been told that the
“Quid pro Quo Syndicate” had fixed up the
line. As far as this writer knows there
is nothing to -connect Allen Taylor with the “Quid
pro Quo Syndicate,” and he is not prepared to en-
dorse Gregory Wade’s reference to the “Allen Taylor
brickyard.” But the fact remains that a section
of N.S.W. politicians is continually making dark
allusions to the fact that certain Ministers are
under Allen Taylor’s thumb, and it is idle to deny
that a certain proportion of citizens give heed.
Therefore the time srems quite ripe for a thunder-
storm to clear the political atmosphere. ‘Has any-
body got a thunderstorm ?

He Keeps His Word
The men who do
big things and set
the pace, also set Big
Ben. He keeps his
word so they can
keep up the pace.
Big Ben is a big watch-
like alarm clock, with an
eye for accuracy and an
ear for duty.
He serves as the morn-
ing round-up in more
than three million fami-
lies, calling always just
as he’s told, and when
he’s told.
He stands seven inches tall,
well-poised, massive, and richly
plated. If you have him oiled
every other year, there is no
telling how long he will last.
Big Ben is made in La Salle,
Illinois, U.S.A., by Westclox. His
price is 12/6, postage 1/- extra. If
you cannot find him in your town,
send a money order to the makers’
Australian office, Westclox, cr. King
and Kent Sts., Sydney. They will
have one mailed to you by the
nearest jeweller.

The Inky Way.
“II.S.”: The dailies reap profit from
advts. on all sorts of personal topics—births,
deaths and marriages; silver and golden
weddings; rolls of honor and in-memoriam
notices; intimations that Mr. Smith will no
longer he responsible for the debts con-
tracted by his wife, and solemn warnings
that if Mrs. Blank doesn’t cease slandering
Mrs. Dash’s name she will be required to
prove her mendacious words without respect
of persons. All these and many other side-
lights are cast on the social life of the com-
munity ; but the following in a Cairns (Q.)
paper is a novelty: —
ENGAGEMENT.
To Mr. J. GEORGE—
I hasten to offer you my most sincere congratula-
tions and hearty good wishes, on your Engagement
to Miss Alice Williams. May Heaven bless both of
you.—Your Sincere Friend,
J. MALOVICH.
It opens up so many possibilities. For
example:—•
DEBT PAID.
To Mr. Oofless, —I hear you have paid Henry
Mutton, the butcher, the bill which you have owed
him for so many months. Splendid. God bless you.
-—Your faithful pal, H. STONE-BROKE.
Or again:—
SACKED.
And so, my dear Arthur Alcohol, you have got the
bullet from your job in the Manchester Department,
and are now despairingly considering the question of
enlisting for King and oountry. Accept my sym-
pathy. Gott strafe the employing classes. —Yours
ever, JAMES BEER.
Now what enterprising newspaper pro-
spector will develop this new field?
* * * *
“8.X.” : When I first saw G. B. Simpson
as a Supreme Court Judge I had just joined
the law-reporting staff of a Sydney morning
daily; and as the old man expressed some
interesting views from the Bench that day
I promptly published them in a paragraph.
Next morning I was warned off the course
by one of the veteran hands, who told me
I’d be very lucky if Simpson didn’t contra-
dict the paragraph from the Bench when
the Court met, and invite me to see him in
his room.- He didn’t, as a matter of fact—
perhaps he had been really misreported this
time! —but, anyway, the warning almost
cured me of any desire to turn pleasant copy
out of this particular Judge. I was cured
definitely a few weeks later, when he
screamed out that he had been misrepre-
sented by one of the most accurate men on
the Sydney press over some tiff with Judge
Gibson, and sent orderlies and various other
persons scurrying all over the city to find
the writer and drag him into his presence.
Next to his persistence in turning down
something he didn’t like the look of in cold
print, the most potent qualities possessed
by Simpson were an excellent knowledge of
law and an astonishing capacity for worry-
ing himself and everybody else in Court
because an affidavit hadn’t been correctly
filed, or wasn’t properly gummed, or some
other non-essential.
* * * *
On no authority whatever The Bulletin
learns that the promotion of the Grand
Duke Nicholas to be Viceroy of the Caucasus
was due to the Czar’s desire that his dis-
tinguished relative should have time to com-
plete his forthcoming work on “How I Lured
Them On.” the main features of which have
been so often appreciatively alluded to by
Australian daily-paper writers on Russian
strategy.
MY DOG PROCLAMATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Why not increase the dog tax? Make it £1 per
head, with the present 2s. 6d. per head rate on
sheep-dogs and those used in gaining a living in
the country. Thus we could raise revenue and
get rid of useless mongrels at the same time.
-—Cowardly suggestion in Sydney paper.
Let us all stand at a high window and contemplate
the universe of dogs!
Old dogs, young dogs, gay dogs and sad dogs —
Well-bred dogs and dogs that were more or les3
accidental;
Dogs that were not required, but which, having
occurred,
Have to continue somehow to be dogs;
Dogs without homes or owners or religion or poli-
tics ;
Dogs with problematical futures, intense presents,
And pasts which they would rather not talk about;
All trotting and sniffing and hurrying hither and
thither,
Without any prearranged plan or plot whatsoever,
Getting out of the way of trams —getting into the
way of people;
Drifting affably sideways with tongue lolling
thirstily ;
Proceeding economically with a minimum of wear
and tear on three legs;
Or bolting expensively on all four with tail tucked
well into safety,
Hatches battened down, all fleas stowed and ears
laid flat back.
Consider, I say, this shifting haze or phantasmagoria
of dogs !
What lazy department of Providence orders their
lives
And leaves them to root for their bones amid hatred
and greed ?
I dislike every dog that has fleas —and no dog is
without.
Yet I find all these dogs of great use in my trade
or profession.
1 write of the inward-bound dogs and the outward-
bound dogs ;
And the crosswise dogs are also of enormous im-
portance.
I stand at this high window where I cannot possibly
smell them
And write of their comings and goings, their loves
and intrigues,
Of their manners and customs, their politics, wars
and expressions,
And the profit is simply immense while the fun is
worth having.
So I say let the dog 3 still remain, for my floor is
the seventh ;
I can’t smell or fall over the brutes, and my dustbin
eludes them.
Let us play no mean dog’s-tricks on dog 3! Seek the
revenue elsewhere!
That’s enough for the present!
Let me hear no more of it!
N.S.W.
M. KODAK
(of Paris).

The Black Camel.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WOKE as I watched by him; I had
fallen into sleep—-
Between the midnight and the morn
how slow the hours creep !
And where the slats were open wide above
his tossing head
1 saw the street as bright as day across his
tumbled bed ;
lie slept and struggled in his sleep beneath
the drowning drug,
It caught him by the pulsing throat and
gripped him like a thug
dust for a little. Ere the dawn the evil
drowse would pass
And Bain wou.d come and twist his face
like Deatli seen through a glass.
The moon was on the hills to-night, and in
the pinewo.id's gloom
Between each bough her lingers shone like
candles in a room ;
The street was white as seashore sand, and
down (lie bridle track
I saw a ghostly presence come; a humped
and jetty back,
A strangely swaying lizard head and muffled
feet it had.
It turned the little mountain trail into a
camel pad,
And I was glad he should be free the dreary
day before.
I prayed the strange black camel come and
kneel outside our door.
The houses were all darkened now along
the blinded street;
No one but I to see it kneel and hear its
stealthy feet.
Bod knows it was because I loved that I
would have it free
The frenzied brain, the fell disease stealing
my man from me.
He would be mine again when dead, for
Death can ne’er claim all,
And so I watched the moonlit street, the
shadow on the wall.
The houses were all silent. Stay—that win-
dow yonder there
Sends forth an orange challenge from a
single night-light’s flare!
A figure leans across the sill, across the
wide, white bed ;
’Twas but a month ago these twain came
hither, newly wed.
I almost hated her, so close he held her in
his arm,
Where my man can no longer hold my body
tight and warm.
Then the pain clutched, and I forgot. I
looked not there again ;
Their joy seemed such a little thing beside
my mighty pain.
I grudge them neither life nor love. Both
freely I resign
Only to ease my man from pain, and find
him cold—and mine !
Once I had thought Death was the worst
life held for him, for me;
But there are other things which make
Death’s threat but mockery !
It seemed as though my weak hands held at
tension back the pain.
O God, send the black camel here before he
wake again !
The pale sea-poppies were in bloom along
the water-way;
The cormorants were gathering for the dawn-
ing on the bay ;
The silver whiting shoaled and swam, un-
witting. for their feast,
And still the moonlight blanched the world
and checked the rosy east.
He. stirred upon his torture bed. a furrow
marked his brow.
I looked between the open slats—the street
was emnty now. . . .
Far up the little mountain trail I saw a dark
shape pass.
And here a fallen slipper lay like rose-leaf
on the grass!
They draw the blinds across the way. an
empty bed to hide;
A bridegroom sobbing to the morn heart-
hunger for his bride.
And I. crouched low beside his bed. hear my
man moan again
A broken curse on God who gives another
day of pain !
Queensland.
M. FORREST.
Corsets and Sausages.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“TV X him!” sighed the advertising
I w artist, blowing a cloud of blue
tobacco smoke.
“Who?” queried the verse-writer.
“Mugson, the sausage man! And while
Providence is at it I would also like Her
d—d too !”
“Which Her?”
“The corset countess up at Chiffons, Ltd.
I would like them both d—d very care-
fully in the special sausage and corset hells.
That is, if there are such places.”
“Why?”
The advertising artist drew his pipe-stem
from between liis lips and looked keenly at.
the writer. “Have you ever done any adver-
tising work?”
The verse-writer grew red in the face,
dashed his clenched fist on the arm of his
chair and started to shout. “Advertising
work! My God ! Haven’t I just! I remember
a filthy little bounder once got me to write
a four-line verse about disinfectant. Look
here ! If you”
“Exactly! That’s why I want the sausage
king and the corset countess condemned—
because they’re just like the disinfectant
bounder and the rest of the loathly tribe.”
The verse-writer subsided and lit his pipe.
“Tell us about it.”
The advertising artist laid his pipe aside
and leant forward in his chair. “Last Satur-
day I got a ’phone message from Mugson,
the pork butcher, who is so dull that he is
practically half-witted, to go down and see
him about a new sausage he was putting on
the market. He had a sample of the thing
there, and lie gave it into my hand as though
it were a diamond necklace and asked me
to have a look at it. To me it looked just
like any other sausage, and after a liasty
look I thrust it back into his podgy paw.
I hate the cold, clammy touch of the
things.
“‘What d’ye want me to do?’ I said.
“Mugson looked pleadingly into my face.
‘I want yer ter dror it,’ he gasped, dangling
the filthy thing in front of me. T want yer
ter dror it on a willer-pattren plate with two
bits er parsley—one on each side. D’ye
catch me meaning?’
“ ‘All right,’ I said.
“But he wasn’t done with me yet. ‘Don’t
’ave it sideways like that,’ said lie, swing-
ing his bag of meat before my nose. ‘Make
it a sorter three-quarter portrait, like that,
if yer know what 1 mean.’
“ ‘AH right,’ I said.
“ ‘An’ put th’ sliadders in delekit like,’
moaned the dreary old wretch. ‘I don’t
want it mistook fer a black-puddin’. Don't
make it look too solid —if yer know what I
mean.’
“ Wes,’ I said.
“ ‘An’ don’t fergit about th’ parsley—two
bits, mind, not three!’
“ ‘No,’ I said.
“ ‘An’ be careful ter show it’s a wilier
pattren plate, not a common kitchen one.
A good thing re-quires a good settin’.’
“ ‘Bight you are,’ I said.
“ ‘Slioor you’ll re-member all that?’
“I said I thought I would, and asked him
to wrap up the dashed sausage and let me get
away.
“‘Wrap up th’ sossige!’ he screamed.
‘Wrap—up—th’—sossige !’
“ ‘Why not?’ I asked.
“He held the disgusting thing before my
eyes, and I noticed that his hand was trem-
bling with rage. ‘D’ye think I’m goin’ ter
wrap up that sossige?’
“ ‘Well,’ I said, ‘I can’t take it away
naked!’
*‘ ‘I ain’t goin’ ter ’ave it flattened!’ lie
yelled. ‘I ain’t goin' ter ’ave that sossige
ruined !’
“‘What about a jewel-case?’ I said.
“The old brute didn’t notice the sarcasm.
‘l’ll git yer a cake box from th’ confecshlin-
er’s,’ he roared. ‘Though w’y a dam’ artist
is too proud ter carry a sossige like that
beats me!’ Then he went off mumbling, and
came back presently with the thing packed
away in a cardboard-box and the plate in a
separate parcel. As I hurried away I heard
him howling something about ‘two bits o’
parsley, mind yer—not three!’
“When I got back to the studio, Hollars,
my partner, told me that the manager of
Chiffons, Ltd., had been ringing up, and
wanted me to go along and see him at once.
I took out and arranged the sausage on the
plate, according to old Mugson’s directions,
so that I would not forget. Then I left it
handy, and hurried up to Chiffons, where the
manager took me straight lip to the queen of
the corsets —confound her! She was a tall,
proud woman in a trailing black dress that
swished like a fire-hose, her hair was butter-
yellow, and her job seemed to be to look the
whole world in the face and give it the
glassy eye. She gazed at me as if I were
dirt, which of course I am (we are all dirt),
and I asked her humbly enough what was
wrong; and she snorted and said everything
was. Then in her queenly way she showed
me a dozen of my own corset drawings, and
pointed out where the lee-braces were all
wrong and the other rigging a perfect dis-
grace to womanhood. I knew they
were all right, and that she was
merely working off a fit of temper;
but I had to pretend to listen to her
while she displayed her ignorance of art and
showed a section of a slim, black leg through
the slit in her trading clothes. When she
ran-down I parcelled up the sketches, said
I’d fix ’em up, and got out while a loafing
shopwalker with a frock-coat down to his
patent-leather heels tore at his lip-fluff and
looked like a fierce, relentless poodle-dog.
“When I got back to the studio with the
corsets I found Ruby, the little model, just
finishing her lunch. ‘I cooked a sausage I
found here,’ she gurgled over the rim of her
teacup. ‘Looked everywhere for the rest
but couldn’t find them.’
“‘But I wanted to draw that sausage!’ I
yelled. ‘I brought it back especially to draw
it!’
.“‘Oh, I’m so sorry,’ she said. ‘Wait a
second and I’ll get you another!’ She started
for the door.
“‘You can’t!’ I groaned.
“You bet that astonished her. ‘Oh, what
rot.!’ she snapped. ‘One sausage is just like
another. You wait a second !’ She slammed
the door and ran downstairs.
“In a little while she came back with
another sausage, and, so far as I could see, it
was just like the sample one Mugson had
given me. I smoothed out a wrinkle or
two, posed it on the plate with two sprigs
of parsley Ruby got from the caretaker’s
wife, and got straight to work on it. It
looked fine when I had finished —in fact, I
was so proud that I sent the boy straight
round to Mugson’s. If you will believe me,
that old cow fairly flew into the studio ten
minutes after the youth’s setting-out. He
had my drawing unwrapped in his greasy
paws, and his little eyes were starting from
his head with fury. Jabbing the meat por-
trait with one stubby finger, he roared:
‘That ain’t my sossige ! That's a dirty for-
gery, that is! Where’s th’ curves of my
sossige?’
“‘What are you talking about?’ I said.
‘That’s your sausage. It’s a faithful draw-
ing.’
“ ‘lt ain’t!’ he bellowed. ‘Heitlier you har
no hartist or else you’ve gone an’ got
snother sossige ter sit fer yer!’
“‘Oh, don’t be" insane!’ I roared. ‘Don’t
be a d—d fool ! How can a sausage sit—or
be different from another sausage?’
“‘ Diff’rent!’ he shrieked. ‘Diff’rent!
IJain’t you aware lievery sossige ’as a—’as
a liindervidjoolity—don’t you know that?’
“The old brute was getting on my nerves
by this time. ‘You mean individuality!’ I
sneered.
“ ‘O’ corse, I do ! Hindervidjoolity !’
“ ‘The drawing’s right, anyhow,’ I said,
trying to bluff him off.
“He threw the thing on the table and
jabbed another pound of grease on it with his

KODAK.
All that Were Left of Them.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was over small coffee and cigars that
my hotel acquaintance grew irritable
with me.
“I don’t know where you get your quix-
otic notions of the honor of women,” he said.
“But your story is too strong,” I re-
marked.
“Well,” he said, “if you don’t believe me,
come round to the manager of the Trans-
continental Bioscope, right here in Sydney,
where it happened, and he’ll tell you it is
true.”
Now, it was a bit too far to the Transcon-
tinental, and I’d much rather believe him
than verify it, and I’m passing the story on
to you, and if you don't believe it you can
go toAhe Transcontinental for yourself.
The doorkeeper of the Transcontinental,
whose name was Me Johnson, was standing
in front of the picture hall in the glory
of his gilt-edged, chocolate uniform. It was
towards the end of the matine.e, and he
gazed on the passers-by with that fat air
which assured one there was a full house
inside.
A little man in a great state of agitation
came along the pavement and paused in
front of the doorkeeper as if he wished to
ask a question, but the words were sticking
in his throat. Me Johnson did not lend him
any assistance, it being beneath the dignity
of his brave uniform, and experience had
taught him that it did not do to help a man
to words, as it might mean a money request.
But the little man was not after a loan ;
he only paused because his mouth was dry,
and the words stuck in his throat.
“I wish to ascertain,” he began at length,
“if a lady dressed in a tight-fitting dress, and
wearing a black hat with a white plume,
accompanied by a clean-shaven gentleman in
a dark suit, passed in to your picture show.”
Now, though Mc.Tohnson looked a man of
importance in his gay clothes, he was only a
stupid fellow, and he said “Yes” instantly.
As soon as he saw the electrical change that
came over the little man, however, he saw
his mistake. But it was too late, at least
for Me Johnson, who was, as I said, a stupid
fellow.
The little man slapped himself on the face.
“Then it is true,” he said, trembling with
excitement. Then he slapped himself on the
face again. “This comes of being a shop-
assistant,” he went on. “If I had been a
taxi-driver this might never have occurred.”
“Why, what is the matter?” asked Mc-
Johnson.
“The woman in the tight-fitting skirt and
the black hat is my wife,” said the little
man. “The man in the dark suit is her
lover.”
Ilis features began to work convulsively
for a time, and he could not proceed. McJohn-
son just stood and looked at him with his
mouth opened.
“I have suspected for a long time that she
has been false to me, but I could never prove
it. That is the worst of working in a shop.
One cannot watch his wife. If I had been
a taxi-driver”
Then he suddenly broke off, as if he
thought “What is the good of ifs?”
“A friend came round and told me he saw
her come to your moving pictures with that
man. I went to the governor, and asked
him to let me off for an hour to see if it was
true; and he gave me permission. But before
I came here I went and- bought a revolver.
I gave thirty bob for it.”
He showed the revolver to Mc.Tohnson.
Me Johnson was horrified. He felt he ought
to say something, but didn’t know what
would be the right thing to say to a man
who was going to kill his wife.
“But you are not going to kill her?” he
said at length.
‘ Yes, ’ said the little man. “I’m going to
lull her first, and then I’m going to kill that
man, and after that I will kill myself.”
Me Johnson’s mouth opened wider. He
looked awfully silly standing like that, but
he could not rise to the occasion. In a
vague sort of way he felt that he ought to
be doing something, but he just stood rooted
to the spot, fascinated, struck dumb. If the
ground had opened beneath him he wouldn’t
have had enough wit to get out of the hole.
The manager came out, and seeing his
lieutenant looking so ridiculous and forget-
ful of his dignity as doorkeeper of the
Iranscontinental, he called out, “MeJohn-
son !”
This brought the doorkeeper to himself.
\es, sir,” he said, and came forward.
“What is that man doing there?”
“Ilis wife’s in the show with another
bloke, said Me Johnson, u and he’s waiting
to blot ’em both out, sir.”
“Nonsense!” said the manager, looking
towmrds the little man.
“Fact, sir,” said McJohnson.
And the manager knew when he looked at
the awful face of the little man that it was
true. He saw that there was going to be a
newspaper scoop that afternoon unless he
could prevent it. He did not think of the
papers. He selfishly thought only of the
disastrous result a tragedy outside the door
of his bioscope would have.
“For God’s sake, McJohnson,” he said
keep that man engaged. Talk to him. Don’t
let him leave the front. I’ll have to do
something.
He rushed up to the operator’s box and
gave some hurrried instructions, and then he
opened the back door on the right-hand side
ot the platform. When the reel had been
shown lie had the lights switched on, and
mounted the platform.
“Ladies and gentlemen.” he said to the
crowded audience as coolly as if no little
man was outside waiting to blot out his wife
and a bloke, I oughtn’t to cause this inter-
ruption, but there is a lady seated in the
hall with a gentleman friend. I wish to
warn that lady that her husband is outside
waiting for her, threatening to kill her when
she comes out. The management does not
wish to have any disturbance. I have opened
the. door on the right-hand side of the stage.
1 ms' leads through a passage to the next
Suieet. As the next picture, which is the
last one, is being shown, I wish that lady
and her friend would kindly go out by the
back way.- I thank you for your kind atten-
tion, he concluded, and bowed himself off the
platform.
The lights went down, and the last moving
story of photographic art flickered its
length on the screen, and then the lights
went up, and the orchestra began to play the
audience out. But where were all the
people ? All that was left of them were a
few old ladies and a number of children.
ROBERT BROTHERS.
The Girl with the Crimson Hair.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
mHE red light stands for danger,
The red flag cries “Beware!”
O love, you dimpled stranger,
Without a rag to wear,
Why did you send ’twixt sun and sea
That vision wonderful to me,
The girl with crimson hair?
At morn like live gold shining
It clusters round her head,
In noon’s white light refining
It turns to copper red ;
But sunset brings the ruby fire
And hushed eve, breathing soft desire,
Makes purple every thread.
When first I saw it flaming
My dull heart missed a beat,
Then wild, beyond all taming,
Its hot pulse, summer sweet,
Went throbbing like a battle drum
That bids the laggard victors come
To aid the foe’s.j’etreat.
The girl was not retreating;
T She bravely stood her ground.
Near by three cows were eating
Grass with a pleasant sound.
The picture ravished all my soul —
Ehe light, the girl, her hair, the whole
►Sun-burnished landscape round.
No jot of time I lingered ;
Straight by the maid I stood.
The sunset, happy fingered,
Fondled and found her good.
And there my breathless love I told,
In phrases that the Age of Gold
Made in some Grecian wood.
She heard with bright lips smiling
And large grey eyes ashine,
Fhe soul of me beguiling
To dream that she was mine;
And so she really seemed to be,
For e’er the evening dusked the sea
I led her forth to dine.
Thereafter summer weather
My rapture made complete.
Love-linked we went together
In tram and train and street,
And neath her bright hair’s carmine spell
1 spent my cash and fed her well
With all that she could eat.
The red light stands for danger.
Oh, girl with crimson hair,
You walk no more a stranger,
But fill an ample chair
In that suburban home whence I
Creep daily forth to earn and buy
The things you love to wear!
Meekly my load I carry
From dreary week to week.
No thrusts of scorn I parry,
But turn my smitten cheek.
A very humble worm I seem,
A ho yielded all for one loud gleam
Of braided hair and sleek.
But oft when sunset flaming
Has rrtocked the western skies
I walk in anger shaming
The color scheme it tries ;
I shake my fist and hiss and spit
And tell it what I think of it
And all its crimson lies!
N.B.W.
PAT O'MAORI.
